THE RED PAGE.
The Ink-Black Art.
I AM the M.D., the Master Mage. lam told
that there are in broad Australia a hundred
thousand tyros in the Ink-Black Art of
writing something which shall not be too
unlike literature. I am asked to shut my eyes
and watch the long procession defiling past me—
eager youths, innocent maidens, men and women
cramped in middle age, veterans .whose paralysed
fingers will scarce hold the pen—denizens of city
and bush caught together in the terrible net of the
Idea that some day, somewhere, somehow, they
will write something worth printing, possibly (O,
my God !) worth paying for. lam instructed to
feel a wave of infinite pity come over me as I gaze.
And I am desired, as M.D. and Master Mage, to
whisper to these neophytes the horrid secrets
which I have learnt in my Black Book of the
Pharmacopoeia, that perchance they may not .soil
their souls to the Scribblers’ Devil in vain.
Thus doth the Master
inuruct the Neophyte
in the drawing of
Aubrey Beardsley.
I hear and obey. My Neophyte, lend me your
ears !
* 9 *
You are, let me suppose, an ingenuous youth
with a bubbling brain-pan, and you wish to be a
writer, perhaps a professional writer ? You de-
sire to shake the world with' the rolling thunder of
reverberating Macaulayese ; to titillate it by the
delicate stimulant of verse with an incisive edge
which shall penetrate the public hide? Perchance
the tragic drama attracts you ? Or comedy, with
the moral of “What cannot learning and a wife now
do?” spread delicately over three well-builded
acts to set the papers talking ? Is your dream a
lyric whose lines ring mellow as the string of
golden bells on a nautcli-girl’s anklet ? or a patri-
otic ode. wherein the voice of a nation roars defi-
ance at the massed millions of a frowning foe ?
Listen, my Neophyte ! Be not discouraged by
small initial difficulties, for all these your desires
are within the reach, of the trained writer.
“Trained” —I use it in the athletic sense, to
imply concentration and development of a power
to the extremest notch of original ability, and the
art of wielding power at that acme in the perform-
ance. of a task. You write, my Neophyte, with your
brain; and your training must be directed toward
the' development of a highly-specialised power of
discharge in certain collections of neurons, which
we call cerebral centres. You may aid this dis-
charge by the paralysis of the inhibiting or con-
trolling centres, but in addition need to
“ riffle the sjiuice.” (as miners say), so that the
rushing stream of superfluous energy will not
carry off the lighter gold. The life you shall lead
is not a “healthy” life; that hackneyed mens
sana in corpore sano is out of date in our Art. The
healthy body overloads the mind (a mind which
needs to be at its highest" only in one parti-
cular spot, or group of spots, at a time),
weighs it down under a peine forte et
dure of grilled steak, 16-mile walks, and
domestic bliss. Not from the mens sana come the
r savage flame-pictures sketched against the hell-
smoke of Krakatoa, or the operative glimpses
where the very mystery of life itself lies quivering
for an instant before the scarlet jet of an artery
floods its image off the retinal plate.
“ Health -pah ! I tell you the greatest work
nas always a pathological foundation ; it is born
in agony and bloody sweat. The healthy normal
brain, with a thousand channels of outflow, can-
not specialise or cull till a group of centres jets
force like a hydraulic nozzle eating its way into a
hill-side. You need to stop those waste channels,
to concentrate your “ head ” on a single flume, and
then—to have your nozzle clean. . Know that
your normal man is a creature of physiological
control in his most ordinary desires and needs.
But to make a bull (and the Irish faculty for bulls
is but an expression of the thousand channels of
outflow in the Celtic brain which forbid concen-
tration on a single idea), brain stimulation is for
the greater part a question of inhibition. My
Neophyte, learn to inhibit! So. may you employ
your producing neurons and simultaneously re-
press your controlling centres, so that even when
satiated the poetic neurons or what not will dis-
charge unchecked. Is this obscure ? I speak of
the Ink-Black Art. Shall Ibe M.D. and Master
Mage to no purpose ?
To inhibit—that is the problem. Theoretically,
it is simple; but m these elementary days ot
brain knowledge even I must be content to show
you a roundabout solution, an empirical solution,
with the very variable factor of individual per-
sonality always ready to rum the equation,
Nevertheless, my Neophyte, listen !
Most great writers—most writers in fact, so they
be professional—have a system, though it may be
practised unconsciously. The Gargantuan feast-
mgs, the torpor, the feverish sunrise work of
Alexandre Dumas in his literary-supply days ;
the fasting and exercise of Bishop Butler; the
midnight absinthe and grotesque sensuality of
Paul Verlaine;—these were all based on (probably
unconscious) principle! To eliminate the gross
body altogether is impossible, but we may per-
chance attack it with our inhibiting neurons—-we
may take captive that body and set our inhibiting
centres to guard lest it revolt. The captivity, my
Neophyte ! is always to be in proportion to the
effect desired—a column on the prospects of the
wheat-season does not require absinthe and a
French dinner with Suez cigarettes,,. That is a
treatment which would better suit the psycho-
physiological school of Hubert Crachanthor.pe.
It would make a mess of the wheat-article. So-
to the Art ! !
* ■* *» *
Firstly, of Tobacco. This is the prime factor of
the literary life. You smoke, my Neophyte—you
smoke cut tobacco. Be bold, be bold, but not too
bold ! Not all tobacco is your inhibito-stiiuulant.
You must have your special brands, and you must
discover them for yourself. Do you inhale a pipe ?
When writing of, for example, the tragic side of
city life it is well to preface by inhaling one pipe
of heavy plug —it gives contour and perspective to
the misery. Cigars are of a lyric or essay type if
of moderate strength. (I speak only of good
cigars.) The “long breakfast” Manila (lasting
from after breakfast till nearly lunch-time) makes
an excellent medium for harmonious descriptive
work where no special colour-touches are re-
quired. Curiously enough, a black Trichinopoly
to follow (in place of lunch) will often give
mellow pathos. For a light novel a sufficient sup-
ply of medium Batavians or Manilas should always
be laid in, Havanas being more suited for respect-
able business articles. Cigarettes ? They occasion-
ally produce light, airy verse with a pretty rhyme
in it, when inhaled in the company of a flippant,
smart-tongued woman who smokes but does not
inhale. Under such circumstances they yield a
certain femininity of touch—a delicate insight to
those middle depths where the pseudo-motives
lie. I refer, of course, to the cigarette pur et
"simple; such modifications as the 1 Havana
cigarette, the mingling of opium or musk, are dis-
tinct in themselves.
Secondly, of Alcohol. This is highly import-
ant, and the Americans understand it better than
you, my Australian Neophyte. Some day, per-
chance, a literary barman, of the type of the
urbane, clean-shaven jugglers who dispense busi-
ness acumen and hilarity at the Hoffman House,
N.Y., will stand behind a well-fitted Australian
bar and respond to your orders. “ Popular scien-
tific article for middle-class magazine ! Yes, sir.
One moment—thank you, sir!” “ Two assays
and a slashing review, gentlemen ? Here
you are ! ” See the potentialities of liter-
ary fame swirl in an opaline flood, a
block of ice swaying on its bosom, cold as a
prejudice in a good woman’s soul ! But the brutal
Australian habit of using fiery whiskey for light
, sentiment, and limb-tangling colonial beer where
clear reasoning is required, cannot be sufficiently
condemned. Generally speaking, a good old
brandy in slight excess produces heavy, strong
pictures; whisky has a similar effect in a lesser
degree, but lacks the savage directness of brandy.
The latter (in conjunction with other media) may
be used freely for certain political articles. Note,
my Neophyte ! how professional democrats com-
bine whiskey and strong cigars. Absinthe symp-
tom&tises in diluted cynicism, cheap at first but
tending to accumulate. Vermouth is concen-
trated devilry if- taken after two petite verves of
brandy. Chartreuse, yellow or green, is decidedly
sensual; Cura§oa and Benedictine are bland and
broad-minded, despite the former’s brandy-mix-
ture. Of wines, the Burgundies and Clarets
proper are marked by breadth of grasp without
much care for detail. The Hock and
Chablis class may be used in two
ways:—(a) Alone for light, airy society-pleasing
stuff; (b) following heavy indulgence m brandy
overnight. In the latter case the physical exhaus-
tion is removed, and the result is frequently
trenchant. Beer is useful for readable newspaper
padding of a respectable nature, albeit
when taken in excess it may produce in heavy
temperaments of strong physique effects some-
what resembling those of brandy. For daily-'
paper leaders drink beer moderately in the early
morning, when you get the required lead in the
pipes. Gin is maudlin sentiment diluted with
hypochondriasis. Most excellent ballads of the
G. R. Sims type may be written on gin. Rum
had better be left alone, although I once knew a
man who wrote gallant pirate-verses on rum.
Thirdly, of Woman. She varies exceedingly. It is
always ivell to have a passion at call—for preference,
some stately dame of noble soul whom you are not
acquainted with. (Beware of coming too close !)
Well-read Women of cold temperament often
supply excellent ideas, and even quotations : from
these, bright, airy pseudo-literary matter may
be obtained at times. A small, pure - faced
girl of the old-fashioned type, if she can be
brought to admire, you (but no more), makes a
gopi 1 mental background for sonnets. Yet
Woman is better out of the scheme altogether,
except under the condition given. She stimulates
in the wrong centres and spoils concentration.
Fourthly, of Food. It is a large subject, and in
this, our preliminary lesson, its value can be sug-
gested merely. Some day I, will write a bile-
stirring treatise on the' literary aspects of dys-
pepsia, The humorist who remarked that he
always took a light breakfast of hot buns and gin
when he wanted to be serious touched on a wider
subject than he knew of, Indian curry (real
curry, not the cliee-chee abomination trading
under its name in Australian restaurants) is excel-
lent for Eastern stories of imperialistic tendency ;
while cold turkey and lobster-salad at lunch pro-
duce a fine melancholy suitable for pessimistic
essays dla Schopenhauer. Food needs many
adjuncts, and the very presence of such adjuncts
vitiates observation. French cookery, in the
opinion of many good authorities, is absolutely
necessary for the production of literary work pro-
perly so-called ; but from well-timed starvation is
born a fierceness of detail unattainable by other
means.
Fifthly, of “King’s Peg.” I suspect Kipling
of “ King’s Peg,” as he himself names it, in many
of his toups de force— li The Mark of the Beast,”
for instance. It is a subject needing separate
consideration, and I purposely place it apart
from tbeUommon Drink .class, for it is to be used
heedfully. In fact, King’s Peg is not a drink :it
is the essence of a brand-new soul fresh forged in
the cold starlit ether where Force juggles with the
constellations. Do not use it without cause, for
its-,magic departs with repetition. When the time
comes that the elusive hussy Fame has goaded
you into madness and you hold the dice-box for
one last throw, then turn to King’s Peg. No
lunch ; but much tobacco of the black Trichino-
poly type" during the afternoon—at least enough
to spoil your dinner. Be discontented with
a well-sbiected meal of very light character
(oysters, clear soup and, say, a breast-slice of
chicken with a delicate sauce); then take caff
royal, and more cigars. Then to the street or the
bush for an hour alone. Then if it be vour last
hope of-credit, do nqt Omit this—take a moder-
ately cold small bottle of the best dry champagne
(NOT brut), and pour into a long glass about two
inches of the best liqueur brandy. Fill up with
the champagne, and, after a moderate draught,
s ip. Half-an-hour afterwards the jade Fortune
lies quivering in your arms. Hold her, my
Neophyte, hold her ! .... Ah . !
Sixthly, of Drugs. There are certain deserve
notice.. Caffeine, for instance, has a reputation'
but it is purely stimulating and requires inhibit-
ing adjuvants. It is best used as coffee. A cup of
strong black Mocha at 6.30'a.m., preceded and
followed by a glass of hot water, sets the average
brain going like a windmill for six or eight hours ;
and similar doses at 2.30 and 9 p.m. will keep the
effect up into the next day, especially when aided
by judicious starvation. Tea also contains caffeine,
but to a less active extent than coffee. The most
marked period of "reaction to both tea and coffee is
present up to two or three hours after taking.
Morphia and cocaine are powerful agents for one
accustomed to them, having both stimulatory and
inhibitory effects; intensely concentrated work
may be done by their aid—of the omniscience-
strained through-omnipotence class beloved of
some later social essayists. Marcus Clarke
wrote a strange tribute to the powers of
haschisch as a mental stimulant, but (alas
for those who quote the Student Martialis
as a haschisch-born vision!) the Melbourne sur-
geon who .had proposed the idea to Clarke trem-
bled at the last moment, and gave Clarke two of
Plummer’s dinner-pills instead. Hysteria and
brandy were the parents of Martialis. There is a
drug, however, which contains most of the elements
requisite for a true brain-regulator—the yerba de
nuaca of South America, tt- is at once inhibitory
and stimulant, the very essence of all the fays that
ever danced on the moonbeams of a madman’s
cell.
• * * *
I count it enough, my Neophyte, to have in-
dicated roughly a few of the main points to be
considered. The ever-variable • personal factor
must never be forgotten, and self-experiment is
necessary to success. Until physiological demon-
stration can actually show the dendritic filaments
advancing and retreating under the command of
that probably-electrical force which i.s the agent
of the process we call thought—until the effect «f
therapeutic factors on the circulation which cheeky
or renders active each collection of neurons i»
thoroughly explored—we must proceed more or
less empirically.
I end my first lesson with a few prescriptions
illustrating possible combinations towards a given
effect. The subject is a man of strong physique,
agile and cultivated brain, and nervous tempera-
ment —you, O my Neophyte !
For Essay, Pseudo-Scholarly, Polished.

Woman, well-read, platonic ... 1

I

Mage,
PUTYAKA.
A question in N.S.W. Parliament brought the
answer that James Bon wick is paid at the rate of
£636 per annum to transcribe documents in Eng-
land for N.S.W. Historical Records. Mr. Bon-
wick’s knowledge of early Australian history is
valuable; but at 83 years of age his services for
six hours daily are not quite worth that.
Ambrose Pratt’s (the Sydney writer) serial
story, just ending in local Telegraph, shows an
uncommon power of invention, and the interest is
very fairly maintained. But the young writer’s
fault is still prominent: the characters have no
individuality—-they are all Pratt in various cos-
tumes. Still, there is considerable advance on
“ The Emperor and His Double,” of last year.
The strife between Sydney Chess societies has
ended in the affiliation of the independent Sydney
Chess-Club with the Chess Association ; and on©
side sounds muffled bells of triumph, while swift-
fingered Achilles and his comrades sulk in their
tents. The local chess-player is an amusingly
jealous person ; but now that union has come all
sides would do well to bury their squabbles for th*
benefit of the game.

has maintained its supremacy in the face of the whole

[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The Curse of the White Man.
Wild son of fair Australia’s plains,
I sing in no unfeeling strains
Thy blighted hopes, thy bitter pains,
O black, but human, brother!
A “savage” heart beats in thy breast
As white as all the savage rest,
And e’en thine ebon arms have pressed
A human form—thy mother.
In happier days behold him roam,
The boundless bush his haunt and home,
And o’er his head the azure dome,
A king in every feature !
But now, alas ! behold him come
From out his ancient groves of gum,
To cringe or beg for beer or rum—
A cursed and ruined creature !
His placid lakes with sable swan,
Where generations, ages gone,
Found food and sport for sire and son,
Dread white men haunt and plunder;
They fell and spoil his ancient trees
That bore a whole millennium’s breeze ;
They roam and smite where’er they please,
And cleave his woods asunder.
Beneath that tangled roof of hair
There hides a brow unmarked with care,
And fierce his fiery eyeballs glare ;
He heeds no forest barrier.
Behold him hurl his wooden spear !
Within his breast there dwells no fear ;
But free he roves from year to year—
A stout and wily warrior.
His words lived only on his tongue,
His songs are lost, his harp unstrung,
His deeds of daring die unsung
These myriad generations.
Perchance he had his Iliad, too,
Now vanished with the morning dew;
His deeds and he pass from our view
Alas for Christian nations !
The curse of Cain is on our head ;
And since his race will soon be dead
We send him clothes, we give him bread,
But first we gave him brandy !
We landed on his native shore
And drove him rudely from our door,
And where he reigned a king of yore
Now struts a British dandy !
We owned no kinship save from lust
That filled e’en “ niggers” with disgust!
We trod his daughter in the dust,
Then asked for love and beauty.
We s' ot him when his manhood rose
(To d( a’ as man must deal with those
Who strike the deadliest blow of blows)
Constrained by sacred duty.
And were not life and honor dear
To him who owned a hemisphere ?
Poor black ! I’ll drop an honest tear —
My heart can do no other.
No more thy wild corroboree !
No more thine ancient dynasty !
And who will write thy history,
Thou hapless human brother?
W.A.
J. BUFTON.
Wattle’s Abloom!
There’s a scent dropping down from the side of
the hill,
There are patches of gold in the gullies so still,
There’s the bluest of blue in the canopy calm,
There’s a breeze that is sweet with an odour <
balm,
There’s a bosom that throbs with an emulous play
To match with the joy of the garrulous Day.;
There’s an eye that’s astir with the gleam of a
star,
For a whisper comes flashing anear and afar—
“ The Wattle’s abloom ! ”
The Wattle’s abloom —for the bountiful land
Hath taken the gold from her breast: in her hand
Hath spun it oat craftily, twined it with care,
And fashioned a crown for the brown of her hair ;
Hath come from the mirk of the Night as a
Queen,
Hath shown us the joy of her coronal sheen,
Hath bidden us bow as she passeth us by,
Hath laughed with the light of the spring in her
eye—
“ The Wattle’s abloom ! ”
A plague on your twaddle of party and town !
Old Care is a dog that we’ll throttle or drown.
We’re fools —if you will—but we certainly know
That there’s life and there’s love in the blossoms
that blow ;
And the land that they tell us is sombre and bare
Is fragrant of breath and surpassingly fair,
Is gay for the nonce as an innocent bride
Who paces so dear in her virginal pride—
“ The Wattle’s abloom ! ”
The Wattle’s a-bloom ! and a wreathing of flower
Hath fashioned above us a fabulous bower,
Hath bordered the banks of the petulant stream '
With clusters that drop like the clouds of a dream ;
And lie who may wander and ponder will feel
His palpitant breast in a jubilant peal,
Will marvel that mortals should sing of the rose
When every sweet chorus might tell at its close—
“ The Wattle’s abloom ! ”
q.
P. LUFTIG.

The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

applying to MS.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 1, 1900.
Federal Railway Construction.
When it was suggested, in the days while
the Australian Constitution was in process
of construction, that the Federal Govern-
ment should be the sole railroad-owner and
railroad-builder of the Commonwealth, the
Parochial multitude, and many of the weaker
Federalists as well, said that the suggestion
was revolutionary, and that it gave them a
severe shock. They asked, in the tone of
one who propounds an absolutely crushing
objection, if it was not a fact that, under the
proposed circumstances, N.S.W. would be
unable to extend its railway into Sydney, or
build a half-mile branch at Parramatta, with-
out the consent of the other provinces.
Being frankly informed that that was the
exact size and nature of the situation, they
said that the impracticability of the scheme
required no further demonstration. They
inquired, also, what guarantee there would
be under these terrible conditions that “our
State ” wouldn’t be grossly wronged and de-
frauded in the matter of new railways, and
being informed that “our State” had only
the same security as all the other States they
replied that, under these circumstances, the
idea was obviously visionary. They further
desired to know, in a horror-stricken voice,
if it was actually proposed that the other States
should dictate to “us” what lines we should
or should not build. And being informed
that the other States would dictate to “ us,”
and that “we” would equally dictate to
them, and that, in point of fact, Australia
would dictate to itself on railway-construction
matters, just as it does now, only that it
would do so as a whole instead of as six sec-
tions, the Parochial party rose to its feet
and denounced the idea as an obvious con-
spiracy on the part of that unknown person
who was always supposed to be laying plans
to steal something from “us.” They also
said something about giving away our Inde-
pendence, and being Crushed beneath the
Iron Heel of Despotism.
The fact that the existing railways must
be federated sooner or later has already
become too evident to be disputed. It
is the only way to secure a uniform gauge,
uniform rates, economical working, to wholly
abolish the border war of tariffs, and
to arrive at many other advantages. And
yet it is a logical certainty that federating
the existing lines must also mean handing
over the work of railway construction to the
Commonwealth. If the Central Govern-
ment, when it has bought up the existing
lines, leaves the work of construction to the
States, and allows them to build what lines
they please and work them under State
control, then there will be no uniform
Federal management after all. It will
become a case of starting the old confusion
over again, and where there are now six
hostile and disjointed State managements
fighting among themselves there would pre-
sently grow up six hostile and disjointed
State managements still fighting among
themselves, and also fighting with a seventh
hostile and disjointed Federal management.
Also by leaving new construction to the
States the greatest benefit which can
accrue from Federation will be lost. Aus-
tralia needs to be opened up by railways,
and to be opened up as widely and cheaply
as possible. Every place in the interior
which can give reasonable cash excuse for
a railroad wants connection with the nearest
seaport that will answer its purpose, so that
its railway will be as cheap as possible, and
will be built as quickly as possible, and
fares and freights will be as low as possible.
And that can't be done by the State Govern-
ments, which have powers of construction
only within certain areas, and which are
paralysed by an ink-streak on a map, or
stopped short by a meridian. Further, the
Commonwealth can’t leave the States to
construct what lines they please and then
take them over, for two reasons : It
can’t make the railways pay if it is liable to
have hopelessly unprofitable new under-
takings dropped upon it at any and all times
by a power which it can’t control, and which
might build unprofitable lines quite reck-
lessly when the after loss would not be
its private funeral. On many lines the after
loss is a far larger consideration than the
first cost. And it can’t build a scientific,
economical railway system—that is, one
which will take the shortest road to the most
profitable destination, irrespective of county
or parish or State boundaries—if it leaves
the work of construction to authorities
which are brought up short by a parallel, or
a meridian, or a peg stuck up in a dry
wilderness to show that one side of the peg
is Us and the other side is You.
* * * *
After all, how much is there in the alleged
horror of being dictated to by outsiders as to
whether or not a new two-mile branch or
loop shall be laid down in Sydney, or Mel-
bourne, or Adelaide, or Perth, or Hobart, or
Brisbane, or any other place ? Under the
existing separate management it is impos-
sible to lay half a mile of new rails at Bris-
bane if Central and North Queensland,
situated from 500 to 1300 miles distant,
object, and a paltry little siding that is much
wanted at Warwick may be refused by one
vote—and that the vote of a cantankerous
Australian from Carpentaria, much more
than 1000 miles distant. That is quite as
bad as if the same proposition was liable to
be lost in the Federal Parliament by the
vote of the much nearer and not more can-
tankerous member from Sydney. Brisbane
can’t get the money to build a new ticket-
office without asking the assent of Cooktown,
and that is fully as humiliating, except to
those unaccountable souls who find balm and
consolation in a streak on a map, as asking
the consent of the member from Melbourne,
who is very much nearer, and who can hardly
be less sympathetic. Albury, in N.S.W.,
can’t get any new railway expenditure with-
out the permission of Bourke and Sydney
and other places 400 or more miles distant,
and the horrors of having, under a Federal
railway construction system, to consult
Wodonga, in Victoria, three miles distant,
are not so grievous as they look. It is quite
as painful an outrage for Sydney to be un-
able to patch its dilapidated railway station
without submitting to the dictation of Broken
Hill and Wentworth and Tibooburra and
other far-off places on this side of an
imaginary streak, as it would be if it had
to consult Mildura, which is about five miles
nearer than Wentworth, only on the other
side of an imaginary streak. Esperance
Bay has to argue about its railway with the
member for East Kimberley, and East Kim-
berley is a shade further off than Melbourne,
and much more remote than Adelaide.
All the alleged dangers of handing over
the work of railway construction to the
only authority which should possess it—the
Commonwealth—is based on a Grovelling
Reverence for Imaginary Streaks. It is held
to be quite fair, just, and obviously reason-
able that Portland’s railway-extension policy
should be dictated from Melbourne, 250 miles
east; but the proposal that it should be con-
trolled in any way from Adelaide, 250 miles
west, is too horrible for words. The fact
that Sydney has waited 30 years for a decent
central railway terminus, and can get no
further forward, through the hostility of the
country districts, including places like Ten-
terfield, and Bombala, and Milparipka, is
considered regrettable, but it isn't looked
upon as an outrage on the liberty of Syd-
ney- But the proposal that Boorgunvah or
Tintaldra should be at liberty to vote for
Sydney having a decent railway station im-
mediately is regarded in many quarters as
good cause for revolution. They are nearer,
certainly, than Tenterfield, but the fetish or
Joss or Mumbo-Jumbo of Imaginary Streaks
on a map makes all the difference.
• 9 * *
And yet the States, under the present
system, are, in one important sense, less in-
dependent in railway construction matters
than they would be under Federal control.
As an instance, N.S.W. has built a miser-
ably-unprofitable line to Cooma, not far
from the Victorian border. It might be
made to pay if it were part of a second
through line to Melbourne, but Victoria
refuses to move an inch at the construction
of its end. of the line. N.S.W., under the
present policy, might vote till the Day of
Judgment for the extension of that line to
Melbourne, and get no further forward. If
railway construction was the work of the
Commonwealth Parliament, where N.S.W.
will have great weight, its vote might decide
the construction of that through line whether
Victoria agreed or not. As it is, under the
alleged policy of State freedom its railway
management in this matter is dictated
by Victoria. When the inevitable trans-
continental railway to connect the east
and the west comes in view, N.S.W. will
meet the same trouble in a more aggravated
form. If the building of the new lines
rested with the Federal Legislature N.S.W.
would have a voice—the loudest voice—in
the laying out of the whole line. Under the
existing policy the question whether N.S.W.
will ever get direct communication with the
west rests wholly with the little intervening
population of S.A., and N.S.W. can only
get that communication on such terms and
by such route as S.A. sees fit. Queensland,
in like manner, is pushing its railways
towards its western border, and when they
get there further progress stops, unless the
western province is appeased and propitiated
with sufficient grovel, and allows the lines to
cross its territory and develop into another
transcontinental system. At present there
are only three places in Australia where
railways cross the Imaginary Streaks that
make all the trouble. When the iron roads
extend to the borders in many directions it
will become apparent that under the old
separate policy no province has real control
of its own railway system, and that each one
is subject to the irritating dictation of all
its neighbours. Moreover, under the separate
system there is no remedy for this dictation.
The smallest State, if it happens to be in the
way, can dictate to any possible combination
of big neighbors with absolute impunity.
• * * *
As for the plea that, when the Federal
Parliament is the sole railway-builder, there
will be no guarantee that each component
part of the Commonwealth will get its fair
share of the new lines, that must be ad-
mitted. It is just the same way now. It
will be just the same way if the States con-
tinue separately in the railway-building
business. It will be the same way if the
Commonwealth takes the business over. As
things are now, South Queensland is liable
to be unjustly treated by North Queensland,
1000 miles away, in the division of the avail-
able construction money, and, as things will
be, it will be liable to be treated just as un-
fairly by N.S.W., 250 miles away. All the
other parts of Australia are in the same situ-
ation. If any attempt had ever been made,
or any means had ever been devised,
for ensuring a fair division of the cash
under State management then that would be
a reason either for demanding that State
management should continue, or that Federal
management should give an equally good
guarantee of fairness. As things are, and
always have been, the worst that can be said
is that, in this one particular, Federal con-
trol is open to the same drawback which has
always attended State control. To put the
situation in a sentence : If the work of rail-
way construction is handed over entirely to
the Commonwealth things are liable to be no
worse in this one particular, and they are
certain to be much better in all others.
These are The Bulletin’s reasons for being
in favour of absolute Federal control of the
present and future railways of the Common-
wealth. And on the other side there is the
fetish of Imaginary Streaks on the map, and,
possibly, the plea that a consolidated rail-
way system will be too vast an undertaking
for one head to manage. But the latter
difficulty can be surmounted by judiciously
dividing the work and the responsibility
among district managers, and district rail-
way committees ; and as for the gospel of
Imaginary Streaks on a map, it is only a
very small superstition, or Joss, or Mumbo-
Jumbo.
The Plague.—A Foreword.
“ Potyaka ” (a medical man) to The Bulletin :
Sydney—apathetic Sydney—is knotting for herself a
scourge that will yet send her howling into the wilderness,
a scorn and a reproach among civilised cities. Sydney
smiles and reclines along her harbor, fondling her pretty
toys of armed men in shops, armed men ashore, horse-
races, picnics, and general gaieties— never heeding the
tread of the executioner, with the brand hissing in his
hand to sear her flesh. The alleged guardians of the pub-
lic health are bound hand-and-foot by three-penn’orth of
newspapers, which order a grovelling' Parliament to put a
stop to the unnecessary and unwarranted expense of pre-
cautions against the re-introduction of the Plague. The
opinion of a fifty-shilling-a-week reporter, loaded to his
unshaven chin with the eleemosynary whisky of interested
capitalists, is sufficient for the opening of the door to
Death, and a muddle-headed capitalistic proprietor stands
ready on the threshold to take the visitor by the hand.
And, in case Death should not hear the summons, behold,
every fool in the land who craves for cheap notoriety
rushes howling at any further-sighted man who dares to
lift his hand in protest.
* * * *
You have forgotten, you people of Sydney ! You say
there were but a hundred or so of deaths about which to
make all this pother ; that the disease has gone, anyhow,
and won’t come back. You blurt out all the noxious
fatuity that is in you, begotten and nourished by the pro-
perty-owner-inspired pennyworth which leads you by the
nose and forms for you what you call your “ opinions.”
You have forgotten the Days of Terror, when you rushed
with jerking, pallid lips and pumping hearts to the very
men whom you now try to belittle, for hope and protec-
tion. You have forgotten how those of you who could
fled the city, leaving wife and children to stand their
chance while you took a trip “on urgent business.” You
have forgotten how, as the cases mounted up day by day,
you talked in your shops, your offices, your hotels, your
trains, of the Plague, till the word burned into your
brains and maddened you, as maniacs are made by the in-
cessant sounding of a single musical note. You have for-
gotten your GoD-smitten city, where you toiled and dug
frantically, tearing out God only knows what of congested
filth and rottenness. The hand of death rested quivering
above you, and was stayed, as is the fashion in this
disease —but it rests above you still, and the stroke will be
to your very heart. You whitewashed your streets, then.
Make more whitewash, and save it for your next year’s
tombstones.
* * * *
It is the whim of the Plague-Demon that he measures
his stroke, as does a swordsman with a touch of the blade,
before he smites. The Great Plague of London did not
spring like some foul beast out of the darkness upon an
unprepared city. There had been five plagues of London
in the preceding sixty years. Take the dry official
records of the Bombay and Hong Kong outbreaks of the
last six years, and their history is as that of Sydney
in its scorn of warning. The plague-centres within easy
oversea reach, the initial doubtful case or two, the
smitten rats crawling and lumbering into the open air to
die, the recognised hurilan case, the gradual climb-
upwards of an accompanying curve of proportionate
death rate, the occasional small rises and small falls as
the poison fermented and spread in the rotten soil of rat-
tunnels filled with putrid carcases, or was affected by
temporary meteorological conditions ; the longer period
of apparent quiescence with the flushing of sewers by
heavy rains. So far you have gone. But in these other
cases—after a period of quiescence while the smiter
measured his distance—he struck and struck again ;
struck till the hospitals overflowed and paved the streets
with swollen corpses for the pariah-dogs to gorge upon
until they too staggered and died.
* * * *
The condition of some portions of Sydney is almost on
equality with that of the native quarters of Hong Kong
or Calcutta. The Asiatic is constitutionally no more
liable to plague than is the low-type European. The “in-
fected area ” is but a portion of Asiatic Sydney, and
despite the prate of “ vigorous action ” and the rest of the
fanfare made, you have only scratched the surface. You
talk of the “ clear Australian air.” Plague cares nothing
for air, so long as it has filthy houses or filthy soil in
which to generate. On the southern slopes of the
Himalayas, where the pine-forests sing in the pure dry air
and the roaring snow-torrents speed through the gorges—
there the festering villages perched on cliff and scarp
make of Nature’s sanatorium a home of Plague. Some say
that the summer climate will stamp out any remnants.
How then is it that the putrid huts of Uganda, sweltering
under the Equator, yield a yearly dole to endemic Plague?
It is the filthy house, the garbage-sodden soil, the rotten
refuse of a score of rubbish-tips, that are even now
forming a culture-medium better than the gelatine of a
laboratory. Poona, eighty miles from Bombay, had an ex-
perience such as you may yet have. A small outbreak—a
mere twenty or so of deaths a day—was “ stamped-out’
as the newspapers tell you your own outbreak has been
stamped out. The people forgot—as you have forgotten
—and for months the scourge lay quiet. The rains had
washed the gutters as they have washed yours ; but the
leaven worked. Then, as the poison grew and gathered
strength, there came a case here and there, till suddenly
the pest leapt beyond human interference and laid the
town waste. In your streets and alleys are the same piles
of garbage, the same foul, greasy stench. Ships lie un-
protected along your quays, free to bring infected rats to
you or to distribute them among your coastal ports. In
the hotels where you eat and drink are the same abomina-
tions in the shape of public conveniences that demand
the hanging of those responsible for their condition.
Thousands of working men herd in hovels built over the
rotting filth of the city, their children playing among the
lazar-rags of the tips.
* * * *
By inoculating earth from rat, and rat from earth, in
turn, the Plague organism can be kept alive indefinitely,
increasing to epidemic strength with each successive cul-
tivation. Even now, rats burrow and die under your
feet and in your streets, repeating the experiment of the
laboratory on a gigantic scale. You will argue, with your
cheap cocksureness, that rats in time become immune;
but do you know how many years it takes for this to
come about ? Do you know that, as the immune rat-
population dies out, others with no such immunity swarm
in from ships and from the outlying districts, to take
their places and to die in their turn ? Some say that rats
are necessary scavengers. Is the present typhoid-scourge
a credit to their administration, if that is the case ? You
prate of your slain and paid-for sixty thousand ! Make
the foreshore of your harbour one vast rat-trap, kill six
millions —and perhaps you may rest awhile upon your oars!
Search out the burrows with jets of superheated steam,
and cover the ground with chloride of lime when you
have done it. Protect your shipping and yourselves by
the enforcement of proper health-regulations. Clean-up
your city. You have the chance now of doing it volun-
tarily ; but in a decade you will be cursing yourselves at
having been driven into it in terror-stricken hurry.
* * * *
The July rains saved you, this year; but they will not
have power to intervene again, for your soil will be rotten
through-and-through unless you bestir yourselves. The
bacteria are multiplying in geometrical progression in
the very bowels of your city, while you throw money
with both hands to the beef-fed idols of your blood-lust.
You love to read in your newspapers of death and misery
in foreign countries where your young men go to slaughter
farmers and to die of enteric fever, but shortly—very
shortly—you will have Death himself among you, thank-
ing Parliament for the facilities afforded for his entry.
You have forgotten now, but afterwards you will terribly
remember!
* * » *
You have spoken of the Boer war, howling your idiotic
thanks to a God who doesn’t care whether the Austra-
lian ant slaughters the Boer ant, or vice versa. You
speak now of the Chinese war, demanding, in your silly
blood-fury, vengeance from Him on a people who in their
daily lives are immeasurably better worshippers than
yourselves. You will speak of the Plague, asking mercy
in sobbing whispers—those that are left of you.
* * » *
And—hearing you, perhaps, for an instant out of
Eternity—God will smile.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Is It Worth While?
The mighty English match-manufacturers, R.
Bell and Co., have lately caused trouble in Maori-
land. This firm received a Govt, subsidy of £l5OO to
start the match-making industry in Maoriland,
and after refusing to pay decent rates to Maori-
land girls, imported some London “ ’Arriets ” at
a starvation and body-selling wage to do their
cheap and nasty work. They were promptly
brought before the Conciliation and Arbitration
Board. The solicitor for Bell and Co. said that
if the wages demanded by the court were paid,
the industry would be “ seriously hampered
which remark called forth some very plain
speaking from the President (Judge Martin).
He said it was a consideration with the court
whether it would not be better to stop a trade or
industry altogether which could not give its em-
ployes a wage that would enable them to lead
decent lives—“ The province was not bettered
by an industry which forced girls on to the
streets to supplement their earnings.” The
statement did all possible credit to the Judge’s
heart, though, perhaps, hardly so much to
his head. The matcn-making trade can pay
a living wage as well as any other trade —
though whether it can do it and yet sell a package
of 12 boxes for 3d. is another matter. And no
decent community (are there any decent com-
munities?) wants 12 boxes for 3d. if they can only
be supplied by starving the work-girls and driving
them to prostitution. But to abolish the local in-


dustry is no remedy. That only means importing
matches instead of making them, and getting
cheap matches out of the misery and prostitution
of the English girl instead of the Maoriland. one,
which is quite as shameful, only not quite . so
visible. The remedy is to get rid of the English
firm of Bell and Co. with its sweating policy and
its cheap labor traditions; to have locally-
owned factories which will sell matches
at the cheapest rate consistent with civi-
lised conditions ; and to keep out the
foreign slave-wage-made matches with a heavy
duty. It will make matches dearer, most
assuredly. If matches are cheap because the
miserable girls who make them work incredible
hours for ss. a week, you can’t give them decent
hours and 15s. a week —which is little enough to
live upon—without making the product dearer.
No Christian community wants to sell its immor-
tal soul, and be a slave-driver, and to force its fel-
low-creatures to prostitution in order to keep skin
and bone together—merely for the sake of getting
matches a little cheaper. Of course the extra
price arising through the payment of a wage on
which a white girl could live and be a decent mem-
ber of society would be quoted by the glib and
one-idea’d Foreign-trader as another proof of the
evils of Protection —but the Freetrader doesn’t
matter.

The Weary, Weary Reid.
N.S.W.’s wearisome drawback, ex-Premier Reid,
who, in some respects, should surely be able to
hold an easy first place as the most incredibly in-
solent political charlatan on this earth, turned up
the other night in the Assembly to defend what
he calls his financial reputation. Not satisfied
with having his accounts reported on in the most
blighting fashion by three well-known business
men, two of whom were placed on the committee
of inquiry at his own suggestion, ex-Premier Reid
has been having them further overhauled by
another committee of three, which is all his own
committee this time. Like Premier Lyne, he
submitted certain questions to the committee to
answer, but with a wide difference. Lyne’s one
central question was : “ Supposing each year was
debited with its own fair and reasonable expendi-
ture—the expenditure properly chargeable to it—
leaving all fakes and devices out of the question,
how do each year’s finances pan out ? ” That
was the gist of Lyne’s question, and that
was just the one subject that N.S.W. wanted
to hear, about. The question which Reid
put to his committee —the committee which he
paid himself—was apparently, judged by its
answers, about this: “Did the revenue received
during 4£ years of my term of office fully cover the
expenditure which I charged to these 4i years ? It
isn’t your business to ask what should “have been
charged. Just stick to what I charged.” And
Reid’s committee reported that the revenue for
the 4J; years for which Reid was solely responsible
exceeded the sums actually debited by
Reid to these 4| years by £llo,llB—that there
was £llO,llB surplus, with certain reservations.
The reservations include a number of things. It
is specifically mentioned that sums paid on
account of Martin-place and Centennial Park
resumptions are not reckoned in the expenditure,
and these amounted to £161,445. Attention is
also drawn to the fact that no account is taken of
the fact that £201,647 for interest from 31st March
to 30th June, 1899, was not charged against the
revenue for the year ended 30th June, 1899. Then
Reid’s own committee begins to say things like *
these about Reid’s Cash Basis Fake:
The accounts of one kind and another actually owing at,
the close of the year must consequently always amount,
we should imagine, to a very large sum, and by charg-
ing back to previous years the accounts owing on June
30th, 1895, when the new system came into operation,
and not bringing into account the outstandings on June
30th, 1899, the expenditure between these two dates must
have been made to appear much smaller than would have
been the case under the old system.
Moreover the new system gave another considerable
advantage, especially during the first year—
The year of George Reid’s £330,000 or £340,000
surplus
—and it may be that the revenue account derived some
benefit from the new system introduced of spreading the
cost of certain unproductive works over a period of 30
years instead of meeting the whole cost at once out of
revenue as in years gone by.
So Reid’s own committee, paid by Reid, and with
the lines on which its inquiry was to be conducted
carefully fixed by Reid, practically and to all in-
tents and purposes reports thus :
If we don’t reckon £161,445 which you paid away, as
being paid away, and say nothing about interest (£201,647)
which you left unprovided for, and say nothing about the
fact that you saved a lot of money on paper by not
charging yourself either with the amounts owing when
you took office or the amounts owing when you left office
(charging one lot back to your predecessor and the other
lot forward to your successor), and if we say nothing
about how you made your outgoings look smaller by
charging to a new series of 30-year loans ordinary expen-
diture which your predecessors paid out of revenue, and
if we don t inquire at all about what ought to be charged
to each year but simply inquire about what you did
harge—then you had a small surplus.
Reid s finances have now been inquired into by
no less than three committees. The first one was
wholly his own committee. He appointed it in
1896, to guarantee the absolute genuineness of his
famous surplus, and, after overhauling things
carefully, it replied that it wasn’t prepared to say
that the surplus was genuine. In the second com-
mittee, two out of the three members were ap-
pointed on Reid’s suggestion they were men
whom he named and with whom he declared he
would be perfectly satisfied. And this second
committee showed up Reid’s political dodges,
fakes, and misrepresentations as no Aus-
tralian .Treasurer was ever shown up before. And
the third committee was all Reid’s committee,
selected, not directly, but by persons whom he
entrusted with the work of selection, and paid by
.A n d^ it says it can only throw a mode-
rately-favorable light on Reid’s accounts by not
■charging him with a vast number of things which,
it broadly hints, should be charged against him.
Dan t this impudent trickster find one intelligent
and well-known man of figures in the community
who will give him a straight-out certificate
ot political honesty and solvency ? So far,
even when he pays the man out of his own pocket,
he can’t get a clean bill of health.
State Governors.
William Lewis to The Bulletin :
At this moment the status of provincial or State Gover-
nors m the sister Dominion of Canada should be of in-
terest to Australians. These officials are invariably
Canadians, taken from the ranks of leading - public men,
and appointed by the Governor-General, acting - on the
advice of his responsible ministers. The present Governor,
or, to call him by his official title, Lieut.-Governor of
Ontario, is Oliver Mowat, for many years Premier of that
province, and former leader of the Liberal party. On-
tario has a population of over two millions, and is by far
the richest and most prosperous member of the Canadian
Federation. A* salar y the Executive is but
£2OOO. Crossing Lake Ontario we find the State of New
York, with a population verging on seven millions, in-
cluding within its bounds the second largest city of the
world, paying its Governor only the same stipend. His
office is justly considered as second only in dignity and
importance to that of the President, and has freer fllled
by men of even more than national reputation. Governor
Tilden, in ’76, missed the greater prize of the Presidency
by the merest fluke, while Cleveland in ’B4 found it but a
step from the State House at Albany to the White House
at Washington. In the matter of recompense to our
State Governors the question should receive more
careful consideration than has hitherto been given
to it. N.S.W. politicians are in an ultra liberal
frame of mind, stimulated, may we suggest, by the fear
that the neighboring southern State may outbid them in
the matter of gubernatorial salary. It cannot be con-
sidered censorious to say that £5OOO is an extravagant
sum with which to reward an office which must, of neces-
sity, be of a subordinate character. To the ordinary tax-
paying citizen it seems anomalous that a State with only
two-thirds the population of the Canadian province of
Ontario and hut one-fifth that of the State of New York ,
should seriously propose to pay its executive a salary one
hundred and fifty per cent, i.t excess of that paid to an
official performing practically the same functions in either
o/ the Commonwealths designated. It is well to have an
eye to the future. Increase of wealth and population
means increase of the executive duties and, properly, an
increase of recompense for the performance of those
duties. To initiate the office with a salary of £5OOO as
proposed, is to mortgage any possible increase for genera-
tions to come. As to the personnel of the State governors,
the office is not one likely to be sought after by English-
men of the first rank. Such men will not covet a position
in which they must play a subordinate part to that of the
Governor-General., If we insist on the Colonial Office
appointing these officials, pur choice lies between that
class of callow politician novices who have little save their
names to recommend them, and the great cloud of diplo-
matic has-beens who haunt the purlieus of Downing-
street.
Better to follow the safe Canadian precedent. No
Australian should hesitate in his choice for State governor
between a fellow-citizen of high character who has filled a
.prominent and honorable position in the affairs of his
colony, and an English appointee from either of the
classes indicated.

A Tale from the “Front.”
The glories of war ! Private Jas. Bartlett,
formerly of Newcastle (N.S.W.) police, returned
to Australia the other day, and was interviewed
by Newcastle Morning Herald. He fully bears
out Burdett-Coutts’ impeachment of the medical
arrangements at Bloemfontein. After telling,
among other things, that many of his comrades
were so exhausted by fatiguing marches that they
actually went to sleep in their first experience
under fire, the narrative proceeds:
Bartlett shortly afterwards contracted fever, and was
carted into Bloemfontein, where he was placed in No. 12
Brigade Field Hospital. The patients were coming in
very numerously, and six were being buried in a blanket
at the one time. There were nine or ten hospitals, and
from the former the deaths averaged 10 a day, the
average death-rate being 40 a day, principally from
enteric fever. In No. 10 the treatment the patients re-
ceived was simply scandalous. There were no sisters in
any of these hospitals, the orderlies of the R.A.M.C. doing
all the work, and there was only one stretcher for every
10 men. When Bartlett was admitted to the tent he was
placed on the wet ground, with not even a blanket to
cover him. The patients were supposed to get three pints of
milk and two pints of water a day, but they never received
the regulation allowance of anything. He subsisted 21
days on this diet. If a man came into the hospital
after 11 a.m. he was not seen by the doctor for 24 hours.
It was impossible to get a night-pan or a drink of water,
for that matter, during the night, and if a man was unable
to shift for himself he had a bad time. He had frequently
seen an orderly kick a man lying at death’s door in the
ribs, and tell him to get up, when the poor fellow was
barely able to move. Medicine was ordered two or three
times a day, but if a patient received one dose he con-
sidered himself very fortunate, and then it was not given
in anything approaching a decent manner. The bottle
was placed to the man’s mouth, and by the time he had
swallowed the medicine he had possibly taken three times
more than the doctor prescribed. They were also sup-
posed to have a wash and to be sponged all over once a
day, but Bartlett only had two ablutions in 30 days. He
frequently" asked for a bucket, but could never g-et one,
and the only way to get water was to fill the water-bottle.
The way he managed to get a wash was by one of his com-
rades pouring the water into his hands and swilling his
face in this fashion.
Nos. 9 and 12 hospitals were the worst of the lot, and if
he had not witnessed the scenes with his own eyes,
Bartlett could not have believed that one white
man could treat another in such a brutal manner.
He has known patients to be ordered brandy
and milk, but the orderlies kept the brandy, and the suf-
ferers did not even get the milk. There was in reality no
attention paid the patients, and it was little wonder that
the Tommies always expressed a wish to be sent to the
N.S.W. Hospital. No. 12 hospital was eventually re-
moved half a mile from the town, and on that day none
of the patients saw a doctor. Bartlett was told to get on
to a waggon, which already contained a huge latrine, and
upon saying that he could not walk, let alone jump, he
was told to b well walk. The tents were pitched any-
how, and the walls not being pegged, the cold night winds
fairly whistled through. One man crawled outside, and
as he did so the ants and flies almost carried him away.
He was lying face downwards, with not a soul near him,
and eventually he crawled back into the tent, and died.
Next morning one of the officers came along and shoved
the man with his boot, telling him to get up with the
others ; but the poor fellow remained motionless, and it
was then the officer discovered that the man was dead.
There were only two pans in the hospital for the use of
all the patients, and the only wonder to Bartlett is that
they did not all die, so crueily were they treated.
Several times he saw the orderlies quarrel over the dis-
tribution of a dead man’s kit. As soon as a man breathed
his last a rush was made for his kit by the orderlies, and
it was nothing short of an outrage to see how they quar-
relled and fought over the possession of the poor fellow’s
effects. He frequently heard them refuse to give a
patient medicine, saying that it would only be wasted on
such a man. When he applied for a blanket, the officer
gave Bartlett one covered with filth, and fairly alive
with vermin. He told the surgeon that he would not
place such a blanket in his dog-kennel, and it was then
taken from him. He afterwards saw a stretcher lying in
the tent, and with the aid of a comrade he managed to
place it under him, as the damp ground was producing
aches and pains all over his body. The stretcher, how-
ever, was wanted the next morning to remove the dead
men, and he had again to lie on the wet ground. There
was really no care or comfort whatever in the R.A.M.C.
hospitals, but everything in connection with the N.S.W.
was carried out with the utmost satisfaction.
Reforming 1 the Victorian 48 Rich
Landlords.
When Victoria’s House of 48 Rich Landlords
showed signs of “turning dog” on the Woman’s
Suffrage Bill for the fifth time, the local Age pro-
posed, as a means of coercion, that the Assembly
should refuse to pass the Federal Electorates Bill
unless the Upper Tomb gave way. The
refusal would mean that, at the election
for the Federal House of Representatives,
all Victoria would be one constituency
on the one-man-one-vote principle, and the
Liberal party would sweep the polls, whereas
under the electorate system there are at least two
constituencies that are a gift to the Tories. It
was a good idea in its way, but if it succeeded,
and the Woman’s Suffrage Bill (which applies
only to the already democratic Assembly) was
carried, the 48 Rich Landlords would be left as
Tory, as unreformed, as much a stumbling-block
to all progress, as ever. What is wanted is a
workable scheme for compelling or inducing the
Dreadful Four Dozen to reform themselves—not
one for inducing them to consent to further re-
form of the Assembly, which is a Liberal institu-
tion now, but which sees most of its progressive
measures rejected by the House of Landlords.
The Age, with all its vague demands for
reform, has never propounded any such
scheme, good, bad, or indifferent. The
Bulletin, which doesn’t believe in vague, unpro-
fitable yearnings, has frequently proposed the
introduction of a Bill providing that the Council
shall in future be a paid body, with no property-
qualification, elected by absolute adult suffrage
with the whole province voting as one consti-
tuency, and further setting forth that Parliament,
m view of the great services of the present 48
Rich Landlords rendered for many years without
pay (this would be irony, but the fact of its being
irony needn’t be mentioned) decreed them a per-
petual free railway-pass, the permanent retention
of the title of “ Hon.,” and a life pension of £lOOO
a year each. It would be shameless bribery cer-
tainly, but nothing save bribery or insurrection is
at all likely to shift the 48 Rich Landlords, [f
the thing was put nicely it would probably catch
enough votes to carry it. Many of the Landlords
are sufficiently mortgaged to make it a very hard
thing to resist an offer of £lOOO a year—they might
refuse it once or twice, but they would grab at it
m the end. While they hesitated it would
bring a rush of new candidates at every Council
Election new candidates yearning to get in and
vote for the Bill next time it appeared, and get
the £lOOO a year for themselves. That bait always
dangling in view would upset all the Council’s
traditions, and shatter its holy calm into 70,000
fragments. When the offer was at last accepted
it would oiily cost £48,000 a year at most, and it
wouldn’t cost that for long, as the Councillors are
not young men, and many of them look like
walking incarnations of Apoplexy, Cirrhosis, and
Fatty Degeneration. And the new reformed
Council could promptly pass an extra land-tax on
big estates which would yield more than £48,000 a
year, and thus make the class which created the
48 Rich Landlords pay for their extinction. Then
an absentee-tax would also be possible, and a law
for the compulsory resumption of large estates for
closer settlement- in fact everything would be
possible. Victoria is full of democrats who are
“ in favor of the reform of the Upper House,” but
not one of them has yet advanced a scheme for
inducing or compelling the Upper House to con-
sent to its own reform, and while that great want
lasts all these democrats are quite useless. Will
the Victorian Labor-party either adopt this
scheme or produce one of its own ? It is time to
drop vague good wishes for reform and set about
real reform.
Victoria s Crown Law authorities have an over-
powering respect for legal forms. Recently a
village settler at Kooweerup swamp was fined
2s. Gd. for failing to send his boy to school for the
statutory number of days in the preceding quarter.
The lad was only absent two days short of the
required number, and there was also the good
excuse that he had to walk seven miles to school,
through swamps, etc., barefoot, the family being
too poor to provide boots. The settler couldn’t
pay even the poor half-crown, but promised to
stump up as soon as able ; nevertheless, a few
days later a constable called and demanded the
penalty, and, failing to get what the man hadn’t
got, actually took him 30 miles to Melbourne, and
locked him up for the alternative six hours ! At
the end of that period he was turned adrift
in the streets, 30 miles from home, without a
penny in his pocket. Meanwhile, there are
thousands of truants in Melbourne 800 in
Hawthorn alone—and nobody makes any attempt
to round them up and find out why they
never go to school at all. And think of the sinful
waste of sending two policemen 60 miles and one
defendant 30 miles all to impose six hours’ gaol
for non-payment of 2s. 6d. ! Why can’t the bobby
in such cases carry a portable india-rubber gaol
under his arm, and let the defendant do his six
.lours in it on the spot ? Or supposing defendant
has a big trunk or a woodshed or a hollow tree,
why shouldn’t the bobby be authorised to proclaim
it a temporary gaol within the meaning of the
Act, and put defendant in it for the necessary six
hours—or 4i hours, when the ordinary remission
for good conduct is deducted ?

The seizure and lodgment in gao. of N.S.W.
aborigines, under the Breelong panic, is a more
serious piece of police or Executive high-handed-
ness than the community appears to realise.
These handcuffed and leg-ironed aborigines have
an undoubted, right to legal redress. It was never
ruled that aborigines come under the Vagrant Act.
In fact, there are numerous rulings showing that
they are purposely excluded from the operation of
that Act. Only for this they could be arrested
wholesale all over the territory. Any backbloeks
squatter could clear his countryside right away.
Yet this Act is the only defence set up by the
authorities for laying by the heels as many of the
unfortunate race as scared policemen thought it
necessary to imprison. Probably a spell of gaol
will, in the circumstances, do the blacks little
harm. But that is not the question. The real
harm is the tyrannous official distortion of law.

Jack Want or somebody wrote the following in a
disguised hand, and blackened his face and put on
a false beard, and posted it at midnight, and the
S.M. Herald published it: —
Sir,—Might I suggest as the crowning act preceding the
proclamation of the federal constitution, and as a small
token of the public gratitude due to the Right Honour-
able the learler of the Opposition, the meetness of in-
augurating a monster demonstration of the citizens of
each large centre of population in the colony, led by the
citizens of Sydney, in recognition of the practical initia-
tion and actual accomplishment of Australian federation,
due so largely, if not solely, to Mr. Reid. I would propose
that this great movement should be simultaneous—that
is, on the same day throughout N.S.W. This would
be supplemental to the recognition of the energetic
and untiring ideal and theoretical work of others, and a
just honor to him who realised and carried into effect
what they so ably and unceasingly preached. I feel sure
that Mr. Barton, to whom so much is due for his federal
work, and Mr. M'Millan, who so generously recognised
Mr. Reid’s major share in the accomplishment of Federa-
tion, as well as those others to whom the colony dele-
gated their federal interests at the Convention, will
heartily endorse and work together in carrying out what
only justice demands and honor as recognisedly due
exacts.—l am, etc., Federal Australian.
What are they giving us? No- Yes was always
anti-Federal, but after Barton and others had,
at infinite pains, blown life into the dead ashes of
Federation and it was dangerous to longer ignore
it, Reid, wishing to nobble Federal votes, moved
in the matte ]of the Convention. At its sit-
tings he evinced his ardor by snoring dis-
cordantly, and when it concluded -he stumped
N.S.W. against the Bill and denounced clauses
he had himself voted to include, but, still
with a glazed glare on Federal support, stated that
in spite of its imperfections he would vote for the
measure. Also, as an earnest of his zeal he
allowed the iniquitous minimum vote clause to be
added to the Enabling Bill. It is safe to bet that
had George, usually an adept at guessing how the
cat will jump, foreseen the immensity of the Union
vote, he would have yelled himself hoarse for the
original Bill. He expected the measure to be lost
and then he could have posed as the chap who
tried to pass it; but circumstances were too strong
for him, and a very inconvenient matter would
have been satisfactorily squelched for a long time.
It is to be hoped that the proposed meetings will
be held, and a resolution to this effect passed ;
“That George Reid endeavored to wreck the
cause of Australian union, and that he is a
political posturer and bounder and rail-sitter of
the worst type.”
The city of Brisbane is rapidly earning for itself
the reputation of being the most wooden-headed
municipality in Australasia. Not content with
having allowed that magnificent asset, the city
tramways, to drift into private hands, it appears
to be prepared just now to allow the electric light-
ing- of the town to become the business of a
syndicate also. A poll of ratepayers taken
a few days ago on the matter gave
the following amazing result. One thousand
and ninety votes were recorded in favor of
the Council’s undertaking the installation of the
electric light on its own account, while 1319 votes
were given against the proposal, which was thus
defeated by 229 votes. The defeat was bad
enough, but the public indifference displayed in
the matter was infinitely worse. Out of 8953
votes on the roll (or (1442 voters) the total number
of individuals voting was 1332. And this on a
matter of the most vital interest to every ratepayer
of a city where the taxes are mounting by leaps and
bounds. From this poll one comes to a variety
of conclusions. In the first place the people of
Brisbane haven’t any faith in the old order of
aldermen, otherwise they would have rolled up
to support them and their private lighting
proposals. Then again, for a similar reason, it
might be argued they haven’t any faith in the new
municipalising order of aldermen. Further, it
would appear also that the Brisbaneites haven’t
any faith in themselves, or their votes, or their
capacity for running things. Well, perhaps
they’re right. A city that went frantic with
enthusiasm over the Anti-Federal business and
set up the idiotic Brisbane Telegraph as its Joss
and Bible, is quite right to mistrust itself and its
capacities to the bitter end.

Premier Section proposed in his financial state-
ment toabolish theMaoriland duty onmatches, and
this has been promptly followed by the shutting
down of the local match factories. Freetraders, of
course, say that if an industry which has been
protected by a duty for ten years or so can’t get
along now without a duty it ought to go—which
is just as wise as saying that if Britain, after
having been protected by an army and
navy for five centuries or so, can’t get on
now without an army and navy it ought
to go. Matches are made cheaply in England
because the girls who make, them work hideous
hours, under hideougly-insanitary conditions, foi-
ls. or ss. a week, and in many cases are driven to
eke out their miserable earnings by prostitution.
Even after 1000 years of Protection the Maoriland
match-industry wouldn’t be able to compete in the
open market with the slave-made British product
while Maoriland pays its girl-operatives a decent
white girl’s wage. So Maoriland has to choose
between paying a trifle more for its matches
while giving the makers a living wage,
or getting them a trifle cheaper at the
price of patronising and encouraging sweat-
ing and compulsory prostitution. And Pre-
mier Seddon, who is apparently leaning towards
British Freetrade ideas as another result of his
new outbreak of British war-patriotism, throws in
his lot in this matter with the cause of cheapness
and sweaterdom. It is just as mean, disgraceful,
unchristian, and undemocratic to patronise the
European sweater, when it is possible to avoid
doing so, as to encourage the local sweater, and
the old Seddon would never have done such a
thing. The new Seddon appears to be built on
different lines, somehow.

That was a significant answer made by Premier
Lyne to a question put him as to whether he
favored the occupation by one man of seats both
in the State and Federal Parliaments. Lyne
wriggled, but, in the end, vaguely defined his alti-
tude. He said “he had not given the matter
very careful consideration” (!!!), and then he
“ gave it as his opinion that the ques-
tion should be dealt with at the general
elections.” So he hadn’t given the matter
“ very careful consideration ” ! Incredible.
The matter is not only one obviously including tf.e
very political life-blood of Premier Lyne himself,
but it has been a burning one in Cabinet and
Parliamentary circles for months past. Ex-
Premier Reid, four weeks ago, made an emphatic
declaration against dual seats. Just look through
this escape door held congenially ajar.
The next general elections may not take place till
October, 1901. The present Parliament does not
expire till August. The Federal poll will be taken
in February and March, and Lyne and four of his
colleagues, and more than 50 per cent, of members
are in the field as candidates. If the question of
dual seats be postponed till the next State Parlia-
ment determines the matter, then Lyne, if
successful in the Federal fight, might retain
the State Premiership and a seat in the Common-
wealth Parliament for eight months at least; and
his successful colleagues would be somewhat
similarly favored; while successful State mem-
bers would draw double salaries for dual duties
from the meeting of the Federal Parliament till
the dissolution of N. S. W. Assembly. Again, should
the Lyne Ministry be returned at the General
Election by a good majority, and should
many State members also hold seats in the Federal
Parliament, is there any guarantee that Lyne, in
October of next year, will have given the test
question “any serious consideration”? As he
believes in shirking the question now, he may
be relied upon to shirk it with greater de-
termination than ever should he successfully run
tlie gauntlet of the Federal and State elections.
The idea of awaiting the verdict of the State
elections is the shallowest of subterfuges. The
people are just as unanimously opposed to dual
seats as they are opposed to dual billets with dual
salaries in the civil service. If Lyne is only
temporarily opposed to legislation to meet the
question then he is seeking a privilege which will
not be extended to any future State premier. If
the principle of dual representation is admittedly
pernicious why should its abolition be deferred in
the special interests of placemen for the time
being ?
The Boer plot to kidnap Lord Roberts and kill
his staff may have had its genesis in the reading
of a book written by a most excellent English-
man. Sir Thomas More, in his “ Utopia,” thus
describes the methods of the Utopians in their
conduct of war:—
As soon as they declare war they take care to have a
great many schedules, that are sealed with their common
seal, affixed in the most conspicuous places of their
enemies’ country. This is carried out secretly and done in
many places all at once. In these they promise great
rewards to such as shall kill the prince, and lesser in pro-
portion to such as shall kill any other persons, who
are those on whom, next to the prince himself,
they cast the chief balance of the war. And they
double the sum to him that, instead of killing the
person so marked out, shall take him alive and put him
in their hands. . . . They very much approve of this way
of corrupting their enemies, though it appears to others
to he base and cruel; but they look on it as a wise course,
to make an end of what would be otherwise a long war,
without so much as hazarding one battle to de-
cide it. They think it likewise an act of mercy
and love to mankind to prevent the great slaughter of
those that must otherwise be killed in the progress
of the war, both on their own side and on that of their
enemy, by the death of a few that are most guilty ; and
