SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “ The Rose of Persia.”
Royal “ How London Lives.”
Tivoli Rickards’ Co.
Criterion “ The Power of Wealth.”
Palace “What Happened to Jones.”
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “ Trilby.”
Theatre Royal “ Serving the Queen.”
Bijou Rickards’ Variety Co.
A great deal of money Happened to Jones
on that maddened commercial traveller’s opening
night in Sydney. The Palace theatre overflowed,
and the public surged in the painted vestibule,
and jammed on the marble stairs, and poured over
the costly decorations generally. The boom has
rather increased than diminished since then as
Jones and his lost coat-tail grow more familiar, so
it seems that the American bagman has come to
stay. Arnold’s new play is fun pure and
simple. An unworldly professor is beguiled to
a prize-fight because he is told it is to be a
great exhibition of “science,” and he gets a
black eye when the police stop the exhibition,
and as no such disgraceful thing ever happened to
him before he tries unworthy means of conceal-
ment. Therefore when Jones, who has stoushed
a policeman in getting away and is “ wanted,”
takes refuge in his house, and refuses to quit till
the coast is clear, the sad old man with the black
eye lacks the courage to throw Jones out. He
becomes a temporary slave to Jones as the price
of that airy bagman’s silence about his eye and
the prize-fight. He lets Jones pass himself off as
a clerical brother from Australia. He doesn’t
even murmur at Jones’s clothes being concealed in
the piano, and when Jones, in his capacity as a
■relative, kisses the family generally, he only has
fits silently in a corner. The play is wrongly
named, for nothing really happens to Jones —it is
Jones who happens to the old man. Even when
the real clerical brother turns up, and, because
Jones has his clothes, moons round the house in a
blanket, the old gentleman takes it in a chastened
spirit. “ Jones ”is built on the same lines as a
hundred other farcical comedies ; the old man who
has been out on a frisky night and has a black-
ened optic and a mystery to conceal from his wife
is the oldest of comedy props, and Jones as a
clergyman trying to explain the nature of the
kangaroo, is brother to Walker of London as an
African explorer expounding the ourang-outang.
But the new production is good enough to go all
the same.
* * * 9
Charles Arnold has brought an excellent
company with him. Hans the Boatman of olden
days is a revelation in farcical comedy; it is a
line of business of which he was not generally
suspected, and the cheerful, airy insolence of his
bagman Jones reveals the poetic Hans in quite a
new character. His brightness does wonders to
disguise the fact that the resources of “Jones”
are in themselves a trifle limited. It looks a very
clever comedy as Arnold does it, but in the hands
of anyone a little slower and heavier it would pro-
bably fall down very suddenly. Frederic Sharp’s
crushed professor is memorable. He reveals new
possibilities in the way of the crushed husband
and flattened parent; he is an ambling apology
and a chronic cry of woe. Five ladies of the
flattened one’s family—Miss Agnes Knights, Inez
Bensusan, Hope Mayne, Dot Frederic, and Sallie
Booth —give such general satisfaction that the
work of disentangling them is postponed. Miss
Dot Frederic, however, has the largest oppor-
tunity, and she fills it like a gleam of sunshine, and
is only restrained with great difficulty from finding
Jones’s pants in the piano. Also, Miss Ada Lee
deserves a line to herself; the sluggish, foreign
slavey, who blackmails her master in a foreign
accent, is an institution that is going to be very
popular.
* • * •
Miss Frederic bas another opportunity in
a pretty little one-act introductory play, “An
Empty Stocking.” This is an attic idyll, in which
an unsuccessful writer is trying hard to bring up
a hungry child in cold weather. A good deal of
pathos centres round a dubious leg of mutton and
an orange. The small and empty one has hung
up one of her two stockings to see if Santa Claus
will put anything in it, and owing to extraordinary
circumstances that mythical person is more punc-
tual than usual. A baby gem, little Edna Arnold,
gets most of the credit out of this little scene;
but Miss Frederic, as a disagreeable girl from
downstairs, comes a good second, and there is
some honor left over for Frank Denton and
George Willoughby—the former in particular.
** * *
A gentleman who can’t be too warmly en-
couraged has opened a novel variety show at
the Temperance Hall, opposite Sydney Criterion.
It is mainly anatomical, and deals with sections of
the human frame in glass cases and elsewhere. As
one goes in, the bony, hairless vestige of what was
once his best girl, or what his best girl will one
day become, stands before him in an easy attitude
in the centre of the f100r... She has nothing on—
not even her flesh, and she is kept upright by a
wire. Then the visitor realises for the first time
how ridiculously long his best girl’s toes really
are, and how they look like five distorted pen-
holders sticking out of her foot. On the left are a
scalped head showing the skull; another with the
skull partly sawn off showing the brain like a mis-
cellaneous substance in a jug; and another
with the brain emptied out —the thinking
substance being alongside on a saucer.
Samples of heads troubled with uncommon
diseases are plentiful—one very shocking head
in particular is calculated to make the public
swear off drink for ever lest it should meet the
wearer with that head on in the garden of its
dreams. The internal organs of people who died
through drink are a strongfeature. One of them is a
huge, flattened-out, brownish substance that
seems to have settled down in its case like a legal
precedent settling down on the chest of unborn
generations. There is a baby ribbon, or some-
thing akin thereto, round this affrighting object,
and it is somehow reminiscent of the countenance
of the policeman that Happened to Jpnes. It is
the hobnailed liver of a person who was brought
down on the bottle, and, judging by its size, he
must have worn it doubled up. A casual, baggy
circumstance that won’t stand up by itself and re-
quires to be propped proves to be a plain, ordi-
nary, every-day stomach, and the stout, darkish
objects that might be small footballs struck by
lightning are kidneys. A nicely-dressed lady in a
case, completely clothed except as to her head and
feet, is lectured upon and unscrewed to show how
one’s best girl is constructed.
* * * •
And, after seeing all these and many
more, including the full-sized skinned citizen, no
person of imagination could really want to have a
best girl again. And no one would respect a great
thinker again; he would simply say: “I know
what he does it with ; I saw it in a saucer !” It is
all very good to realise that we are fearfully and
wonderfully made, but it seems a | ity that we are
made so red and damp, and with so many bluish
streaks and stringy organs and baggy substances.
Also the writer has a grievance that he will never
get over against some of his own vitals after seeing
how they sink down and spread out in a large pool
on a plate. Still he will probably go back and
look at all these things again. It may not be
pleasant to think that he looks like that,
but it is satisfactory to reflect that many
people whom he knows and doesn’t like
look like that also. When he considers the
huge brown liver, and remembers that everyone of
his creditors has a matter of that kind inside him,
there is satisfaction in it. Also when he looks at
the baggy internal organ, and remembers the man
who didn’t appreciate his best poem, there is balm
in the conjunction of ideas ; a person containing
such a common-looking substance would naturally
be unable to understand poetry. In short, every-
body who is blighted or lost or unappreciated
should go to the Temperance Hall and see what
his detractors are composed of, and then ask him-
self if anything better can be expected from people
with such contents. This scribe, anyhow, has
now sworn off the foolish habit of lending money—
has sworn off for ever. He never knew before
what kind of thing he was lending it to. Now
that he does know he would as soon trust a fantod
or a jim-jam.

“Rose of Persia ” has only another week
to run at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, and this de-
serving opera will depart much regretted. There
is a general impression that it might have stayed
longer if it had wanted to, but possibly previous
arrangements got in the way. The Bulletin will
try to fix up a fitting epitaph next week, and
meanwhile that section of the public which hasn’t
yet seen Brownlow’s latest monarch is warned
that time is fleeting and seats in the front row are
scarce.

Bland Holt undertakes to produce this
Saturday an entirely new play called “How
London Lives.” Therefore the “ Absent-Minded
Beggar ” will cease on Friday night, and he bids
fair to leave De Wet still uncaptured, and the
drama’s namesake still hustling vainly after the
agile Botha.
** * *
The earthquake which brings down a large
building on top of the erring wife at Sydney
Criterion is beginning to attract some notice.
The “ Power of Wealth ” is a deserving play', and
some of the characters in it are more than deserv-
ing—notably Dampier himself, Buffer’s cynical
old clo’ man, and the bright and original Italian
drunk mentioned last week. And the scene
wherein Miss Lily Dampier finds that she has re-
married her lost husband —the man who was
buried alive —is a really powerful piece of
work. But the impressive earthquake which
should end this scene is apt to be erratic,
and on two occasions a piece of the building has
fallen prematurely on Miss Dampier, and some-
what injured the coherence of things. The writer
here diverges violently to state that Mr. Dampier
lately gave a benefit in aid of St. Vincent’s Hos-
pital. Gross takings were a little over £110;
expenses, £lO 10s.; profit for the hospital, £lOO
and some fraction. Which casts a little extra
disgrace on that much larger society-benefit at the
Lyceum, where the takings were nearly twice as
much, and the balance which was left for charity
was £3 14s. Id. That £3 14s. Id. will take a great
deal of hard forgetting. By way of further diver-
gence and parenthesis it may be mentioned that
“ Hamlet” is still on view at the Criterion Friday
nights.
** * *
No new stars have appeared in the Tivoli
firmament, but several old stars have furbished
themselves up a little. Madame Cordelia has a
song that seems to be the request of a young man
tohisgirl to be as pure asthespring. Present writer
has not noticed any more notable purity about the
spring tide or the spring mattress (whichever is
meant) than about any other bed or season.
Charles Walker’s song, “Take your clothes
and go,” is punctuated by the clothes
in question, but they are quite harmless —
merely a bundle of collars and one cuff. The
Coon Ballet is beginning to be a weariness, mainly
because the coons are rather stiff in the joints and
the coon-leader rather small in the voice.
** * *
“Constance Clyde ” is justly severe on a
“ drammer ” :
There has just concluded, at Sydney Royal Standard,
an impressive “drammer” called “Waiting for the Ver-
dict,” in which a good many crimes are offered to an
appreciative audience for 3d. (best seats, Is.). It is the
usual pathetic tale of the man (young this time), who
clings to the old home, though the Lord of the Castle has
offered a goodly sum for it, and though his wife is sick and
his aged parent is enjoying nothing to eat in the same Old
Home, which, as staged, seems to consist mostly of one wall
gone askew and an eanviggy attic. The hero, who is very
well dressed, declares himself to be out of work, and as
there is no prospect of his getting any work, his conduct
in clinging to the Old Home where the wife and the aged
but invisible parent starve haughtily, when he might get
much money and buy food with it by selling the place, is
as much out of dravving as the Old Home itself. Mean-
while, the agent of the Lord of the Castle is so anxious to
add the Old Home to his master’s stately halls that he
bribes his own special agent to get the hero arrested for
poaching. The agent’s agent thereupon gives the hero
an alleged hare tied up in a bag, whereupon the agent
and the local magistrate and a policeman appear upon the
scene, and proceed to try the case in the hero’s backyard.
Nobody thinks of looking into the bag to see if it really is
a hare, so the noble-hearted clergyman pays a five pounds
fine for what may just as well be a cat, or some-
body’s washing, or to-morrow’s leg of mutton. Later
the Lord of the Castle is shown as overcome
with remorse for having led somebody’s sister astray.
The led-astray girl's brother then comes in and offers to
settle the matter by a duel in the moonlight glade. The
young Lord goes to the glade aforesaid and whiles away
the time, while waiting, by counting over a thousand
pounds in notes, which he happens to have about him, in
order that the agent’s agent ma.v have some reason to kill
him with a club, which he promptly does. The hero, who
has come along to hit the agent’s agent with another
club, finds the Lord of the Castle beside a blasted oak,
and for no visible reason rushes off, leaving his hat
as incriminating evidence behind him. Thereupon,
the led - astray girl’s brother, who intended to kill
the Lord himself, is very indignant at this das-
tardly crime, and vows to bring the murderer
to the gallows. There is nothing whatever against
the hero except the hat, so he is put in a felon’s cell, and
dressed in an effective navy-blue suit with small arrows
all over it, and a liberal allowance of clanking chain. He
is about to be introduced to an uncertain and harmless-
looking gallows, when various people wander casually
into the place of execution to say that he is innocent.
The led-astray girl's brother, or someone very like him,
tumbles in with a reprieve which he finds he hasn't got,
because the agent, who is still determined to get the cot-
tage, sneaked it away from him. Then the wife rushes
in with a club, or a bar of iron, or something, with
which the deed was really done, and at last the
agent's agent comes in and confesses to the crime
in order to annoy the agent. As the A.’s A. will be
hanged himself, and as nothing very much can be done to
the agent, who has not killed anyone, this scheme of ven-
geance seems a little bit off the track. Anyway, by virtue
of the bar of iron, and the confession, and the reprieve
that wasn’t there, the hero gets off and retires to the
Humble Home to starve cheerfully as before. The moral
of this play seems to be that small, ricketty cottages are
a great inducement to crime in people who possess large-
sized castles, and that if poor people were forced to live
in really big, comfortable places a great deal of stage-
villainy would be spared.
*« * *
The last but one performance of the
Brough Co. at Melbourne Princess’s was an in-
teresting revival of “ The Village Priest,” with
trusty Robert in the name part. To call R.B. a
“ great "’ impersonator of the good old abbe would
be an exaggeration. He doesn’t thrill his
audience in the miracle scene any more than
Titheradge did of yore. The story goes that the
late Bill Leake once upon a time gave a startling
impression of the priest whose open Bible gets
touched by the hand of God. The Bulletin will
not quarrel with the theory that Leake (rest his
soul!) was a stronger artist than Brough in his
feeling for a holy stage miracle. All the same
Brough’s treatment of the character is highly
effective, and as further evidence of his versa-
tility it is remarkable. The man who can
in one short season produce clever pictures of
Lord Bapchild, two or three polished worldlings,
the clownish police constable in “ Dandy Dick,”
and finally L’Abbe Dubois, is an actor with a
large “ A.” Everybody remembers Brough as
the escaped convict in “The Village Priest”—
the nervous force of his railing against the unjust
judge, the sincerity of his grief, the many touches
of nature (i.e., art) that won sympathy for Jean
Torquenie. In Mario Majeroni’s hands the con-
vict seems not half as real as he used to be,
although tall Mario is an intelligent player and a
striking stage figure. This comparative failure is
merely instanced out of compliment to the
admirable all-round artist who runs the co.
** * *
On Monday the Broughs revived “The
Tyranny of Tears ” for their farewell appearance,
and now they are in Adelaide, or thereabouts.
The Princess’s for the next seven weeks will be
awaiting the arrival of George Musgrove’s opening
show —a grand opera, the name whereof is about
to burst into print. The pretty theatre is to be
refurnished and generally touched up at vast
expense to G.M. aforesaid, and rumor suggests
that the return of the prodigal manager from
London —Nellie Stewart is to be of the co.
—will be celebrated by an advance' in the
Erice of boss seat 3. Meanwhile, the stage is a
arren waste, and the electric-light is turned off
at the meter.

Miss Edith Crane, and other people whom
Melbourne either affectionately remembers or
will be glad to meet for the first time, falls due at
Her Majesty’s on Saturday. They start with
“Trilby.” The Svengali drama made its biggest
Australian success in Melbourne four years ago,
and since then it has gathered some more noto-
riety from a burlesque, so, both things considered,
the revival of “Trilby” should hum joyfully.
#* * *
It happens that Her Majesty’s is likewise
out of work for a couple of nights. Nance O’Neil
was advertised to wind up her profitable season on
Wednesday, and at time of writing the advt.
showed no signs of departing from its word.
There will be a return of Nance anon, but for the
present she has passed away .from Melbourne,
leaving two widely different opinions behind her.
The writer of this notice holds on with both hands
and all his remaining teeth to the opinion that
Nance in her capacity of “star” actress playing
famous “star” characters is a gigantic de-
lusion, and to anyone who quotes the gor-
geous notices bestowed on Nance by the press,
and points to her large audiences as proof of her
ability, this scribe replies that Amy Castles got
gorgeous notices, and her show was rushed by
24,000 people in two nights. And yet this scribe
will cheerfully admit that Nance does not altoge-
ther disappoint her non-admirers, meaning those
who say they “don’t see so very much in her.”
As a matter of fact, they do see very much in her.
She is the biggest actress of commanding charac-
ters they have ever witnessed, and she is doubtless
the only Fedora, Magda, Camille, &c., in the
wide world who looks out of place when she sits
down.
»* * *
The ancient “ Ingomar” was Nance’s bill
last Saturday night. “ Ingomar ”is a queer old-
fashioned yarn, in stilted verse (blank and other-
wise) of a mythical barbarian who wore long,
greasy curls on the back of his head, until a white-
robed maiden, of mythical artlessness, soothed his
savage breast and induced him to get his hair cut.
“ Ingomar ” is not an exciting play, and the day
when leading ladies fancied themselves as Par-
tlienia is yesterday, as a rule. Beautiful Mary
Anderson brought Parthenia back to light some
years ago, therefore Mary’s charming young dis-
ciple, Essie deny ns, played the part in Australia.
A graceful woman, who is also a finished elocution-
ist, can make herself very pleasant as Parthenia,
especially if she be contrasted with a heavy and
barbaric Ingomar. Nance O’Neil is not conspicu-
ously graceful, neither is her elocution good, or
her face poetic in its expressions, so the contrast
between massive Nance and beauteous Plimmer in
the name part was an entertaining novelty. It
set one wondering whether Nance was taming
Plimmah or Plimmah was taming Nance.
** * *
At Melbourne Royal, the drama of the
moment is a fairly peaceable matter, known as
“Serving the Queen,” and its course of military
color and gilded sin covers five acts. The charac-
ters belong mostly to the Queen’s Own Lancers,
and they often threaten to go forth and save the
Empire, as British soldiers should, but some of
them are busily engaged in wrecking the happi-
ness of Inman, whilst the others are proving his
innocence, so the Empire has to keep the Flag
flying without any assistance from the regiment
in question. “Serving the Queen,” in fact,
is the sort of military drama that might
just as well be based on the boot - trade
or me butter industry, but for the moral
effect of the uniforms that furnish a neces-
sary clue to the title. Inman, apparently, is a
simple, virtuous trooper, beloved by the gallant
Colonel’s daughter. Also he has a mother who
nurses lunatics at a public asylum, and when
mother and sort meet a learned judge by acci-
dent at Shorncliffe camp, the old lady casually
informs her offspring that the learned judge
is his father. Nothing comes of the re-
lationship until Inman has been sentenced
to a term of lunatic asylum for murdering
a parson, after which the villain, H. O. Willard,
is run to earth at the instigation of a
Hebrew money-lender with a kind heart. In one
of the preceding dramas villain Willard didn’t get
overthrown, which explains the particular delight
of the gallery at Willard’s downfall on this occa-
sion. The “comic relief,” by Bert Bailey,
Herbert Linden, and Miss Hilda Fraser, is not
the saddest part of “ Serving the Queen.” In the
agony department Inman, Miss Duggan, Willard,
and Godfrey Cass are leading features, also Harry
Douglas is a somewhat commendable Israelite, and
the scenery plays its part with much intelligence.
“Serving trie Queen” will run for three weeks
and then retire in favor of John F. Sheridan and
Co.

Harry Shine is the latest returned wan-
derer at Melb. Bijou. Harry’s talent for getting
through a lot of remarks in a brief period helps to
balance the programme as it were, because the
Martinettis say nothing in forty minutes. Panto-
mimist Paul and his co. produced “The Keyhole”
on Saturday night. “ The Keyhole ”is an in-
genious little dumb-show, containing more humor
and less extravagance than the knockabout “ Duel
in the Snow,” but seeing that the Martinettis will
be moving along towards England in another week
or so, they would supply a want by reviving
“Robert Macaire” before they go. “ Macaire”
is the panto, that has a strong backbone and an
immortal soul.
The Wild Cat Column.
When the A.M.P. Society issues its ballot
papers for a straight “ yes” or “ no ” vote on the proposal
to give the directors power to extend the business,
if they choose, to England, Canada, Sierra Leone,
India, Ceylon, Burmah, Hong Kong, British Guiana
and all the other healthy or unhealthy spots of
the British empire, The Bulletin urges every mem-
ber to vote No, for these reasons among many
others : (1) The assurance that the extension will lead to
a great increase of business is quite true, but in a mutual
concern increased business does not mean increased profits
for the members, unless the new business is as profitable
as the old. The Australian and outside policy-holders
must share the profits equally, and even if the A.M.P.
doubles its business by going outside Australia, and even
if the new business pays handsomely, still unless it pays as
handsomely as the Australian business does the Aus-
tralian policy-holders will get less profit. (2) Tiie
Bulletin’s reasons for saying that the British
business won’t pay as handsomely as the Aus-
tralian business does are these: (a) The death-rate
is very much higher in Britain than Australia, which
means less profit. ( b ) The interest-rate is lower in Britain
than in Australia, which means less profit, (c) No British
ordinary life office earns such high interest or pays such
large bonuses as the A.M.P., so unless the A.M.P. people
are going to England to teach their grandmother how to
suck eggs—are going to show the great British life insti-
tutions that they don’t know their own country and their
own business —the A.M.P. must also earn less profits in
Britain, and thereby reduce the average profits all round.
And (3) the fact that the officers of the society are largely
in favor of the extension doesn’t necessarily prove that
it is good for the policy-holders. The officers have
interests quite apart from the policy-holders, and may
be unconsciously biassed. Expansion, even though it
may reduce the policy-holders’ profits, and so be bad for
them, is dead sure to be good for the officers. It means
more billets. The secretaryship in London will be a very
handsome prize for somebody. The London directorships
will be more handsome prizes. London is a pleasant
place, and it is the great life assurance centre of the
world, so the men who go there in a subordinate capacity
have chances of transplanting themselves into better
positions in great English offices. There is no lack of in-
ducement for the officers of the A.M.P. to believe in the
expansion policy.
It is at least to be hoped that the vote this time
will be not influenced by office interference. In the
matter of the proxies, branch managers, by instructions
from head-office, hustled round for proxies which they
gathered in freely, often from people who hadn’t time
to think, or hadn’t heard the merits of the case, or didn’t
really know how their proxies were to be used.
The men that the policy-holders pay, and the machinery
the policy-holders’ money provides, were employed to
rush the policy-holders into deciding as their servants —
the board—wanted them to decide. And the policy-holders
who are against the extension object to any part of their
premiums being used in promoting the extension policy.
They don’t ask that the cost of their own anti-extension
propaganda shall be paid out of the premiums, and they
want the management to fight fairly.

The success of the flotation of the 60,000 fully
paid-up shares in the Western Consolidated Silver Mines,
Zeehan, Tasmania, is now practically assured. Shares
have been applied for largely in all the Australian colonies
and New Zealand. The list will shortly close. The direc-
tors have secured the services of Mr. P. G. Morris as their
mine manager. Mr. Morris has been principal under-
ground manager of the Western Silver Mine for seven
vears, and this gives him a thorough knowledge of the
Zeehan lodes, and as he has been engaged in them on the
very boundary of the Western Consolidated co’s property,
it should be of special value to this co.*

* * * *
The Austral Otis Engineering Co. have just
completed a concentrating test on the Wilfley Table in-
stalled at their South Melbourne works of a parcel of
crushed ore representing the second product from the
concentrating Mill at the Conrad Mine, owned by Mr. J.
Howell, which hitherto has all been stacked on the mine
waiting the advent of a machine that would profitably
treat it without re-crushing. The result of the Wilfley
test is a high-grade concentrate, and tailing waste show-
ing very little values, and the Otis Co. have despatched
the tables for erection on the mine.*

The quantity of sulphides now treated per week
at the “ Barrier” compares favorably with the output of
any silver-lead mining field in the world. The difficulty
in treatment of sulphides is being successfully grappled
with mainly owing to improved systems of mechanical
“dressing.” In this the Luhrig Vanner plays a very
prominent part. A large number of these machines are
now in use there, and fresh orders are still being given to
the agents, Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, Melbourne. The
South Mine people are remodelling their mill on up-to-
date lines, and will install twelve or more Luhrig Vanners
of the 1900 pattern. The British Co. is also erecting
more of these superior machines.*

14 months, and the results are :
Income.

Deficit on 14 months’ working, £4021. The Standard Life
Association represents things somewhat differently by
charging only £7998 of -the expenses to this year and call-
ing the other £5OOO an asset under the name of “ New
Easiness Extension Account,” it being understood, of
course, that this will be gradually written off in future
years. Still, the above is the exact situation. The co.
started with £12,500 paid-up capital, of which £8479 is
still apparently intact.
Whether the results are bad or moderately good it
is difficult to say owing to the form in which the accounts
are presented. The Standard Life Association does both
ordinary life insurance and the penny and twopence and
threepence a week industrial business. A new office,
doing ordinary life business only, finds a great part of the
first year’s receipts swallowed up in expenses, for it is new
life business that costs (renewal business is comparatively
cheap) and its business is all new. (The A.M.P. in its first
year received £268, and expenses absorbed £173 of this.)
New industrial business is still more costly ; it is so costly
that more than the first year's premium on a new indus-
trial policy usually goes in expenses incurred in persuad-
ing the insured to" take the policy. If the first 14 months’
business of the Standard Life Association consists
mostly of ordinary life business then the expense rate
is a great deal heavier than it ought to be. If it consists
very largely of industrial policies then the expense rate
may be really much lighter than is usually the case in the
first year of a company which goes in for that thriftless
and unsatisfactory line of business whereby everybody
who pays Is. a week gets about fid. worth of value in the
shape of insurance while the other 6d. or thereabouts
goes in expenses. The Standard Life Association doesn’t
say how much of its premiums come from the ordinary
life business and how much from industrial insurance.
So no estimate can be formed as to the economy or other-
wise of the management. If it proposes to keep on
lumping the two together no estimate can ever be formed.
The best thing it can do is to re-issue its balance-sheet
with the accounts of the two branches distinct, and to
keep them distinct in all future balance-sheets. The
Standard Life Association has done a remarkably large
business for an office in its first year, and its investments
so far are mostly in safe Government securities.
The industrial assurance business is always
more or less amusing, though the Standard certainly
treats its insurers quite as well as the average. For in-
stance, under on£ of its endowment tables a person, by
paying Is. per week, can get from the Standard Associa-
tion at the end of eight years, if he is then alive, £2l—
is his money back and 4s. interest. (The same
amount, put in the Barrack-street savings-bank at 3|- per
cent., would yield about £23 10s. in the same time). If
the insured gets tired and drops his payments after (say)
3 years and 9 months (there is no surrender value until
half the period of the insurance is expired) the £9 15s. he
has already paid is absolutely forfeited. (The Barrack-
street Savings Bank—G. Dibbs, manager—would give him
back his £9 15s. and interest as well.) If he dies within
the eight years the co. doesn’t pay the full amount of the
policy ; it hands back the premiums paid without in-
terest. (The Barrack-street Savings Bank hands them
back with interest). So this remarkable table doesn’t
give any life insurance ; it is merely a system under
which the co. takes care of its customer’s savings at very
much less interest than the Savings Bank pays, and for-
feits them if he stops saving before half the term agreed
upon is expired, which the Savings Bank doesn’t. The
industrial insurance business is full of hilarious jokes.

The accounts of the Union Bank of. Australia,
for the six months ended 28th February, 1900, show a
most remarkable improvement, and this is by far the best
return this institution has shown for at least 8J years.
Comparative figures are as follows

The Union Bank has thus a surplus of £73,708 in the last
8J years. Of this £20,000 has already been written off.
It is now proposed to add £50,000 to reserve fund, raising
it from £750,000 to £BOO,OOO, and at the same time £50,000
appears to be written off the Contingency fund of £250,000
(created in 1896 by transferring that amount from reserve)-.
This £250,009 was set aside to cover losses then existing,
or anticipated, so by writing off £50,000 the bank does
nothing but formally wipe out certain losses already
known and acknowledged. The reserves at 31st. August
1891 appeared as £1,016,156 (reserve fund £1,000,000 ;
undivided profit, 16,156). They now appear, after writing
off £70,000, as £1,019, 861 (reserve fund £BOO,OOO ; Con-
tingency fund, £200,003 ; undivided profit £19,864).
The Union Bank’s half-year’s profits are equiva-
lent to £7 7s. 2d. per cent per annum on the shareholders’
capital and reserve funds. Its stock of cash and liquid
securities is equal to £5,069,246. And a notable matter is
that the Board has decided hereafter to keep the whole
reserve fund of £BOO,OOO separately invested in British
Government securities. So that reserve fund, at all
events, will have a definite existence and location if ever
it chances to be wanted.

Melbourne Hydraulic Power Co. presented a
rather cheering annual report for the year which closed
on 30th June, 1900. Going back as far as 1893 this in-
stitution shows the following results :
To

The year 1896, when the profits dropped so suddenly, was
the one when the co. was struck by a heavy verdict over
the lamentable squashing of a popular lady singer in a
hydraulic jift. Apart from this disaster the Melbourne
Hydraulic 00. has been a very steady-going and pro-
gressive institution. It has a paid-up capital of ±.90,000.
There is no general reserve fund, but there is a fund of
£20,065 to cover depreciation and renewals, and as the
entire amount sunk in the co’s undertaking is £106,550,
this is equal to about 20 per cent. Also, the co. has
£579 undivided profit.

The Land Mortgage Bank of Victoria continues
its career of misfortune. At 30th June, 1894, it was still
making a profit on paper, and it had reserve funds,
strictly on paper, of £118,161. About that date it effected
an arrangement with its £900,000 worth of debenture-
holders which fixed the interest at 4£ per cent. Since
then things have gone thus :

June, 1900 .. .. 3,244 5,562 8,806
Many other things have happened to the Land Mortgage
Bank which it is impossible to fully trace by reason of
the reticent character of some of its statements. It has
written-off amounts (not always specified) for irrecover-
able interest, expenses of converting debentures, &c. It
has called up some fresh capital with difficulty, and
applied it to buying up debentures at a discount, thereby
making a profit (not specified) which was sometimes
added to the balance (not specified) at credit of contin-
gency fund, while at June, 1899, the unspecified profit
made by the repurchase of debentures was set against the
unspecified loss by sale of property below book values, so
that both were unspecified, and remain in that state even
unto this day. But the net result is that where the Land
Mortgage Bank had £118,161 of reserves (reserve fund,
£105,000, and £13,161 undivided profit) at June, 1894, it
has now no reserve and an accumulated loss of £60,560.
The present situation is about this. The Land
Mortgage Bank, despite a slight diminution in the gap
between income and outgoings, is only earning enough to
pay 2,j per cent, interest on its debentures, ft is paying
3J per cent., and it obtained that concession with the
utmost difficulty. The concession, too, is only temporary ;
in another 18 months the rate reverts to 41 per cent.
Income being insufficient to meet outgoings, the concern
necessarily keeps selling its assets piecemeal in a bad
market for whatever they will fetch, and making more
and more losses, which might possibly be avoided if it
could pay its way out of revenue and nurse its assets. It
is living on its own tail, in fact. Paid-up capital is
£412,677, of which£6l,s6o is obviously lost, lea ving£352,117
nominally intact. Liabilities to debenture holders are
about £711,000. The invested assets yield only £2 2s. 9d.
per cent, gross on their nominal value, and assuming that
they yield 4 percent, gross on their real value, then said real
value is so low that the paid-up capital is all.lost, and there
isn’t enough, by at least £150,000, to pay the debenture-
holders. But there is about £700,000 of callable capital,
and with that asset in view the debenture-holders want
their lib. of flesh. If there was no callable capital they
would probably agree to reduce interest to 2J per cent.,
at which rate the Land Mortgage Bank might pull
through and come right after many years or thereabouts.
A prompt calling up of more capital to pay off the deben-
tures might also save the institution, but the small calls
already made were collected with great difficulty and not
very much success. Moreover, the uncalled capital is all
mortgaged to the debenture-holders, who have to be con-
sulted before anything can be done. And they are in
England, much scattered, difficult to get at, and appar-
ently not in a good temper.

The boom of the National Bank of Maoriland
still continues. At March, 1891, this concern had no
reserve-fund, not even an undivided profit-balance. It
had had a painful cleaning-up and was starting afresh,
after a great writing-off of capital and reserves. Since
then it has progressed thus
Carried )
Profits. to ' Dividends.
Reserves.
March, 1892.. £19,335 5 per cent. = £12.500
~ 1893.. 16,344 6 „ = 12,500
„ 1894 .. 16,997 5 „ 12,500
„ 1895 .. 16,431 5 12,500
„ 1896 .. 18,090 £20,000 5 „ 12,500
„ 1897.. 21,461 10,000 5 „ 12,500
„ 1898 .. 25,538 10,000 6 „ 15,000
„ 1899 .. 40,966 20,000 7 „ 17,500

£217,509 £BO,OOO £127,500
The National Bank has thus accumulated in nine years a
new reserve fund of £BO,OOO, and the other £10,009, less
£2500 set aside as the nucleus of an officers’ pension-fund,
leaves £7509 undivided profit. The profits are at the very
remarkable rate of £l3 13s. 2d. per cent, per annum on
the shareholders’ capital and reserve fund. For some
reason or other the two Maoriland banks, doing business
in a country where capital is supposed to have been
“ driven out ” over and over again by socialistic legislation,
leave the Australian institutions hopelessly behind at
present in the matter of earnings. A good part of the
National Bank’s gaudy prosperity is doubtless owing to
the fact that it does a very large business on a very small
capital. The entire shareholders’ funds are now £337,509,
and the deposits and other liabilities to the public are
over £3,200,000. The National Bank’s reserve of cash and
State securities is £717,506, equal to a little over 22 per
cent, of all liabilities to the public. This is reasonably
strong, but no more. The policy of keeping the cash
reserves as low as can be done with anything like safety
is doubtless another thing which helps to keep the
earnings high. It is always just possible that some time
of panic might prove them a shade too low. Still the
National Bank of Maoriland seems to be prospering in a
most satisfactory fashion, for the present at all events.

Broken Hill Water Supply Co. closed its best half-
year up to date at 30tli June, 1900. Profits were £19,557,
and £27,319 brought forward from 1899 made £46,876
available. A dividend at the rate of 12J per cent, per
annum absorbed £11,656; an addition of £lO,OOO was
made to reserve fund, and £25,220 was carried forward.
The Broken Hill Water Supply 00. has paid regular
dividends since 1894, and since the beginning of 1897 they
have varied between 10 and 15 per cent. It has now
nominal reserves of £45,220 (reserve fund, £20,000, and
undivided profit, £25,220). Yet its situation is hardly
good enough to justify the dividends it is paying, and its
reserve fund is largely, if not wholly, nominal. Among
its assets is an item of goodwill or preliminary expenses
described as “ Water rights, leases, Parliamentary Bills,
&c., at cost, £25,193.” This is a purely fictitious and
irrecoverable item which should be entirely written off.
Then there is a very big item “Construction account,
filtration plant, freehold land and dwelling house, at cost
£160,883.” In all the years the co. has been working
there has been apparently no allowance made for depre-
ciation of this huge asset, yet the depreciation must be
considerable. Plant isn’t immortal, by any means.
Probably it would be a fair and reasonable thing if the
balance-sheet of the Broken Hill Water Co. was made up
like this :
Liabilities.

It would, at least, be much more business-like to write
the illusory item of “Rights, Leases and Parliamentary
Bill ” off the reserve fund, even if no allowance is made
for depreciation. This would reduce the nominal reserves
to about £20,000.
* ■ * » *
By the way, a remarkable paragraph in thereport
of the Broken Hill Water Supply 00. reads : “ It is the
intention of the directors to shortly issue the 13,500 shares
held in reserve by inviting tenders for same, the proceeds
from which will be placed to the credit of Reserve Fund.”
If the proceeds are put to reserve then there will naturally
be no dividend on these shares, and they will be quite
unsaleable, and the money paid for them will simply be a
present made to the other shareholders. In fact, they
won’t be shares at all. Perhaps the board means that the
premiums obtained on the new issue will be put to reserve
fund, which is intelligible enough. But that is certainly
not what it says.

Junction North'made a gross profit of £11,858
for the 12 months, but the directors write-off £7699 for
wear and tear of plant; the net profit is thus £4159. To
make this trifle 42,360 tons of ore have been taken out of
the mine and treated. This ore contained about
564,6000 z. silver and 7836 tons lead. The product, how-
ever, was 7065 tons concentrates worth 29'8 oz. silver and
61 - 8 per cent, lead = 210,5330 z. silver and 4366 tons lead.
That is, 210,5330 z. silver was recovered in the concen-
trates, and 354,0670 z. was for the present lost; 4366 tons
lead was saved, and 3470 tons wasn’t. Nor is the metal in
the concentrates all saved ; as there is an average of nearly
7 per cent, zinc in it, a considerable quantity of both
silver and lead must be lost in the smelting. A number
of improvements in the mill are promised ; and clearly
the process can stand improvement. Yet it is not the
fault of the machinery so much as of the ore, which is
singularly refractor} 7 on account of garnet sandstone and
rhodonite. On the other hand, there is a recent improve-
ment in prospects underground, though, on account of
the position of the workings, it will not be possible to
extract the ore in large quantities economically. At the
end of the co.’s year (July 31), it had surplus liquid assets
over liabilities of £6763.
• • * *
Great Cobar Copper Co. : Royalty received for
half-year, over £29,000, of which i 24,000 has been paid in
divs. The tributors have now an option to purchase the
mine for £500,000, which may be exercised any time
before 15th June, 1902. If the sale is completed before
the end of the tribute the co. will pay the tributors com-
pensation for the surrender, to be fixed if necessary by
arbitration, but not to exceed a sum calculated at the
rate of £90,000 per annum for the unexpired term. It
now remains for the tributors to say how long they will
wait before going into possession as absolute owners
of the mine. Understood they now own over
three-fourths of it, so it is only a question
of paying for the remaining fourth. Nothing is said
as to position of the new tribute. Probably the
compensation the new tributors are to receive is set out
in the agreement. It was only a farce, anyhow—a move
by some directors to checkmate tributors gaining posses-
sion by purchase of shares. The sooner the mine is taken
over the better ’twill be for the other mines in the dis-
trict. At present the heavy royalty of 50 per cent, when
copper is over £5O per ton is a bar against local smelting
which would be removed by the purchase. Instead of
water-concentrating silicious copper ores at the Cobar-
Chesney mine, it would be an easy smelting proposition
to blend them with the ores containing an excess of iron.
The Chesney concentrates are being carted to the railway
station and trained to Lithgow. The tributors have
started a part of their electrolytic refining works at
Lithgow, which promise to be a great success. These
works will stop robbery on the part of smelting-houses in
London, and will only need the addition of a copper-wire
and plating plant to establish another great industry in
N.S.VV. The large amount of copper -wire used annually
in Australasia should be manufactured in the country
where the copper is produced, instead of the metal being
shipped to England and brought back again. A duty
under Federation would help the industry along.

Grassy Gully, N.S.W., is a newspaper-boomed
concern. Crushing starts in a week or two. Said to have
1500 tons good-grade ore at grass. Is this going to be
another N.S.W. disappointment? Writer sincerely-hopes
not, although, as a rule, the big-talked mine has little
results.
* * * *
New Eldorado, N.S.W. This concern has a
ridiculous reconstruction scheme so common to Cobar
management. Proposed to give four shares at ss. each
for every £1 share now issued, and to create 36,000 con-
tributing shares of ss. each, payable 2s. on application and
allotment, the balance in calls. These are to be offered
to shareholders—who aren’t likely to take them when
they can buy on the market original shares at less than
half the price. Eldorados are selling at 6s. and 7s.
One of these will mean four ss. shares, and is
it likely the others are going to be taken with
a liability of ss. each ? The proposition is absurd,
and the face that the directors mostly live at
Cobar is the on \ possible excuse for making it. The Co.
has an overdraft if £3BO and owes sundry creditors £333.
The mine is more- - ban an excellent prospect; four-fifths
of the Mt. Drys !e gold came from within 100 ft. of its
boundary. It ha 3 also a large surface formation which
could be'worked at a good profit in a big way, and it is
now working good gold from the Drysdale 275 ft. level. It
is a mine that deserves better management than that
from which this asinine reconstruction scheme emanates.
• * • *
Mount Drysdale should amalgamate with the
Eldorado, and now seems to be the time to fix an equitable
basis. Its principal gold is lying very near the boundary,
and anyone seeing the mines must immediately conclude
that they should be worked together and not apart. It is
better equipped than the Eldorado, which is not saying
much, for it wants a main shaft and a new winding ana
compressing plant. It has the most puzzling formation
that writer has seen ; but, like the Gt. Boulder of Wes-
tralia, the gold is becoming more concentrated with
depth. In the upper levels, when the rich shute disap-
peared, the mill was often kept going on 10 and 15dwt.
ore. In the bottom level the vein has become compara-
tively well defined, while the formation around it is of
little or no value. Lots of gold has been left in the upper
levels. The mine is well managed.

Still another effort to work the reefs at Mt.
Browne goldfield, in the N.W. corner of N.S.W., has
petered out. Equity Judge Walker has appointed an
official liquidator of the Waratta co. Every co. that has
a look-in at this field makes the same fatal blunder—l-
starts with insufficient capital considering the expensive
ness of everything but sand and sorrow up that way.

Mt. Garnet Freehold (N.Q.) Smelting expected
to start at the end of this month. 12,500 shares placed
in London at 475. 6d., and the purchaser has the option
till November on the further parcel of 12,500 at £3 a share.
Estimate of already-available -ore of all classes, 209,000
tons. The metallurgist says: “The hope that in the
course of opening up the mine, large bodies of silicious
material carrying payable quantities of silver or
copper will be found, which would render
the addition of barren flux to the furnace charge
unnecessary, has, so far, not been realised, and
it is consequently evident that such addition will at the
commencement of smelting operations have to he made.
The present high cost of carriage also precludes the use of
coke for smelting, and a contract has been let to supply
2000 tons of charcoal. Directors’ fees and other expenses
in Melbourne, £1215. London-office expenditure, £282.
Over £16,000 already spent on the smelting-plant, and
total expenditure to date has exceeded £BO,OOO. Once
more Tub Bulletin desires to remark that it takes a heap
of money to run a copper-mine.
Lyell Tharsis (Tas.) : Has been informed by the
Mt. Lyell Co. that: “In view of the purchase of the
South'Tharsis property the Directors do not wish for the
present to pursue negotiations for acquiring the Lyell
Tharsis mine.”
* k * *
Mt. Lyell.—The market in this stock has
anticipated that there will be no div. this quarter on
account of the purchase of the South Tharsis, and other
mine purchases which the 00. has probably in view ;
hence shares have gone back to where they were before
the purchase was made. If they were worth- £7 10s.
without the South Tharsis they certainly should be worth
considerably more with it. There is a marked difference
between using barren flux and silicious ore containing
from 2 to 3 per cent copper, the effect of which should be
seen in the Mt. Lyell returns as soon as the South Tharsis
body is operated on. Writer would not be astonished to
see Mt. Lyell shares goto £lO before the end of the year
if copper keeps over £7O.

In its eagerness to supply news re the
Breelong blacks, Sydney E. News observes:
Governors were seen at Moonbi, near
Mullaley Well, Moonbi is only 80 miles from
M ullaley by shortest route, with Gunnedah, Car-
roll, Somerton and Tamworth in between. Geo-
graphy has no chance against Hysteria! Also,
Gunnedah is “in a state of frenzy,” ditto Bog-
gabri, Baan Baa, Mullaley, Coonabarabran, Mud-
gee, &c., and around _ Singleton (?) “all persons
carry arms.” And this in the province that sup-
plied. J. Bull with so"many “last hopes !!” In
V ellmgton district all the settlers lock or barri-
cade their doors every night, and sleep with an
axe or a gun, or both. Sheer frenzy, or rank
cowardice ?
* * * *
According to Sydney Evening News :
A tracker was following the tracks of the Governors.
Mr. D. Murphy heard loud talk and laughing, and called
to the tracker to stop, saying “ Here is some other party
coming.” Two horsemen appeared immediately on the
left hank of the creek, and when they got opposite Mr.
Murphy and party, who were about 80 yards distant, Mr.
Murphy called out, “Hallo, there is Jacky Governor 1”
The man riding a chestnut turned round, answering,
“Hallo there, what do you want?” Constable
called out, “That’s them —at them, boys.” The party
galloped through a deep sandy creek, about 60 yards
wide, but when they reached the opposite side they could
only see dust flying in Coonabarabran district.
Now, accepting “Mr. Dan Murphy’s” statement
as correct, it boils down thus: When four con-
stables, besides Mr. Murphy and a black tracker,
were all but ridden into by the fugitive Governors,
they exchanged remarks— not shots —and the
blacks escaped. At only 80yds. distance not a
cartridge was burned ! —and after the party had
gone 60yds. all they could see was dust. Why,
Carbine himself could not have gained more than
20yds. in the distance ; and, in any case, what
could the pursuers have wanted better than the
dust to follow ? Either that party should have
had the Governors, or the Governors had them.
Both parties mounted and armed—midday—Boyds.
apart, and six men to two 1 Monstrous !
* * * *
The pleasing news has just arrived that
that alleged offer of splendid wages for 3000
miners and other workmen to go to S’Africa was
al l a joke or a fraud. Somebody, it is explained,
asked one Rendle if lie thought 3000 men could be
got in Australia at £1 a day and a 12 months’
agreement, just as he might have asked whether
3000 men would take Rothschild’s billet if it was
vacant. So Rendle wrote inquiring—just as he
might have asked if they would all like to have
1000 shares in the Bank of N.S.W. or a pension of
£5OO a year. And they, not understanding this
kind of joke, took it for a serious offer. Rendle,
it now seems, doesn’t really know in any personal
sense if there is work for one Australian miner at
even 3s. 4d. a day; he was merely put-
ting a question. This way of putting a question
“oka peculiar. The natural inference is that
the Rand mine-owners and others want
to see the country rushed so that they can
get abundance of men at a starvation wage,
through sheer pressure of competition. The off-
hand inquiry if 3000 men can be got to take a 12
months’ job at £1 a day has already done some-
thing to start a movement Africa-wards which
will help to make labor cheap, yet the mine-
owners are committed to absolutely nothing. Per-
haps the shortest way to counteract this move
now would be for some unauthorised person to
send a letter to Capetown asking if there are 6000
men there who would like to come to Australia,
and get 640 acres of good land apiece free, and
£5OO each in cash to start farming with. That
also would commit nobody to anything.
* * * *
It looks as though the kindness of the
Lyne Govt, in supplying cheap reporters to the two
Sydney thick dailies may be thrown away after
all. Pekin has been taken, and the grandest thrill
of the expedition is apparently over. The great
opportunity for fine, hair-raising descriptive writ-
ing_ was in those dark days at Tientsin, when
Chinese artillery out-ranged and out-classed the
guns of the Europeans and Japs. The weeks of
fighting had filled all the swamps around the city
with unburied corpses, and the river washed them
up in hundreds, and the dogs tore and ate them in
the face of outraged humanity; A city with a
larger population than that of Sydney was on fire
in many places, and the Chinese slowly closed
in and mutilated the dead and tortured the
wounded. Things grew so bad at one time that
the Russian commander proposed that while 400
of his troops worked the guns and fought to a
finish in the crumbling Concession to cover the re-
treat, all the rest of the Allies, military and civilian,
should form a square and, with the women and
children in the centre, try to sneak or cut their
way through to Taku. It was to be a case of
deliberately sacrificing the 400 Muscovites to save
the rest, and if the idea had been adopted they
would have gone down in a struggle fit to
blaze through history beside that of Thermopylae.
But a young Britisher, Jimmy Watts, and three
Russians, whose names mostly ended in -ski,
dashed out of Tienstin on horseback, galloped
through the Chinese lines under heavy fire, raced
through whole villages where every man did
his best to pull them down, carried the news to
Taku in time, and the reinforcements fought their
way to Tienstin and saved the allied army. And
the local reporters got a close monopoly of it all.
* * * *
N.S.W. Public Service was wrought up
last week over the elections for next year’s coun-
cil. Most of the contests were keen, two seats
going on the casting vote of the returning officer,
while two candidates lost by three votes only.
The best known of the men elected are Tom
Garrett, of the Justice Department, the old-time
’Varsity cricketer, and J. W. Turner, of Fort-
street School fame. The general division fought
the clerical and professional divisions successfully
in _ two of the departments, three letter-sorters
being returned for the P. 0., while two of their
number, one the head-messenger, were successful
in the Chief Secretary’s Department.

Editor Bulletin. —N.S.W. National Gal-
lery trustees (including Messrs. Lister and Col-
lingridge, two members of the committee of the

* * * A
There’s a note of true pathos in thosj
cablegrams of sympathy which Fitz Gibbon, the
Melb. Board of Works man, has lately been send-
ing to Her Gracious Majesty “on his own’’and
also on the alleged behalf of Melb. ratepayers.
Fitz is growing old, and the long-expected knight-
hood must come now or never. With Federation
close at hand he is making desperate bids for the
“ coveted distinction.” The sadness of his anxiety
lies in the fact that Her Gracious’s secretary for
loyal messages, or whatever name the gentleman
is known by, seems to have got Fitz’s measure.
The replies up to now have not been encouraging,
and the Melb. betting against a knighthood for
the poor old pleader is about 3to 1. As much as
4 to 1 has been laid at the Victorian Club.

with the gum on the
current N.S.W. postage-
stamps? Is the trouble
bad gum or not enough
gum ?
In his story of “ Mafeking after the
Siege,” Capt. Rev. J. H. G. Auld says—“ Why,
500 Australian Bushmen would have taken the place
in an afternoon.” Modest—soo Australians too
good for Baden-Powell and his 800 prize Britishers !
Mr. Auld also relates, with no apparent disap-
proval, that a Mafeking resident was making out
of shell-cases, “spittoons, with Kruger-coins in-
serted in the concave; the idea being that of
spitting on Kruger’s face.” How truly noble !
and Christian and clean and gentlemanly and
large-minded !
* * * *
The N.S. W. Artillery Band, as much State
property as is the Supreme Court Bench, is com-
pulsorily driven to tootle gratis at private
“society” functions. The man who refuses is
kicked out. To begin the killing of this scanda-
lous abuse, Member X should call for a return of
the times the band been requisitioned “by
authority ” this last 12 months, with the names of
the “hosts ” at whose functions it.has served, and
the gratuities, if any, tendered. Following that
should come a resolution of the House that the
band should be precluded from tootling at private
entertainments, or that it should only be allowed
to tootle at the current rates charged by private
bands. Cheap “ Society ” could then take its
choice.

* * • *
The discussion on aboriginal names for
N.S.W. Federal constituencies has induced inquiry
as to the meaning of several native-named country
towns —and with disastrous results. Cootamundra,
for instance —the beloved Coota. of the residents —
is found to have such an abominable significance
that public mention of it would entail a month’s
gaol.

Be the prematurely abated Sydney pla gue-
scare—what of the dilapidated and germ-infected
Chinese dens at Botany, and elsewhere (built
mostly of kerosene tins, scraps of galvanised
iron, dirt-boxes, and old bags) ? Also, bone and
refuse-gathering carts are allowed to pervade
George-street till nearly lupch time daily.
If England wants to end the Indian
famine she can do it easily enough, as far as mere
money is concerned. But her people don’t really
worry about the matter —a recent proposal to
grant five millions sterling to the famine fund and
chance its ultimate return was simply not listened
to by the Commons. Meanwhile, a gilded Aus-
tralian, who is prostrate on his stomach in search
of social distinction, spent £2OOO the other night in
London in roses to decorate the marble stairs of
his mansion.
* * * *
The unfortunate consumptives in Liverpool
(N.S.W.) Benevolent Asylum have petitioned the
authorities to be allowed occasionally to take a
stroll in the adjacent bush, instead of being, as at
present, cooped-up all the time in a restricted
area within nigh walls. What a grim satire it is
upon the boasted hygienic progress of the time
that sufferers from a disease requiring, perhaps,
more than any other, plenty of fresh air should be
practically imprisoned so as not only to have less
chance of individual improvement but also to
vitiate the common stock of oxygen, and thus
banefully re-act upon each other! A “ forcing-
house ” for phthisis !
BOHEMIANS.
(Written for the Illingworth Bohemian Night.)
Good friends ! By kind request I write
To welcome you, in gentle phrase,
To this, our friend’s, Bohemian Night—
Ye know not of Bohemian days.
These brave Bohemians, heart in hand,
March on their way with spirits free ;
They count not moments, sand by sand,
But spill the hour-glass royally.
And o’er the wall their caps they fling,
And with their caps their hearts withal ;
They paint and carve, and dance and sing—
Lured by a Vision Magical.
They follow, as the Monk the Bird
Did follow through the forest green,
The Song no other ears have heard,
The Sight no other eyes have seen.
In days of dearth, on paths of mire,
Through gleams of hope, and hopeless glooms,
They seek the phantom Flower of Fire
That by the door of Heaven blooms.
For ev’ry man his weird must dree
And they must theirs—and dare the Fates ;
While hunts the lean hound Poverty
The gaunt wolf Hunger through their gates.
Each one is born to play his part,
And each must play as best he can ;
These make the blood leap in the heart,
These keep the soul alive in man.
With wine, and jest, and laughter long
Their lives appear to pass, maybe—
But still beneath the river’s song
There sounds the sobbing of the sea.
VICTOR J. DALLT.

THE DEVIL SENDS COOKS.
The Judge’s head is crammed with lore,
His brain of knowledge is a store,
But oft in vain, for when he sums
His judgment from his stomach comes,
And baited sinners brought to book
Know why the devil sends the cook!
The Mayor of Forbes, N.S.W., was re-
cently requested by a number of residents to con-
vene a public meeting “ for the purpose of con-
sidering the increase of crime in our district,
and the measures to be taken for repression
thereof.” But a lot of the people*who didn’t sign
the requisition are saying not only that the move-
ment casts an entirely undeserved stigma upon
the district, but that some of the 15 signatories
themselves can’t afford to heave any great amount
of rocks.

Westralian aborigines call Sunday, “the
whiteman’s big sleep.” A local Anglican Bishop
is the authority for this and if the blacks invented
the figure it is the nearest thing to respectable
thought that has been cited in their favor.

Knight Snowden, ex-Mayor of Melbourne,
is much troubled about the “ Supreme Court
Amendment Act ” now before Vic. Parliament.
Lawyers, he says, as a rule, are straighter than
the people who come to them, and it is not the
client, but the poor, much-suffering lawyer, who
needs protection. Which recalls the story of the
poor swagman, who explained to the indignant
B< c'i^iv er ky w hom he had been caught in the act
of kdlmg a sheep : “ Look here, I done it in self-
defence. I’ll allow no man’s sheep to bite me.”

The crew of the s.s. Perth
from G-eraldton to Fremantle,
W.A., describe a fight they saw
between a sea-serpent and a
whale. In these kodak days
there is no earthly reason why
anybody who tells such yarns
should be listened to without
corroborative snapshot evidence.
Why not make every captain
pass an examination in photo-
graphy and carry a camera to
photograph sea-serpents and
things, the Museum authorities
agreeing to buy good sea-ser-
pent photos, at a decent
price ?

When a certain church re-
cently advertised for an organist
the numerous applicants were
weeded-out to six, who had each
to play for four Sundays on
trial —so that when at the end
of six months one was appointed
the “screw” for thit period
had been saved ! Yet, from the
pulpit of that church have
been heard sermons against
sweating !
The indecent small-female mania took a
fresh turn in Melb. last week, when a _ woman
charged a middle-aged man with committing a
capital offence upon her infant daughter, aged 16
months ! After hearing her evidence the jury
stopped the case, completely exonerating accused.
Judge Holroyd expressed satisfaction with this
step, and said that had the case proceeded further
he would have been compelled to order an inquiry
into the woman’s mental condition. But lie alto-
gether omitted to order detention for examination,
or her prosecution for perjury—she was a Crown
witness. Also there was no suggestion that the
Crown should pay accused’s expenses and costs,
incurred in defending himself from a charge
which a Crown Defender, backed up, as he should
be, by the police, would have shown to be purely
fictitious at the police-court hearing.
The other day, at Prisoners’ Aid Society
meeting (Yic.), Burrowes, P.M., late Governor of
Melb. Gaol, remarked that once a man enters the
prison door his career as a respectable citizen is
forever closed. Same day Acting-Judge John-
stone sent a young girl (first offender) to prison,
with the observation that three months’ gaol
would probably prove her salvation. And so
experiment in social science trips airily along.

The Narandera banquet to N.S.W. Works
Minister O’Sullivan cost £lO2. The tickets at 7s.
6d. totalled £44. Now there is a local row to
raise the difference.
* * ♦ *
The York Hotel, Adelaide—for years the
fashionable local hostelry—is in danger of extinc-
tion. A Melbourne drapery firm has bought the
site for £38,000, and, it is said, thinks of building
a big warehouse on it. A brewing firm holds a
lease of the York, which has nine years to run,
but as the establishment has not been over-profit-
able of late it may be glad to cry off for a reason-
able sum. There are many hotels in Adelaide
which might be wiped out with advantage, but the
passing of the old York will cause some regret.
* « * *
Writing re an Anglican recently
held in Sydney, S.M. Herald suppressed the
Christian and surnames of all the prelates present,
giving them instead their sectarian titular dis-
tinctions. Thus, the Bishop of Perth, the Bishop
of North Queensland, the Bishop of Tasmania,
and so on. Now, there is no such person as the
Bishop of Perth ; there is no such person as the
Bishop of Bathurst; there is no such person as the
Bishop of anywhere in Australia. There may be
a Bishop, but not the Bishop, and never will there
be.
* • » *
“F.H.” to The Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin —Kurikka and the Finns in Queensland,
are known to the writer as a sturdy, muscular crowd of
tireless workers. Yet for their hard labor and pleasant
manners they receive from the local “poler”—who has
his Is. an hour secure but little encouragement.
Kurikka is a man of education, apart from his knowledge
of English, and is strong-minded and enterprising. In
the navvies’ camp, 300 yards from this shanty, there are
a few Finns, one of whom has with him his wife and two
children (boy and girl), and the noble navvy—anxious
to instruct the children in the language—has begun by
teaching them to swear. I yesterday found the two
youngsters squatting upon a pile of horse-feed, and,
in many bad English words, black-guarding the stable-
boy. Most of the Finns are head and shoulders above the
navvies in physique and appearance, and can give them
points in any direction.
A somewhat historic estate
is being cut up for sale at North
Sydney. Brisbane House, the
old home of the Milson family, is
perhaps the most ancient build-
ing now on the Shore ; it is of
solid masonry, and was entirely
built by convict labor. The
founder of the Milson family
came to N.S.W. in the suite of
one of the early Governors, and
the Milson estate was a grant of
the old days. The headlands by
Lavender Bay are named after
old colonists to whom the adjoin-
ing lands were given, Milson’s
Point and Blue’s Point.
* *
Boiled down, the experi-
ences of one N.S.W. warrior
read as follows: —“Was an in-
cubus on the old folks through
inability to obtain employment.
Volunteered for Transvaal,
passed tests, shipped on trans-
port, trip just long enough to
teach officers what authoritative
swells th ey were. Disembarked,
sent to the front, tucker limited
and indifferent, clothing scarce
and shoddy, endless patrollings,
perpetual drillings, cleanliness
unknown, mother earth for bed,
stars for blankets. Enemy lo-
cated in position, waiting;
frontal attack ordered, usual
repulse, wounded. Ten hours to
field hospital, lightning treat-
ment, cross-cut saw, extraction
of bullets, plenty bandages, ditto
iodoform. Eight days spring-
less cart, rough tracks, water
cronk, tucker rough and tough,
wound undressed, sleep un-
known. Base - hospital eighth
day, wound open, swollen, dis-
charging, spirits low, fever high.
Eight weeks in bed, two weeks
convalescent, invalided home,
still shaky. More incubus old
folks.”
• •
“Tangata” tells a sad
story of the Church of England
in Maoriland :
Have often wondered which sect takes the bun for
“ begging.” All over M.L. are to be seen palatial build-
ings belonging to the “Woman in Red ” and the “ Plaided
Lassie,” but this may only mean that collections and
bazaar-funds are applied to legitimate and reasonable
purposes and not to paying fat stipends to surpliced
magnates of the “Canon Wealthy” stamp. For down-
right poverty and meanness there is not the slightest
doubt the “ dough ” in M.L. goes to the Anglican Church.
For many years I warbled responses, anthems, and
things in a “ quite English” toney M.L. church, and had
a desperate struggle to get the few shillings I was en-
titled to weekly as paid lay-clerk, whilst the plate, which
regularly reached us after the congregation had allegedly
contributed, was stuck under our noses in full view
of the “ House ” whilst singing offertory sentences
such as “ The Lord loveth a cheerful giver,” “ Lay
not up,” &c. Result, a wild tussle with a surplice
to track the weekly 3d. to its lair, and much mental
blasphemy at an array of “tray-bits” already in the
plate, not enough, even, to pan-out my “ screw.” Things
got so bad that Dunedin’s aristocratic Bishop had to go
'Ome on a cadging-trip, despite which the local pro-
Cathedral, established about 30 years, is still gasping out
a mortgaged existence. A well-known and real-“ white ”
minister, who recently took charge of a city Anglican
church at a yearly stipend of £5O, found that he was ex-
pected to pay £52 per year for rent of the parsonage !
Nothing daunted, the good man mortgaged his life-
insurance policy to build a Sunday-school! The mort-
gage and the school still exist—and the church is doing
well. 8
* * * *
Certain proposals for the cleaning up and
purifying of small and smellful Hobart came before
Tas. Assembly t’other day, and it almost looked as
if something might be done. But before cleaning
Hobart it was necessary to admit that Hobart was
dirty, and the insinuation that it was dirty was
thrown back by the Hobart members as a gross
outrage. Therefore nothing happened.
* * » *
It will be interesting to see who’ll succeed
Gov. Gormanston in Tasmania. The Lewis Govt,
has brought in a bill to fix the salary at £3OOO. Of
course His Ex. will have quarters with light and
fuel, but £3OOO won’t go far when Tas. society has
to be fed. The effect of the reduced salary (to fit
in with Federation) will be to make Tasmania a
breaking-in place for Governors, like the Leeward
and Windward Islands. Before he left Tasmania
Lord Gormanston made it known that during the
seven years he held office he spent £6OO a Jiear
more than he got from the island, and it was lately
stated in the Assemby by Parspn Woolnough that
Gov. Robert Hamilton “ lost by his billet.” One
minister advised Private Secretary Rawlinson to
pply for the Tas. Governorship now that ho had
the work at his “fingers’ ends”; and, joking
apart, it’s such a man as Rawlinson that Rasp-
berry Land may expect when the screw is pulled
down, unless Tasmania strikes for a local man to
fill the billet—as it should do, and as Canada
does. “Big” men won’t play second fiddle to
the Gov. General. The Tas. Govt., however,
wants to compel the next Governor to spend some
money in the “ provinces.” £5OO is to be allowed
him for “travelling expenses” in Tasmania.

It was probably apropos of all the sickly
business which accompanied the departure from
Hobart of Gov. Gormanston that Fowler, member
for Launceston, when it was said ip the Assembly
that there ought to be a Govt. House in the
“Northern Capital,” declared that he’d be no
party to voting money for “the few people who
called themselves society and contributed little or
nothing to the revenue.”
* * * *
Despite the fact that Lieut.-Col. Burns
published his refusal to accept the resignations of
Messrs. See and Lyne, juniors, whom he had
appointed lieutenants in N.S.W. Lancers, the
young men wisely insisted on withdrawing.
Young See bravely entered the ranks of the regi-
ment again, Lyne leaving the whole concern in
great disgust. As Quartermaster-Sergt. M‘Mahon
receives a commission in the regiment, the ulti-
mate result is a triumph for the troopers. The
men as much resented their favorite non-com.
being passed over as they objected to two inex-
perienced boys, simply because they were the sons
of Ministers of the Crown, being placed over
them. While the fact of a body of troopers so
easily setting at nought the wishes of their
superiors may be subversive of discipline in volun-
teer ranks, the complete climb-down of so many
persons who are high and mighty in the land jus-
tifies the men in their action.

Battery had 6 guns, 80 men, and 30 horses, and according
to a statement by Gen. French commented on in The
Bulletin, the 30 horses, though less than one-fourth of
the proper complement, didn’t get work enough to keep
them in health. I saw some of the battery out when the

have had a whole “ division ” (not “ brigade ’’-—there are
no brigades of artillery) if a senior-major was present.
Trust them for knowing all about that !

In a Sydney hairdresser’s shop recently a
young man from the country, who was getting
shaved, was complimented by the barber upon his
fine set of teeth, and induced to let them undergo
a small scraping process, which he took to be, he
deposed, “an act of either charity or friendship.”
But when—to quote the song—
He handed them over a sovereign bright,
And they said there was not any change—
Although they assured him ’twas “ perfectly right”
He thought it remarkably strange !
Defendant produced a price-card, which he said
had been hanging in the shop at the time, arid
which bore amongst its items—“ Teeth, £1 Is”—
but the magistrate expressed his opinion of the
cheerful little transaction by promptly finine
defendant £5 —“or a month.”

A leading M.L. paper editorially prints
this:—
What could be more fitting than to make of Pekin itself
a monument, to wipe this infamous capital from among
the cities of the world, to leave nothing living in it nor
one stone upon another ?
As the paper in question is owned by two eminent
Methodists, whose chief recreation is local-preach-
ing, and edited by a leading Congregationalist, it
may fairly be taken as representing up-to-date
Christianity. The proposed slaughter of more
than a million Chinkies in one act shows that
there is nothing mean or retail about their rever-
ences’ ideas. As an example of Christian benefi-
cence, the article would be of great value to future
missionaries—
J. H. Gossip, of Wagga Express, is the
first country journalist to introduce the linotype
to the backblocks.
“ Eye for eye, and tooth for tooth,”
Ran the stern old Hebrew law ;
But the Christian’s milder creed
For a tooth exacts a jaw.
