A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, August 28, 1900.
My Dear Myee.—
A Supper that brought a lot of people to-
gether was Mr. J. C. Williamson’s Birthday Party,
at “Ithaca,” Elizabeth Bay, last Sunday night.
As far as I know, it isn’t a secret that J.C. has
just celebrated fifty-five. He doesn’t look it.
The supper was an informal, merry gathering.
Around the long tables sat —well, let me think.
It is hard to know where to begin. The hostess
led off a long array of black frocking. Billie Barlow
—Mrs. Stuart —was there next to Wallace Brown-
low, looking charming in black, embroidered
in jet over pale grey. Her warm hair and bright,
brown eyes were the only touches of color. Mrs.
Bland Holt was also in jetted black, with nastur-
tiums in her low-dressed hair. Miss Winston -
Weir wore more black. In off-stage dress she is
one of the smartest women you could see, with a
well-coiffed hair, and a particularly “drawing-
room” manner. Dolly Vane, who sat beside
Charles Arnold and didn’t talk much, wore lilies
of the valley in her hair and a high-necked
black gremidine frock. Dolly is still recruiting
at Coogee. Her marriage comes off next week,
I believe. Mrs. Bernard Espinasse and Mabelle
Darley were both in blue. Pretty Cecil Engle-
hart (whose portrait, I am told, is being painted by
a very deft craftsman) wore a tucked cream silk,
with elbow-sleeves and a chiffon fichu. The
party —it was wholly theatrical, with the excep-
tion of this scribe, who happened to drop in
—included Carrie Moore, Fyvie Dench, Harry
and Noni Rickards, “Goody” and Mrs. Goody,
the Carons (Madame in white brocade, with
red-velvet shoulder-straps), Mr. and Mrs. Lauri,
“Charlie” Kenningham and “Pat” Bathurst,
Mr. and Mrs. Charles Arnold, and, at inter-
vals, two small girls, Marjorie (in white and
blue ribbons) and a little Miss “Goody.” “I
came early,” remarked the latter confidentially to
me ; “I was here at nine thith morning.”
Mr. Williamson’s health was proposed by Mr.
Henry Bracy. Replying, “ The Governor ” re-
marked that he sometimes felt like giving up work
now, and retiring into ” He didn’t say where
he thought of retiring into —with one voice the
company interpolated breathlessly “Suss/”
Which, being interpreted, means “ What will
become of us—of us—of us ? ”
And after that pathetic outburst there really
was nothing for J.C.W. but to assure everybody
that he intended to continue, for many a day yet,
his just methods of looking after “Suss,” him-
self included.
The Art Society opened its 21st. Annual
Exhibition last Saturday. Yes, twenty-first!
Things are getting on. Art is no longer the pale,
freen, callow thing it used to be —Art in Sydney
mean. It is growing up steadily, stealthily.
Soon it will be able to run. Already some of it
has walked away from us, and started feeling its
way round the old world with the aid of chairs and
tables as it were. Saturday morning’s unofficial
opening brought in a fair sprinkle of picture-
lovers, who took the canvases slowly, without a
crowd. The Mayoress was present with Miss
Harris. In the rooms I saw also Lady Innes,
Mrs. Gadsden, and a good many originals of the
wall-decorations. On Monday His Ex. came
along and purchased two little water colors,
“Transport Moravian” by J. H. Young and
“Sydney Harbor” by Lister-Lister.
His Ex.’s purchases are both dealing with
Sydney Harbor. Somebody said he wanted them
to remind him of his arrival in Sydney. I think
it might be of his leaving ditto.
Turning to the pictures. In the oil room there
are some very big canvases. One that jumps at
you as you enter is Mr. Coffey’s “The Curse of
Gold,” rather an original subject for an artist.
Not many of’em can look at it that way. But
Mr. Coffey’s pictures are all over the rooms, and
the red ticket —sold—flares from more than one of
his frames. His “Under the Ti-Trees” was
bought by the Gallery. It’s a strong and vivid
landscape, rich with green and yellow coloring
and luscious water. I believe the correct thing is
to stand in front of it and say, “This isn’t like
Coffey ! ” It is a charming picture now, and it
will improve with age.
But there ! Where would we be at an Art
Show without our little ready-made phrases ?
There are times when one’s feelings are too deep
for words. Then there are times when one has to
say something; when the occasion demands ex-
pression. And then come the good old hoary
patriarchs, that never failed anybody yet. A few
of them are (including the one above-mentioned),
“This is so restful”; “It reminds me of some-
thingand our old friend, “ I-don’t-know-any-
thing-about-pictures—but——
Signor Nerli’s “ Cosette” has many admirers.
It takes a full-length view of a rather mature girl,
with golden hair, and a beaver-colored frock lifted
to show a red lining. She wears a red flowered
toque with white wings. I mention this picture
because, while I was looking at it, it came home
to me forcibly that a man takes an entirely dif-
ferent view from a woman of the beauty of a woman’s
hair. An artist likes his woman’s hair loose,
rough, underdone. Crimps and brillantine, and
smartened-up lines, and a lot of hair-pins, and a
fringe-net, are not for him. And this is true, not
of artists only, but of the male sex in general. It
was cleverly put by a writer lately who said,
“ Coif your hair for a woman, and frill your petti-
coats for a man.” No man was ever caught by a
fringe-net yet.
In the catalogue I saw a “French Hat,” by
Tom Roberts, for ten guineas. But no; he isn’t
imitating Worth. It is a clever and fetching
study of a head as well as a hat, and was quickly
bought. The Art Gallery salved many sores
by purchasing Wolinski’s scholarship picture,
“ After Life’s Fitful Fever.” You will be glad to
hear that the artist gets £2OO for it. Mr. Daplyn’s
retiring little landscape and Miss Cusack’s jar of
Xmas bush and wild flowers have also been bought
by the Gallery. Mr. Moore-Jones shows “ The
Awakening Soul of Adam,” in oils, amongst his
•other exhibits. I understand that it took this
Adam five years to get into Paradise.
Women are remarkably well represented. Mrs.
Stoddart leads with two beautiful miniatures. I
like her “Mrs. Toohey” better of the two. The
"white hair, white lace frock, and the touch of vio-
lets in the corsage are very effective, and the like-
ness is most faithful. The “ Lady Mary Lygon ”
is inclined to stare. But for fineness and delicacy
it would be hard to beat these portraits. And
then there are innumerable exhibits from the
Misses Cusack, Sophie Steffanoni, Alison Vivian,
Margaret Flockton, Mrs. Tom Marshall, Ethel
Stephens (who is very poorly represented), Mrs.
Halligan, Alice Norton, while Miss Louise Hunt
and Miss Gether put in some clever wood-carving.
But oh ! I do wish the observations at the end
of the catalogue could be deemed unnecessary.
When you read, “Her shapely head is covered
with golden curls, while the pensive attitude has
•been depicted in an equally artistic manner,” you
ponder.
And the following sort of thing is what one
wants to find out for one’s self: “ This artist has
given us many pictures of still water, tall reeds,
and waving rushes, but in the one here presented
he has excelled himself. From the sturdy honey-
suckle tree on the left to the tall rushes on the
right all has been carefully studied from Nature,
and the artist must be congratulated on the re-
sult.” That just leaves me room to say that, in
my humble opinion, the Art Society quite excels
itself at this year’s Exhibition.
The Society of Artists’ dance came off last Fri
day, at Needs’ Room. It was a bright, busy little
dance, with a supper that lasted. But Artists were
not very plentiful. I could only count seven. And
one hadn’t learnt dancing, and one was engaged,
and one was married, and only one answered to
the requirements of a dancing man. The remaining
three were women—Mrs. Pauli, the energetic sec-
retary, in grey brocade brightened with pink roses,
Miss Haidee De Lissa in turquoise blue glace with
a lace overdress and violets in her hair, and Miss
Thea Proctor in white. Two sisters with fair,
much-bunched-out hair spun round like spinning
aureoles, while two black-haired sisters waltzed
with the languorous movements of black-haired
beauties.
By the way, our Art Societies have amalgam-
ated. That is to say, both exhibited in one and
the same room last week. The pictures were given
to help the Nelson Illingworth Fund. Afternoon
tea, and music by Elsie Stanley Hall and others,
made up a cheerful invitation crush.
The many friends of Mr. George Lambert will
be interested to hear- that the young artist will not
leave Sydney a bachelor. He starts for London this
next month on his Travelling Scholarship. Before
he goes, his marriage with Miss Amy Abseil will
take place, and his bride will go along with him.
Miss Abseil is known in Sydney as a story-writer;
perhaps she is better known as the original of cer-
tain charming pictures from Mr. Lambert’s brush.
Saturday last saw the departure for Berlin of
Miss M. F. Wright, one of our few penwoinen
who has succeeded in getting a connection with
foreign magazines. Miss Wright intends to study
the Charity System in Berlin, and also discover
whether the domestic Frau is the household slavey
that indignant writers have made out.
The death of Dr. Dagmar Berne, alas, was not
unexpected. This clever, kindly woman, well-
loved by all who came in contact with her, con-
tracted the seeds of consumption years ago while
studying medicine at London Hospital. She was
the first woman to enter a medical course at
Sydney University. Then she went off and fol-
lowed up her course at Dublin, London and
Edinburgh Universities.
“ What Happened to Jones” was what Sydney
had been wanting to know for a long time. A
crowded, dressy house filled the gay little Palace
Theatre on Saturday night. But what a flower-
less, though otherwise warm, reception ! I didn’t
see one green leaf from beginning to end, although
the co. included more than one Sydneyite return-
ing home after months of absence.
For a play that gets so laughed at the frocking
is “ all there ” ! Not paralysing, you know, but
careful and pretty. Miss Agnes Knights’ best
gown is black, embroidered in big tiger-lilies;
Miss Dot Frederic looks fluffy and smart in
white ; Miss Inez Bensusan wears a brocade with
a tucked lace bodice and a vivid green waisting;
Miss Hope Mayne looks her best in a pink
jewelled net, with long ruffed chiffon sleeves, and
a rose in her hair. Opinions differed about
another lady in the co. Is it a man? Is it a
woman? The likeness to Johnny Sheridan’s
Widow O’Brien sets the house wondering if Miss
Sallie Booth is an actual “she”—as indeed
she is.
A concert that I was unfortunately unable to
listen to last week was Miss Ada Parr’s at the
Centenary Hall. Lady Mary Lygon and party
were there, and a crowded house welcomed the
new pianiste, brought out by her teacher, the
indefatigable “ Heather” Fitz-Stubbs. Miss Parr,
by the way, is an A.M.A., Senior Honour Gold
Medallist.
I had a chat with Mr. Steffani lately. He and
Madame have resumed their flat in town after two
years’ absence in Europe. Glad to get back ?
The climate—the climate! And Steffani dwells
eloquently on how French people endure mud,
submit to mud, revel in mud ; how Paris takes no
notice of rain, but just goes on as calmly, and more
coolly than an Australian in his impossible—to
Paris—summer. Florence Schmidt will take
three years to get acclimatised, says Steffani.
Her Bananaland blood cools slowly, and her throat
husks on the slightest provocation.
That pretty sight, the Children’s Plain and
Fancy Dress Ball, comes off at the Town Hall next
Thursday at 7.30. Dancing—according to the
card—begins at 10 o’clock. I presume that is for
adults, who will be heartily welcomed on the pay-
ment of 7s. 6d. The proceeds to go to the Civil
Ambulance.
The other charity hop—the Masked Ball —looks
like a brilliant success to be. Apropos, a quaint
story tells how Lady Blank was going to represent
X., the well-known vendor of What-d’ye-call-it.
Everything was arranged. Lady Blank was to
receive—for the hospital—£so for her advt. Then
X. happened to mention the matter to his mother,
who happened to be a Quakeress. What she said,
and how she said it, is a secret. But she so
worked on her son’s feelings that he decided he
couldn’t have his firm represented on a woman's
back. Arrangements were abruptly shattered.
I have two deaths to report which will deeply
grieve many people—those of Mrs. Kelso King
and Dr. Alfred Shewen. The amiable lady
named, who had long been an invalid, passed
away suddenly and unexpectedly on board the
P. & O. mail-steamer on tier way to Melbourne,
and Dr. Shewen died in England, whither he had
retired for his health’s sake. When first he set-
tled in Sydney, many years ago, he was the medical
associate of tiiat good surgeon and charming man-
of-the-world, the late Dr. Fortescue. For 12
years he was one of the honoraries at the Alfred
Hospital. And so vanishes from earth the price-
less accumulated medical experience of one of our
keenest physicians.
Miss Annie Duncan, N.S.W. Inspector of Fac-
tories, is not an Englishwoman, as constantly
stated. She held the office of Inspector of Work-
shops and Laundries in London for some years,
but was born in Adelaide. Her father, Handyside
Duncan, was Health Officer there.
Sydney’s Potts Point isn’t all luxury. Some of
it is splendid penury. At a certain house they’ll
tell you the story of a neighbor’s Austral-Irish
servant—the true blend for impudence—who,
having received “warning,” was ordered to bring
in a dish of food that had seen service before.
There was a guest present, and instead of bearing
the dish right into the room, the girl put it on the
floor outside the dining-room door, and said
loudly : “Faith, ye’ve been up so often already
that you ought to be able to go the rest of the way
by yerself! ”
In new music there is still much of the Pyetrio-
tic. “Sons of Victoria,” by Angelo Maschereni,
has a vivid rythm in its refrain. Gould and
Co. also send “Farewell to Summer,” by Noel
Johnson, doleful, and palely imitative of Tosti’s
“ Good-bye.” From Paling and Co. come “ The
Nigger’s Lullaby,” a bright and tuneful coon-song
by Ettie Lewin; and “The Overlander,” a bush
ballad by Hugh and Mary Macdonald. These are
local musings, and I can recommend them both.
A burst of music-hall ditties comes via Paling.
“Nancy is my Fancy,” “Belinda,” with a very
taking air, “The Stay-at-Home Tourist,” and
“ The Lads in Navy Blue.” They are all lively
examples of the class of song that coins money
nowadays. There isn’t any definite distinction
between any two airs, but the pleasingness lies in
the fact that the public always feels it is getting
something it knows, and originality would only
bore and disturb the public’s equanimity.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Mrs. Edith Wilkie, petitioner in the sensa-
tional Melbourne divorce suit, is a plump and
golden-haired matron below middle-height, and
whose fascinations are of figure rather than face.
Though nearly all her life has been passed amid
rough surroundings, she speaks with the language
and intonation of the lady born and bred, and is a
splendid advertisement for the society tutoress
who taught her the — vide evidence—“etiquette of
social life” for 3s. an hour. Also Mrs. W. is
quite able to look after herself. Without bravado
or mock modesty she described many delicate de-
tails of her married life to that awful man, Purves,
and even scored off that individual, leaving the
box with an air of calm triumph after three days’
constant testifying. Purves described his client,
Adam Wilkie, the respondent, as a navvy who
had made money, and the description fits him.
Wilkie is simply a well-dressed edition of
any of a thousand labourers to be picked off any
contract. He shows no sign of the constant
wallowing in alcohol charged against him, and
wears his 48 years very lightly. Though giving
no indication of particular shrewdness, Wilkie is
one of a trio of brothers who made heaps of money
out of railway-contracting on an original plan.
The Coolgardie line was the last and most success-
ful venture of all. In the height of the gold-boom
they accepted the contract for £50,000 less than
cost, reserving the right to run the traffic until the
completion of the job. Rushing the rails down
with all speed they had a monopoly of the carriage
to the goldfields, charged what they liked, built
the permanent line by night, and netted so much
that Adam Wilkie took £60,000 as his share.
£23,000 of this he gave to his wife, and an equity-
suit will follow the present divorce to decide its
ownership. Both fortunes, however, are being
vigorously gnawed by the lawyers.
“Big and popular Jim Moloney, M.P., aider-
man of Melbourne, and solicitor, has just amazed
his friends by quietly getting married. He has
wed his little office type-writer. Propinquity,
propinquity ! Jim stands over 6ft.; new Mrs.
Jim is measured by inches. Not many people
know of the wedding even now. It was done
quietly—just like serving a subpoena. Dr. Pat
Moloney, his brother, is now living in Paris, and
has definitely abandoned Melbourne practice.
“And this is what has happened to big, easy-
going, dreamy Jim of many ambitions, after all
his diatribes against Holy Matrimony. The only
intimation James gave his friends of his nuptials
was to say, ‘ Alas ! my brother, it is not good for
man to live alone.’
“Dapper little surgeon T. N. Fitzgerald was
banquetted by his brother sawbones, in Melbourne
t’other day, on his return from S’Africa, where he
has been acting as a consulting surgeon. His
speech on the occasion is described by those who
heard it as a queer bit o.f quaint, unprepared
humor. The first man he met in Natal, after he
had poured himself into a khaki suit, was Brassey,
dressed up ‘ regardless ’ for a call on the Governor.
Old Tom insisted on taking Surgeon Tom along
with him. ‘ But I’ve got no clothes but these,
said Fitz. ‘ Never mind—come on ! ’ said Brassey.
Fitz. went, feeling very uncomfortable among a
crowd of well-dressed folk from England ; but he
avers that, before the day was through, the ‘ little
man from Australia ’ was first favorite. Which is
probably true. By the way, when Lady Hopetoun
comes along he will be able to see how a neat job
of his holds out. He patched up the smart
Countess’s nose after her accident in Melbourne,
and made it as pretty to look at and as good to
smell with as it was before.
“ Marshall-Hall’s accusers are ‘ fit to be tied ’
when asked for proofs (outside the ‘ Hymns ’) of
their bugbear’s alleged devilishness, and seem as if
they’d rather see him laid in his grave than shriven
of his supposed guilt. Some of the stone-throwers
are simple outsiders, misled by cock-and-bull
stories. Scribe’s charwoman pins her faith to a
baseless slander impugning the validity of the cul-
prit’s matrimonial arrangements, while the green-
grocer believes firmly that the ex-Director
presented the daughters of a popular parson with
an ‘ atheistical book ’ —a fallacy on the face of it,
seeing that their reverend pa plumps publicly for
the Prof. At present the petition in Marshall-
Hall’s favor annexes influential names galore, for
Mac-bigot and co. have blundered badly and
created a reaction in their quarry’s favor. They
sent round to all Masters of Arts and sich a com-
pilation of the very worst lines they could spot in
that absurd hymnal. So far from being shocked,
the recipients, surprised to find the written-up
plague-spots so harmless, now pooh-pooh the
Marshall-Hall drivel as ‘mere coarse tommy-rot.’
The compilers were twice as impressive while they
contented themselves with hinting darkly at the
wild-beastliness of the sinner and his works. Now
they have trotted out their bears— i.e., the crea-
tures of the music-man’s literary fancy, and
they’re only small bears, after all, not woman-
snatching satyrs or worse.
“One hears that the childless ‘leviathan,’ Alf
Josephs, just dead at ’Ome, has left a ‘bittie’ to
his nephevv and nieces, young Lincoln and his
sisters, children of the senior partner in the
Lincoln-Stuart firm. Lincoln, jun., dramatised
Marie Corelli’s ‘ Vendetta,’ lately played in
Sydney by Dampier under the name of the
‘ Power of Wealth.’ His sisters are dressy, chatty
members of the Austral Salon. One of them is
the wife of young Dr. Hewlett.

that Adelaide Govt. House will not be his abiding-
place for long, as he hopes to return to ‘ dear old
Melbourne, don’t-fou-know,’ very soon. In what
capacity ?
“ The Melbourne Children’s Hospital Fund is
accountable for two books (one of them not yet
out) that should take the fancy of all Right-
Thinking Persons who have a silver lining to their
pockets. Mr. Byron Moore, our white-hatted
master of the Cup ceremonies, and ‘ energetic sec.
of the V.R.C.,’ as the papers call him every year
when the warm weather begins to set in, is the
author of a happy little booklet published for the
benefit of the fund. Mr. Moore tells the story of
the stories in a prologue. A few years ago he was
living by himself in town, suffering from bank
failures and general gloom. His family were away
at Mount Martha—the place where visitors are
guaranteed to gain 31b. of flesh between Saturday
and Monday—and so, to soothe his mind and
make things look pleasant, he wrote these very
original fairy yarns to his children. And the
youngsters felt all the better for them, no doubt.
They are jolly tarradiddles about imp's and fairies,
and impets and fays, told with nice touches of
literary heart and humor, the sort of tales that a
grown-up can laugh at whilst reading them aloud
to the entranced Tom and Sissie. Has any other
bank-smashed city man ever done anything half
so good for a recreation, I wonder ? The book
includes some clever illustrations and lovely ad-
vertisements, and the Sands and Macdougall
printing is a work of art. Mr. Byron Moore has
given 2000 copies to the Children’s Hospital Fund.
The price is half-a-crown now, but they will be
worth more than that one day as readable
souvenirs of an interesting Melbourne identity
and a historical crisis. Don’t you forget it. The
title is ‘ How the Cruel Imp became a Good
Fairy.’ The other hospital volume, ‘Childhood
in Bud and Blossom,’ is being together by Mr.
Josh Lake, and will go as high as three half-
crowns for an edition de luxe. The list of con-
tributors looks promising for business—Ada
Cambridge, Mary Gaunt, ‘Banjo’ Paterson, J.
B. O’Hara, Donald Macdonald, and others.
Among the others is Major-General Baden
Powell, the delightful fellow and magnificent
advertiser who worried lots of Boers into their
graves. Also, the pictures are to be worthy of
their athletic selector, Josh.
“ Actor Kingston is hopelessly in love with his
charming little wife, who is to make music in Syd-
ney next month. The girls who have sought an
intro, to the actor of the eye-brows are sorry they
spoke—so to speak—for the stage-lover wearies
them with extravagant praise of his dainty and
vivacious partner. A little while ago the turtle-
doves tried to part, and Mrs. Kingston got as far
as Adelaide on her way to England. There she dis-
embarked, threw up her engagement (nothing
else), and rushed back to hubby’s arms.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Jimmy Governor is 23 years of age, Joe
19.
“Times change! ’ The suite of offices
formerly occupied by Crick and Meagher are now
used as chambers by G. H. Reid, Q.C.
Ex - Speaker Abbott’s Parliamentary
phrase : “My duty to my Queen, my God, and
my country.” God second, country last.
Mr. J. C. White, of Bathurst Free Press,
entered his 88th year recently. He is a colonist
of over 60 years, and in the late thirties built the
first Wesleyan chapel in South Australia.
Before N.S.W. Justice Cohen. Counsel,
a prominent M.P., addressing jury : “My honor-
able friend has told you—l beg his pardon—my
learned friend. But he may be honorable, too —
some day.”
Editor Sowden, of S.A. Register, is
believed to cherish Federal Parliamentary am-
bitions, but, like the much-boomed Bonython, of
the Advertiser, fears that defeat would be a bad
advt. for his paper.
Some newspapers are claiming the wife of
Boer General Botha as a grand daughter of Robert
Emmett. Possible, of course, .but not probable,
in view of the fact that Emmett was but 23 (and
unmarried) when he was hanged.
It is stated that the disease from which
John Mackenzie, until lately Maoriland’s Minister
for Lands, is suffering, is internal cancer, located
in the same region as the malignant growth which
killed the great M.L. Liberal pioneer, John
Ballance.
It is stated that Jimmy Governor, the
Breelong slayer, is the illegitimate son of a man
whose family was largely connected with Aus-
tralian coal-interests, and one of whose illegiti-
mate descendants, after inheriting a vast fortune,
recently died insane.
A certain notorious District Court Judge
laughs when members bait him in Parliament.
He openly asserts that half “ the low fellows ”
should be in gaol. As for dismissal, he threatens
to enter politics, when, he alleges, the same crea-
tures will crawl around him in expectation of
small favors.
Walter Griffiths, M.P., who went to
England on behalf of the Westralian Separationists
and who was warmly praised for his Federal work
by Kingston, is lying seriously ill in an Adelaide
private hospital. He is the handsomest, the most
popular, and with one exception, the youngest
member of S. A. Assembly.

Alf. Josephs,

much sea-bath-

and haunted
the shallows at
a pleasant but
u n popular
watering-place
in a most amaz-

Justice Owen, of N.S. W.
Supreme,Court, was called to the Irish
Bar 41 years ago, and has been a resident
of N.S.W. since 1860. He was for a time in the
Civil Service as Principal Under-Secretary.
The Soudan Ministry of some 14 years
ago, is a scattered family. Premier Stuart dead ;
Dibbs m a Savings Bank billet; Cohen on the
Supreme Court Bench ; Copeland Agent-General
in .London ; Dailey and Farnell dead ; Reid (but
he was only on view a little while) still on the
stump; Wright, Trickett and Abbott hanging
around ; and Jennings dead.
Alfred Dufrayer, one of the recipients of
the four “ Queen’s scarves,” is a son of the lady
who, when a girl at her father’s station of
Pechelba, where bushranger Morgan was killed,
was compelled by the desperado to perform at the
piano nearly all the night for his delecta-
tion. Morgan was shot leaving the house in the
morning by a stockman named Wendlaw.
Lord VVolseley’s recent severe slating of
the Aldershot division of the British forces was
directed really at the Duke of Connaught—com-
manding said division and considered by Wolseley
to be occupying a position which would be much
better filled by one at whom he (Wolseley) could
“sling-off” when needful. The incident will not
increase the violent regard which Her Gracious is
known to entertain for Wolseley.
A definite pronouncement has at last been
elicited from Premier Seddon re the M.L. Agent-
Generalship. King Dick avers thUt he has no
intention of supplanting Peeves, who is to be
offered office for another term. Reeves’ accept-
ance, however, is problematical in view of the
well-known fact that the editorship of a prominent
London paper is at his disposal. It may in the
end prove to be a matter of “ screw.”
Mainly about a prospective S.A. Premier :
With Federation accomplished and sundry big poli-
ticians out of the road, John Greeley Jenkins, Chief Sec.
in the present Holder Ministry, is certain to have an early
chance of S.A. Premiership. “ Jinks ” was born in
Susquehannah, Murka, in ’5l, but he is a full-blooded
Welshman. Struck Adelaide in ’7B with a consignment
of books and a Yankee twang, and immediately jumped
into politics. Has been in office more than half his time,
and is one of the sturdiest of Kingston’s democratic
followers. Will be 49 Sept. 8.
Robert Horseil, a man who was aboard the
Coromandel, which really arrived at S. Austra-
lia ahead of the Buffalo, though the latter is
always regarded as the pioneer vessel, died a fort-
night ago, aged nearly 100. The Buffalo had the
officers of the proposed settlement aboard ; hence
the Coromandel had to hold-off while she came up.
Bor years, before there was any railway, Horseil

N.S. W. Postmaster-Generalshipseems to be
the longest-lived portfolio : no less than 14 former
holders of the office still survive—Tighe, Samuel,
Burns, F. B. Suttor, Wright, Trickett, James
Norton, See, Dan O’Connor, C. J. Roberts, H.
Clarke, Kidd, Cook, V. Parkes. The reason why
so many ex-Postmasters-General are still in the
flesh no doubt is that the office is usually held by
the fool of the Ministry, and when a man has no
brains he is never attacked by that most fatal of
diseases, nervous Ability.

Sam M‘Caughey, M.L.C., the Riverina
bachelor millionaire, was once showing round his
Coonong station a young and pretty widow.
Drawing a long breath, he said, “ There is only
one thing that Coonong wants ! ” The lady
thought quickly, and coquettishly queried :
“What is that, Mr. M'Caughey?” “ Water,”
replied the gilded Samuel.
When the late John O’Shanassy was made
a knight he was dissatisfied. He wanted
a baronetcy to hand down to his son Matt.
—who recently died. Had poor Matt,
got the thing he wouldn’t have known
what to do with it—he’d rather
have won Melb. Cup every third
year. But Big John wouldn’t >
have got even the knighthood
but for his old friend Duffy.
When C.Gr.D. was kicked
out of office in ’72, after
being a year in (most of
it was recess), his friend,
Dr. .John Grey (who was
tried with him in ’44 at
Dublin for sedition) and
John Cashel Hoey, hus-
band of the novelist, went to
Gladstone, then Premier,
and represented what he
(Duffy) had done for the
land-laws of Victoria, and how
badly he had been treated, and
asked that he should be soothed
and consoled. Then, when Duffy
got his knighthood, O’S.’s friends
made a row, and so Big John
was bebaubled, too. Which is
true history, till now unprinted.

The demise of Alfred of Saxe-Coburg re-
calls an incident of his Maoriland peregrinations
as Duke of Edinburgh. In Rotorua district he
succumbed to the fascinations of a dusky Delilah,
assumed marital responsibilities, and boarded at
the pah. When he departed, the damsel was
conveyed by her people to a place where she was
strictly secluded for months from the other sex
upon the chance of the appearance of a little
coffee-colored semi-Edinburgh. It was not to be,
however; though it may be mentioned that in
after years other touring Lancelots were more suc-
cessful in this way with the lady, who is still alive.
The slavish way in which Australian news-
papers crib from reference - books printed in
England was curiously illustrated the other day
in Melbourne. The death of Faed, R.A., was
cabled on Friday. Both Argus and Age give
short biographies of him, with a string of the
titles of his best pictures. But neither paper
mentions the only fact of local interest about him
—neither apparently knew it—that the picture
that established his fame, “The Mitherless
Bairn,” hangs in Melbourne Gallery. The Argus
says he made “a great hit with his popular pic-
ture ‘The Mitherless Bairn,’” but is evidently
quite ignorant of the close proximity of said canvas
to the Argus office.
Barton’s English trip seems to have
changed him somewhat. He keeps himself more
in hand; is not, perhaps, more urbane, but more
deliberately urbane; respects himself more in
preserving a more restrained manner. Perhaps
his wits have gained polish as well as his taste.
He seems to have somewhat modified his charac-
teristic tendency to subtilise and refine —a weakness
which has spoilt many of his popular addresses.
Rubbing shoulders with John Morley and James
Bryce may have widened his hitherto mainly
academic sympathy with Liberalism. He seems
to his friends to have grown visibly in mind and
in scope of purpose.. If Barton had to stand up
to his equals in a House of Commons, in the fence
of mind with master mind, he might grow into a
very great Parliamentarian indeed.
In his recent Melb. lecture on Eureka
Stockade, banker H. G. Turner told an awful
story of disloyalty in the weeks preceding the
fight. The American consul’s dinner was proceed-
inr>' at Ballarat when the toast of the Queen was
called. To the proposer’s horror, nobody moved ;
but at last the spokesman of the local men arose
and said: “We respect the Queen, but we’re
asked to respect her maid servant and her man-
servant, her ox and her ass, and this we will not
do.” In connection with the Eureka business, it
is not generally known that the now awfullest
of Vic. Conservative drivellers was among the
contingent marching from Maryborough to the
assistance of Peter Lalor, or that Dr. Enabling,
father of the present sickening Tory, led the
Melb. agitation of sympathy with the diggers.

Trotter Bethune writes :
Some six or eight months ago, you published a para-
graph to the effect that David Syme would cheerfully give
half the profits of the Age to anyone who would cure him
of his chronic rheumatism. Now.l have an infallible cure
for rheumatism. So after reading your par. I wrote to
Mr. Syme enclosing my prescription and hoping it might
cure him. I received a letter in replv signed “G. Syme ”
in which the writer pulled my leg a little. He said that
Mr. Syme thanked me for my prescription and trusted
that it might be as beneficial to him as it had been to me.
A few days ago, not having heard further from Mr. Svme"
I began to think it was about time the half-share of the
paper came along, and I accordingly waited upon him
ostensibly .on mining, business. Turning to leave his
apartment, I remarked, casual-like, “ I sent you a pre-
scription for rheumatism some time ago.” “ Ah,” he said
“ what was it you recommended, was it milk ?” ’ “ No,” I
replied, “it was ” (naming the formula). He remarked
“ Very funny, but The Bulletin said I had rheumatism!
and I’ve had dozens of prescriptions showered upon me
ever since. As a matter of fact, I never had rheumatism
in all my life.”

It is the custom of Yic. Chief Justice
Madden—who could long live on his own adiposity
—never to eat in the middle of the day, and very
often he keeps the court sitting through the usual
luncheon-hour. But sometimes, in deference to
the preoccupied look of a hungry barrister, he
adjourns for 20min. or half-ari-hour. During the
hearing of the Wilkie divorce case, t’other day,
the lawyers, as usual, rushed in at the time for
resuming, munching the last mouthful of an un-
finished meal—but only to be kept waiting for
another half-hour for his Waxelleney’s appear-
ance. Purves, Q.C., entered a mild protest by
remarking that if his Honor had adjourned the
court for an hour several members of the bar
would have been saved from indigestion. “Be
sides,” he added, “two waiters have probably
already died from exhaustion.” His Wax. seemed
hardly to relish the reference to his unpunctuality,
as he snorted, “I might have done so had I the
gift of prophecy.” The lawyers, however, now
hope that the Chief has taken to eating lunch,
even if he doesn't care to say so.
The creditors of the bankrupt Sydney firm
of Meyerfeld and Co. recently recorded by
unanimous vote their appreciation of the conduct
of Mr. H. A. Trower (one of the partners) who,
despite the fact that he had been relieved from
legal responsibility, came forward and honorably
settled the liabilities of the firm, which exceeded
£lOO,OOO. Mr. Trower’s firm went through the
Bankruptcy Court in ’9l, the dividend received
by the creditors being 3£d. in the £. By lucky
speculations on London Stock Exchange, since
his failure, Mr. Trower amassed a large fortune, a
considerable portion of which, he has now
to discharging his old-time liabilities. In many
respects Mr. Trower’s vicissitudes of fortune
closely resemble those of J. G. Ward, the present
acting-Premier of Maoriland. After the failure
of his Farmers’ Co-operative Co. some years ago,
Mr. Ward was forced into bankruptcy, and, at
the instance of some of his creditors, was subjected
to all the harassing vindictiveness of the Bank-
ruptcy Law—which can be very vindictive indeed
when it tries. When all was over, Mr. Ward
calmly set to work, made another pile, and put
his quondam tormentors to the blush by paving
up to the last penny.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Aboriginalities.
After all the talk about “racial hatred,”
“ reversion to primal savagery,” and so on, in the
case of the Breelong black murderers, the captured
"Jacky Underwood’s” evidence makes it look
very much as if the redoubtable Jimmy Governor
is merely suffering from a bad attack of the white
man’s “ Dead wood Dick.” Said Jimmy to Jacky,
according to the latter’s story :
“I want to be the greatest murderer in New South
Wales. I’ll do for the first man I come across, either man
or woman, I don’t care a . The first woman I get
alive I tie her up to a tree, get a lot of bushes, make a
light round her, take her clothes off, get cold water and
chuck all over her.”
Pure Texas this! And again, according to
Jacky—" They think if they can capsize a train
they will be good men.” (“Darklieart the
Destroyer.”) “When they got to the foot of
Nulla Mountain they would slip off their clothes
and blacken themselves all over, let their hair
grow long, put eagles’ feathers in their heads.”
(Indian-warrior fashion), “and when they come
across any blackfellows they are going to take
their gins and live like that for 20 years.” How
schoolboy-like it all is !
* * * *
A bobby, who lately struggled in to Port
Augusta (S.A.) with three aboriginal prisoners,
deserves credit as a plodding man with the grit
that subdues mountains. He captured his three
black brethren away up in the region where there
is nothing but loneliness and rocks, and rode
with them 500 miles to Blood Creek. Then,
finding there was neither feed nor water on the
110-mile stage which remained before the railway
line was reached, he left the horses, and manfully
walked and belted his three niggers over that last
awful stage in four days. The mixed train took
them to Port Augusta, and, having handed over
his prisoners, the trap set out on his return jour-
ney. If he never turns up again at Arltunga the
authorities may find a policeman’s bones on the
dry 110-mile stretch north of Oodnadatta. What
pay does the active and intelligent officer get for
this kind of work? The word “active” isn’t sar-
casm when applied to a man who can hustle three
grumpy aboriginals over 110 miles of dry country
on foot in four days.

A Queensland trooper, near Esk, recently
rode 120 miles in 22 hours after a horse-thief, and
got his man. It wouldn’t do to send him after
the Governors. He might catch them.

“Drill” : Driving to Mt. William (Vic.)
goldfields, the other day, I met two men with a
wheelbarrow, one in front with a rope pulling,
the other behind pushing; barrow loaded with
picks, shovels, cradle, tin-dish, &c. Told me they
had come from Charlton, distance, 140 miles.
Had averaged only Is. 6d. a week up there, and
got sick of it.

their tracks. It wa3 a fearful bungle ; for, though

have done that while the other —or others—fol-

Dear Bulletin. —Why shouldn’t the
"whalers” of N.S.W. have an electorate in the
Assembly? Says Gov. Lewelling of Kansas : “If
these men are an army of tramps and vagabonds,
they are none the less representative in character,
still the people shall rule and the voice of
vagabondage representing the majority, must and
shall be heard.” And what price a “whaler”
Senator, if the swagmen would take out their
rights and vote ?He would head the poll; but as
long as beer is beer they won't take out the little
blue paper. Personally I should be a “ cert.” for
a seat in the Senate, if I qould muster a pint a
man along the rivers, before it is too-late to run
the “ whalers ” in.— Scotty the Wrinkles.

“E.C.” : High time some steps were
taken to curtail the duration of Maori tangis. A
native died from typhoid at Petane (Hawke.s
Bay) recently, his remains were taken as far as
Matatu (Bay of Plenty). The coffin was on the
Taupo road five weeks while the tangis were in
progress at the different places en route. At one
kaianga, the party with the corpse remained 10
days.
“Pelican”: Be the protraction of ob-
sequies over a Maori child until the corpse
dissoluted by effluxion of time and gave a mourner
typhoid. Australian aboriginals can go consider-
ably further. I have seen a lot of infant’s bones
in a gin’s “dilly-bag,” and was told that said
bones belonged to a piccaniny of hers which died
in its first year. The corpse had then been
deposited in the bag and despite the olfactory
inoonvenience, had been kept therein until only
the bones remained—presumably as a reminder
of what might have been.
* * * *
“ Canip ” : Down Leeuwin (W.A.) way
was once discovered, some miles inland, a skeleton
from which all vestiges of apparel had long dis-
appeared, but amongst the bones lay a flint-and-
steel and some gold and silver Spanish coins dated
well back in the 18th century. Marooned, pre-
sumably. The relics are now in Perth Museum,
though they do say that the gold coins exhibited
are not the originals.

“ Chas. F.” writes about rabbits :
“N—d—W” (B. 2/6/’00) takes the N.S.W. and Q.
rabbit-proof fence as a standard of efficiency. I worked
for nearly the whole of ’B9 on a station on Q. side of that
fence. (Herewith, for your private information, is name
of said station.) At the end of ’BB there were
no rabbits on the station, but during ’B9 four
men were employed keeping them down. I know
positively that rabbits (then swarming in N.S.W.)
were thrown over the fence to keep things going ; and
now there is little difference between one side and the
other. Netting is not effective, except in small extents ;
there is always a gap of some sort in long miles of fencing,
and even in the case of small stretches rabbits are thrown
into cultivation paddocks to make things good for any
chance swaggie looking for a job. Also, young rabbits
will squeeze through the mesh when chased by the bucks.
Have never seen a rabbit climb a netting-fence, but have
known them climb upright hollow trees for 12.0 r 15ft.
inside the pipe. Have also seen them hanging dead 6ft.
up in forked mulgas. Rabbits will also jump 3 or 4ft.
over a drooping place in the netting, and some of them
will burrow under a fence. The Australian scrub-rabbit
in any case is not to be judged by the “ dairy-fed ” bunny
of England. The struggle for existence here has
sharpened his wits. He is hardier and more adaptable,
and, in the opinion of competent observers, is developing
the jumping qualities of those aboriginal animals whose
existence depends on the facility with which they can
bound over logs, broken scrub, &c. In Port Lincoln dis-
trict (S.A.) rabbits burrow a lot deeper on account of the
wild dogs, and there I have seen bunnies killed in burrows
(not wombat-holes) 6 and 7ft. deep vertically. I have not
been in the bush since ’94, but I know there was then an
unwritten law among “ travellers ” never to kill a rabbit.
One evening, when eight of us had camped on a Darling
bend, one man went away to get bait for the lines, and
came back with a live rabbit. The rest looked at him
aghast. “ You ain’t going to take a rabbit tor bait, are
you ? ” shrieked the old hand—“ and plenty of good sheep
about I Look here, my lad—by and by you’ll be looking
for work, and the rabbit ’ll give you more than the sheep.
Never kill a rabbit, unless you’re paid for it.”

THE CIVILISING WAVE.
BIBLE DISTRIBUTOR: “Here, my
ignorant brother, is a Book I

IGNORANT BROTHER: “That's all right; but if you've got a new thing by Guyrie Boorell or

W. A. Grenike, formerly a grazier in
western N.S.W. —at one time he was Mayor of
Wilcannia —writes to the Perth papers that Wes-
tralia is making a great mistake m fighting so
hard against a rabbit invasion. From his experi-
ence of western N.S.W, and Westralia Grenike
says that neither hares nor rabbits will ever
become a serious pest in Westralia, while they
would form a welcome addition to the scanty meat
supply of the way-back mining population.
* * * *
“ E.D.” : It doesn’t seem to have occurred
to the gohanna-eaters to try the ugly jew-lizard as
a viand; and yet, of all queer edible creatures of
the Australian bush this repulsive-looking little
beast is certainly the most toothsome. After
being left in hot-water awhile he skins easily,
and when skinned and fried his flavor and appear-
ance are exactly those of the much-esteemed
native black-fish. Indeed, the jew-lizard might
be dished up to black-fish enthusiasts without
their ever discovering the fraud. I know a man
near Cassilis, Vie. (a British new-chum, too), who
developed quite a passion for the jew-lizard, and
devoured it in preference to any other obtainable
tucker.
* * * *
"Fancy Free”: Before shooting-season
opened a Northern M.L. constable met a gum-
digger carrying a dead kiwi. Constable, an
amateur taxidermist, asked for and obtained the
bird. At home, the policeman found kiwi in the
list of protected game, and digger was promptly
summoned. Fine, £1 and costs. But the con-
stable paid the fine. Truly a straight policeman.

“Give some account of the Romans,” was

This is what appeared on one slate :
Dear Sir, —There isn’t many Romans about here, my
sister Kate married a roman but mother didn’t want her
because he only gets thurty bob a week from old S——
and the priest gets a lot of it. I can’t think of no more to
tell you now only the Romans put babys in salt and oil to
chrissen them.—Yours truely ; .
» • • *
Over some thousands of sq. miles of the
semi-desert country of far-west N.S.W., now
carrying a bit more herbage than usual owing to
timely rains, millions of caterpillars have been
gorging on young saltbush and anything else
tender. The matter having come under the
notice of the Agricultural Dept., that sapient
institution reported that the affected plants might
be treated with paris-green and treacle ! But a
natural antidote came along in the. form of a ter-
rific two-days’ duststorm, the wind and sand
whereof simply tore the billions of soft overfed
vermin to pieces.
* * * *
“Yang Anyut ” : Be preserved eggs (“ D.”
B. 14/7/’00). The Chinese bury duck-eggs in a
mixture of charcoal, paddy-husks, and salt. About
seven years are needed to ripen an egg to perfec-
tion, and then when boiled it has a golden-brown
yolk and black-and-grey marbled albumen. It
took me a long time to tackle the first, but I soon
got to like them.

Travelling through western
sheep-stations one is struck by the almost uniform
taciturn incivility of the “hands.” Whether
driving (as I was), riding or walking one meets on
every station the same kind of reception—a super-
cilious stare (seeming to say “Another blanky
stranger!”) a surly “G’day!” and grudgingly-
fiven directions as to route. One loses little,
owever, by this characteristic unsociability, for
of the outside world the ordinary station hand
knows nothing, and as he further never reads any-
thing but “ Dead wood Dick” literature, these
remarks will never hurt his feelings. He has no
ambition to see a city or the sea, sand is content to
remain on the one station all his life-time. I have
come to the conclusion that the evolution of this
type of bush-denizen is due to the fact that the
avoidance of strangers meets the approval of the
boss, who himself has no welcome for visitors,
other than the neighboring feudal magnates.

“ A.P.R.” : The luck of drunks ! At a
bush pub. I once saw a drover come in and hand
over his cheque. In an hour he was scarlet drunk
—got on his nag, cantered 100 yards, and fell off
five times. Then he came galloping back and
threw himself off at the pub.-door. Said he was
thirsty, “ and the horse wouldn’t pull up.” And,
after all this, he didn’t show a scratch.

“ The Bulletin is the most read of all the
Australian weeklies.” —Pierre Leroy-Beaulieu, in
Revue des Deux Mondeh, republished in his Les
SOCIETES ANGLO-SAXONNES.
While the last lot of Bushmen were at
Albany, on the way over, an Indian hawker went
alongside the steamer, with fruit, in a boat. The
soldiers lowered money in nose-bags, and got the
fruit up by same means. The last money lowered
was 45., and for it the Indian only sent up 12
bananas. Ere he had time to get his boat away,
however, two buckets of tar were emptied on top
of him, much to the delight of the whole ship !
Died recently, near London, Journalist
Blanch, founder of the famous Thirteen Club.
Blanch’s notion was to discredit every kind of ill-
luck-superstition ; with. which view he once gave
a dinner at the club to 169 guests, arranged in
parties of 13 at 13 tables. All the waiters were
cross-eyed ; a looking-glass was formally smashed,
to begin with; and the guests made a point of
spilling the salt, and in other ways defying all the
prevalent malominous notions.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they wili
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *

“ Louis M.” on “a deeper Sheol ” :
Re “ Trotter’s ” remarks (B. 23/6/’00), Surat (Q.), from a
travelled “ out-backer’s ” view, may be considered an ely-
sium. For instance, vegetables, beef, bottled beer, fish,
fruit, sheets, bathrooms, &c., are not absolutely paralys-
ing phenomena there. Also, there is usually a fair sheet
of water in the Balonne, and the eye may dwell pleasantly
on the green of the Chinamen’s gardens. Coaches, too,
pass through the place, and dwellers therein may, if they
choose, thus learn much of the outer world. “ Trotter ”
should try a further-out “ hotel ” —where a stranger may
not hope undirected to recognise the main building, or
rather, the hut that bears somewhere on its undressed-
“ slab ” wall the legend of, say, “ Bed Dorg hotle —by—
Bob Maginis.” Sundry “ clay-pans ” dotting the chaotic
scene (these in their turn speckled with various unplea-
sant thihgs) afford the sole water supply, and all around
are multitudinous dogs, pigs, goats, and fowls. Cuisine
—pork and damper with (on rare occasions) “ pigweed.”
Liquors—all from one bottle, and a mystery at that.
Bath—not to be even hinted at. Let “Trotter” put in
five years on a W.Q. “out-station,” broken only by
periodical visits to such bowers of delight as the above,
and he’ll simply howl for the fleshpots of Surat.

“F.W.R. Had a pretty clever dog
once. If you showed him the cart-winkers he’d
bring the cart-horse, and if the riding-bridle he’d
bring the hack. Sent him home once with a calf
which stuck him up half-way and wouldn’t budge
an inch one way or the other. The dog suddenly
tore off home and into the dairy, dipped his tail in
the milk, went back to the calf, gave him the
milky tail to suck, and so got home easy.

“ Canip ” : My black friend Jerry explains
as follows why bees don’t sting niggers
You climb it tree and stuff that fella bee-hole up with
hankshiff ; then you out nutha fella hole longit tree and
put it other fella hankshiff longa that. Wait lily while
an’ that fella bee smell it blackfella longa hankshiff an’ get
very frightun, cause him know it blackfella, and no sting.
You cut him right open, then alrigh.
Jerry leaves it an open question whether it is the
smell of the nigger or the nigger himself that
frightens the bees. If it is the former I can quite
understand it. The rational explanation, I ima-
gine, is that the bees are bewildered by the block-
ing of the holes and the tapping, and start gorging
themselves stupid with honey, so that by the time
the nest is opened up they are in no condition to
show fight. [But bees (European variety) do sting
niggers.]

almost say these words.

“C.H.L.” . The pickling of live pork
was tried in Maoriland long before that
Muswellbrook farmer (B. 28/7/’00) attempted
it. Over a score years ago, the then
young and foolish mistress of a household in
Karori, near Wellington, caused her pigs to be
supplied with the brine from which a lot of dead
pork had just been taken. “Didn’t want the
brine wasted.” Result, within a few days, exten-
sive porcine obsequies.
“ Canip Cera-
todus Fosterii (a
“ lung-fish ”) is un-
known outside Bur-
net and Mary Rivers
(Q.) and their tribu-
taries. True barra-
mundi fexist only in
the Upper Dawson
(Q.). So-called bar-
ramundi on N.Q.
coast —called Palmer
fish at Mackay—is
only a gigantic perch
which grows up to as
much as 2001 b., and
is most voracious.
At Port Alma
wharf, Keppel Bay
(Q.), the way of
catching them is to
bait a shark-hook
with a piece of white
rag, and jiggle it
near the surface after
dark. When a
“barra” is hooked
he strives hard to
entangle the line
with the wharf-piles
—hence the line
should be very short.
Where the gigantic
perch are plentiful,
in tidal creeks, all
round is heard the
sound they make
with their mouths at
the surface very
like the popping of
ginger-beer corks.
Stanton and Cole went out from Grant
(Vie.) into the distant hills to work an alluvial
creek which Cole had found. The latter had
come in from a prospecting tour full of his amaz-
ing discovery. It was in country, he said, where
no white but himself had ever set foot, and it
seemed to be more his pride and glory as a pioneer
than the richness of his find that made him en-
thuse by night and day till Stanton was persuaded.
After a toilsome two-days’ journey, the partners
struck the new field—hitherto untrodden by
Human boot—and they camped. Next morning
they went out with pick and pan, and Stanton
washed his first wash. It yielded about two and-
a-half colors and—a false tooth set in vulcanite.

Gr.C. A.: That conundrum was graven on Cheops’
pyramid by a drunken Egyptian laborer, Ishmael:
What a tame wild man you are! M.L. (Vic.) : “ Flana-
gan ” jingles fairly, but lacks point D.P.L. : “ Days of
Life” too close to A. L. Gordon A.M.M.: Bulletin
believes in the entire abolition of the legal attachment of
wages—this as a step in the direction of the utter aboli-
tion of legal process for the recovery of debt. Imprison-
ment for debt has been practically abolished, and gradu-
ally there has been established in many countries the
freedom from seizure by creditors of a certain amount of
household furniture, tools of trade, life-assurance and so
on. Democracy and civilisation will continue to march in
that direction and credit will ultimately be given or
refused on a man’s personal character and chances, not
upon the creditor’s power to sell up the debtor’s home....

What is the grievance ? The B.N.Z. was not a
State Bank but a purely private affair when it made the
investment you complain of. Maoriland Govt, did not
lose anything by putting the money temporarily to its
current account at the Colonial Bank. It had to put the
money somewhere ; and the Colonial was known to he
able to pay its depositors in full, though the shareholders’
capital was lost. The State has got £441,000 odd from
the people by the sale of Consols, and the annual interest
is £15,452. With the money it has repurchased State
securities which cost in annual interest £16,630, thereby
making a good saving. And what is the difficulty of
realising? When people are willing to buy Consols
at par from the State, they will also buy them at par
from private persons. The talk of £5 charges for selling
£5O worth of Consols is pure drivel. Consols are more
easily saleable than land or houses, anyhow “George

As Good as the Original.
The attention of readers is invited to an adver-
tisement which appears elsewhere concerning the
“ Encyclopaedia Britannica.” The London Times
has reprinted the latest —the ninth —edition of
this monumental work, and is now offering it at
very much reduced prices. The publishers’ cata-
logue price for the 25 volumes of which the work
consists is £37, but the arrangements made by
The Times are such that the purchaser may obtain
the whole work for £l7. That is, if he arranges
to pay cash, in which case he will have the
pleasure of knowing that he has obtained posses-
sion of this standard publication, in 25 quarto
volumes, at a price lower than the wholesale price
at which it used to be sold to booksellers. But,
if it will suit him better to make monthly pay-
ments, the purchaser has only to send a guinea to
the Sydney agent, with an order for the edition in
cloth, half morocco, or full morocco. He will, of
course, have to pay more for the latter bindings,
but if he chooses cloth he can get the whole
edition for 17 guineas. The reprint is excellent
from the printer’s point of view—quite as good as
the original edition sold at more than double the
price. On the payment of the guinea the books
are sent to the subscriber. It would be late in the
day to say anything in favor of the “Encyclo-
paedia Britannica,” on which the best-informed
men of the time have spent their labor. Full par-
ticulars of the terms and so forth will be found in
our advertising columns. It will be noted that
the offer is to be -permanently withdrawn on
Sept. 15th.
«.
“Avenge us ! ” cry missionaries when cor-
nered by the heathen. Taking it as prescribed
that it is their duty “to teach all nations,”
modern missionaries must learn to discharge their
duty as the early Christians discharged it. If
they penetrate China or elsewhere they must
accept the consequences, without right of appeal
to consul or ambassador. That is their Scriptural
bond. If they persist in practically holding that
their spirits are in the proselytising cause, but
that their skins are still the property of their
Government, it will become the duty of statesmen
to legislate that any man or woman who enters a
foreign country to teach Christian doctrines shall
lose his citizenship.

MR. R. VL SMITH’S
The fact that Mr. R. M. Smith intends to\
retire from political life at the termination of
the present Parliament has placed the Con-
servative party in Hawthorn m somewhat of
a predioament, as difficulty is experienced in
finding a suitable candidate. So acute did
the situation beoome that a meeting waa
called last night in the local coffee-palace to m
consider the matter. About 30 electors were
present, and the chair waa occupied by Mr.
Clooper.
The Chairman said that, notwithstanding '
strong pressure, Mr. R. M. Smith had an
nouncecf his intention of not standing for re
election.

“Mr. Edwards, S.M., who presided at Newtown Police Court yesterday,
had before him a number of cases in which well-known local tobacconists were
changed with trading on Sunday.
“The informations, instead of being laid under the old Act of Charles 11., were
laid under the 10th section of the Towns Police Act (Vic. If., No. 2). This section
provides that justices of the peace shall, as far as in their power, cause the Lords
Day to be duly observed.”

POLITICAL
POINTS
Reid avoids Barton as though he were the
Bogey-man.
Surely it is not intended to proclaim the
Commonwealth on 26th January, “the Day We
Were Lagged ” !
S.A. Labor-party takes a plebiscite Aug.
29 to select two Senate and three House of Repre-
sentative candidates.
Teetotalism is having a bad time at
N.S.W. bye-elections. Cold-tea candidates went
under in Philip-division, Bathurst and Canter-
bury.
Up to now, no man whose services would
be worth anything in the Federal Parliament has
publicly indicated that he aspires to hold simul-
taneously a seat in the State legislature.
Quite a storm arose in S.A. Assembly last
week, owing to the discovery that Treas. Holder,
by including the Brookman gift of £15,000 to S.A.
School of Mines in his revenue receipts, had
turned a deficit into a surplus. Although Holder
claimed precedent for his action, it looked at one
time as if the proceeding would produce a no-
confidence motion, and that is not unlikely even
yet.
IN TOPHET.
EX-PUBLICAN: “ Halloo I What brought you here?”
EX-BREWER'S MAN: “Oh! I was drowned in a vat of beer.”
EX-PUBLICAN: “Breathe on me!”
All the lawyers in S.A. Parliament, save
only Attorney-General Gordon, are in opposition
to the Holder Govt. These are Stirling (the Tory
leader in the Council), Glynn, Homburg, Von
Doussa, and John Downer.
The Austral-S’African warriors are gradu-
ally being absorbed in the Rhodesian and Trans-
vaal police; and the N.S.W. Govt., though it
wants policemen, is holding open vacancies in the
force for returned troopers from the Transvaal.
Every Australian soldier has a (hob jy s) aon m
his knapsack.
Price, M.P.. leader of S.A. Labor Party,
asserts publicly that he has never before seen so;
much “ lobbying” as there has been in connection
with a certain Electric Tramways Bill, and it is.
suggested that if only a list of those who
“expect ” to become shareholders were made
public there would he surprises !
Cabled that N.S.W. Agent-General Cope-
land has “expressed strong disapproval of Mr.
Cecil Rhodes’ scheme to introduce Chinese into
Rhodesia.” An interesting side-light on the
Rhodesian legend.
Queensland member Newell spent some
time t’other night in describing to the House the
benevolent nature of syndicates. Numbers of
men, he explained, combine with one common
end. Just so : Divid-end.
Let there be no delay in making the State
politicians understand that they cannot go for
two seats —that they must drop the State bone if
they aspire to the Federal joint—that National
politics are one thing and State ditto quite
another. “Reign in Hell—serve in Heaven”:
these are the alternatives for such people.
The Lyne Cabinet is about equally divided
on the question of one-man-one-seat in the ap-
proaching dual Parliamentary era. Lyne and
O’Sullivan think they are good enough for seats
both Federal and local, but Crick and Hassall are
for each man sitting on one stool. Slight pres-
sure from the House will force action.
The Brisbane crowd that met delegate
Dickson at the railway-station was certainly not
enthusiastic over that gentleman’s return, and was
even disposed to be uncomplimentary. It is said,
by the way, that Dickson is to be the Govt. Fede-
ral candidate for Brisbane division. It may be
safely predicted that his coruscations won’t
dazzle the Federal-Assembly.
Among the many holes through which
N.S.W.’s good money has dropped, the vote to
the Municipal Charities- Association seems about
the meanest. In four years the M.C. Ass. got
£1025 from the Treasury and spent it all in
salaries and expenses. It also raised £BB from
the public, of which £3l went on charity and £57
in salaries and expenses. This subsiding of self-
constituted he and she cats to inquire into
the troubles of their fellow-creatures and then
leave them just where they were is a small and
miserable Paul Pryism.
A page of unre-
corded history. J. B.
Patterson was returned
to Vic. Assembly at
the ’B6 general election
with the avowed inten-
tion of dishing the
Gillies - Deakin Minis-
try. Gillies’ huge bribes
to the constituencies in
the form of railways,
waterworks, and pros-
pecting votes under his
boom-budget made that
an impossibility for the
time being ; and the
Govt, introduced a Bill
providing for a redis-
tribution of the elector-
ates, which, if carried
as proposed, would have
unseated Patterson at
next election. The re-
sourceful J. 8., up to
that time the most
active member of Op-
position, suddenly
changed front, and sup-
ported the Govt, on the
stock-tax, his vote sav-
ing it from defeat. As
a reward, the proposed
redivision of electorates
was amended to suit
him, and he was taken
into the Ministry as
Postmaster-General and
Minister of Customs.
But the reconciliation
was compulsory, and
Patterson got even with
his “ friends.” Under
threat of breaking up
the Ministry if they did
not agree to his views,
he obliged them to call
out the military for the
suppression of the mari-
time strike, and, as a
consequence, theGillies-
Deakin administration
was hurled from office a
few weeks later.
The difference be-
tween political and
judicialsusceptibility to
friendly influence was
well illustrated in a late
application by an ex-
solicitor to be re-en-
rolled by N.S.W. Full
Court. The ex-solicitor,
who is an M.L.A., pro-
duced the signatures of'
113 M.’sP. backing his
application. If Parlia-
ment had had the power
to re-enrol such a round
robin would have settled the matter,
It took Tasmania’s Leg. Council just about
five'minutes to decide that the proposal to have
Q ne ]yj an One Vote at Assembly elections was a
subversion of the Sacred Rights of Property, and
the good old .plural vote was promptly re-inserted,
Still, it was only done by eight against seven, and
if there had been any real fight among the seven
it needn’t have been done at all.
Brisbane Govt, organs eternally wail about
| ; ] ie loquacity of Labor members and suggest the
introduction of a drastic closure system. They
wax particularly eloquent on this theme whenever
the Democrats attempt stonewalling such a
scheme as the Callide railway bill. But when the
Pliilpocracy shuts up Parliament seven months of
the year and adjourns a big part of the other five,
never a word of complaint comes from the
Courier and all its country kin.
A BLOKE.
1ST MOOCHABOUT : “ Who's this ’ere Common-
wealth Bill I hears all the talk about? ”
2ND. DO : “ I dunno; but I suppose it must be
one of them wounded blokes back from the
war.”
South Australia has been trying to pass a
Boilers Inspection Bill for something like 20
years, but the Leg. Council Tories, most of whom
own businesses, have blocked the measure. Re-
cently an ancient, up-country boiler exploded, and
half the township is still in the air. Following
week a septuagenarian boiler somewhere else
struck work and killed an engineer. Parliament
is now again thinking it over.
Five members of the Lyne Govt.—Lyne,
Wise, See, O’Sullivan, and Crick —will be candi-
dates for the Federal Parliament. Lyne pro-
mises to go for the Hume division, O’Sullivan will
be a candidate for the Senate, Crick will contest
the Bathurst-Blayney-Cowra electorate, Wise will
look to the metropolitan railway-line suburbs for
a seat, and See will go for the Grafton side. Three
of them may find Federal foothold.
Premier Lyne, who seeks to represent the
Border electorate (including Albury) in the
Federal Parliament, is really in a tight place. If
he is not solid on Albury as the Federal capital
then he must look elsewhere for a seat, and if he
is sound re Albury’s ambition, then he must look
for no quarter from the fierce jealousy of pretty
well all the rest of N.S.W.—and Lyne cannot
afford to make himself an unpopular provincial
politician.
S.M. Herald, arguing on Wise’s Arbitra-
tion Bill, sympathises with the 280,000 N.S.W.
workers who will be dragooned into union by
the 20,000 trades-unionists. First, how does the
Herald make up this 280,000? Second, does not
everyone know that non-union workers are, as a
body, the most down-trodden and impoverished
in civilisation ? A rise of even a shilling a week
will be better for them than oceans of Herald
sympathy.
How the latest N.S.W. Govt, fake came
about is clear. The Freetrade pressmen appointed
assistant paymasters (at £1 a day) to China con-
tingent looked after Waverley and Rand wick dis-
tricts for their respective papers. A certain very
influential politician lives at-Randwick. The two
reporters were not unfriendly and worked-in with
each other. One conceived the notion, suggested
it to t’other, both set influence going, and hey
presto ! the thing was done.
The bond of affection between Works
Minister O’Sullivan and the N.S.W. Railway
Commissioners grows beautifully less. One after
another The O’Sullivan’s railway proposals form
the subject of withering reports furnished by the
Commissioners whereupon the Minister dumps
the reports down before the House, says “ I don’t
believe a bit of it,” and passes the business on to
the Works Committee. The O’S. alone knows
why, in the circs., he troubles the Commissioners
at all.
S.A. Leg. Council somewhat unbent last
week and passed the second reading of the Holder
Govt.’s Household Suffrage Bill by a majority of
seven. Hardshell Tories like Stirling and E. T.
Smith actually voted with the Govt. The success
is only looked upon by Liberals as temporary, for
the Tory majority in the Council intends to
mutilate the measure in committee, and the main
proposal in the Bill, the conferring of the suffrage
upon occupiers of houses of two rooms and up-
wards, lias no chance of being carried in the
Council as at present constituted.
South Australia has decided to abandon
her communistic village-settlements —there are
seven of them—on the Murray, and no further
advances are to be made after 31st December,
1900. In future the 513 settlers, of whom 418 are
women and children, will be left to their own de-
vices. These settlements have received in State
advances, £85,538, of which but £9490 has been
repaid, and a State expert values the existing im-
provements at £B2OO. The settlements were
established by dreamer Cockburn some 10 years
ago. They might have been a success had compe-
tent experts been placed in charge at the outset.
Originally there were 13, but six of them quickly
expired, and the balance are practically in-
solvent. S.A. Govt, now proposes to apportion
the land amongst the 95 adult settlers, who, one
and all, announce that they are very tired of com-
munism.
Tasmanian Lands Minister Mulcahy is in
some trouble. Land settlement in Tassy
has hitherto been conducted in a happy,
indefinite sort of fashion. Some people
buy off the State. Others just go and
squat in little crowds on State lands, and
when settlement comes their way they
say they didn’t know any better, and ask
if it is a fair thing to turn a man out of
the home he has himself created.. As
a rule Tasmanian Lands Ministers simply
left these jumpers in possession on a sort
of indefinite title. It was the easiest
way out of a difficulty. But Mulcahy,
with a reckless desire for order and
system, is trying to suppress this, and
has been putting a lot of illegal State
tenants out on the cold hill-side. And
by ill-luck it was raining when Mul-
eahy’s agents started in to conserve the
interests of the State. The Minister
Erobably gave the order in dry weather,
ut the rain came on afterwards—and a
Minister who throws anybody’s bed and
infant out into the rain, however good his
motives, is bound to be disliked.
N.S.W. Defence Dept., learning
something from the Boer, has adopted
a suggestion which lately came over
from the squattages yound Broken Hill
that rifles and ammunition should be
handed round to small companies of station-hands
and others asking for them on rifle-club terms.
At first sight it would seem necessary to stipulate
that jackeroos and others should not have their
kangaroo-shooting at part-Govt. expense; but,
after all, it is better practice to shoot at
jumping kangaroos or running turkeys than at
motionless targets. The Boers didn’t learn their
marksmanship at targets, anyway.
The first direct vote of censure which has
been moved in the Westralian Assembly since
Responsible Govt, began 10 years ago, hit Bigjohn
in the eye when Parliament met the other day.
It was moved in both Houses simultaneously, and
the old members received it in much the same
spirit as the American Indians did the first
cannon-ball that struck their previously-quiet
continent.
Queensland Premier Philp says that it is
necessary to hand over future railway construc-
tion work to syndicates because the country can’t
borrow any more money just now. dust by way
of something fresh, how would it be for the help-
less Philp to put on an income-tax and construct
railways in a small patient way with the proceeds ?
It is quite as cheap to pay for railway construc-
tion now as to borrow the money and pay the
interest for ever afterwards. One is taking the
cow by the horns and the other by the tail, but
the difference is only the length of the cow
after all.
One William A. Byrne suggests that as a
delicate compliment to the Liberal statesmen at
’Orae who “rendered signal and valuable assist-
ance to our brilliant delegate ” in securing the
passage of the Bill through the Imperial Parlia-
ment the N.S.W. Federal electorates be named
after them. William selects the following in a
communication to S.M. Herald as the most suit-
able : Gladstone, Salisbury, Parnell, Carrington,
Jersey, Morley, and Bannerman. Of the lot,
Parnell was, perhaps, the most useful to Toby
Barton, because he has been dead the longest.
And picture the scorn of the averagely-informed
Cornstalk youth at perpetuating the name of Ban-
nerman and ignoring those of the only Giffen and
Charlie Turner !
The Queensland Government’s anxiety to
suddenly job away a whole lot of land-grant
railway concessions, has a sinister look. By next
year the Federal Parliament will probably be
ready to buy up the railways and undertake the
work of railway construction itself. Even if the
concessions are granted now, syndicates move very
slowly (it has taken the Tas. Great Western
syndicate five years to start 20 men digging a
small hole), and the Federal Parliament, if left to
itself, would probably get a real start at the
Normanton-Cloncurry railway and other really
important lines long before the syndicates would
have the first pick in the ground. Most of the
syndicates'are probably only rushing for con-
cessions in the hope that the Federal Parliament
will buy them out, and'the policy of thus
systematically heaping up embarrassments for the
Commonwealth, is stinkful, treacherous, and
disgraceful.

BRISBANE AND NEWCASTLE.
The Colonial Office Blue-book on Aus-
tralian Federation shows that Chief-Justice
Lieut.-Gov. Madden made a protest against being
asked by Chamberlain to send per cable newspaper
opinions re the Clause 74 controversy. Madden,
m his cabled reply, said he would have preferred
to express no opinion on the subject. This was
evidently the right attitude, and does the Chief-
Justice credit. The opinions he cabled were those
of the Age, Argus, Australasian and Leader—
as though these were the only organs of opinion in
Victoria, when indeed they merely represent the
views of two wealthy newspaper-proprietaries,
both of which studiously boycott all opinion that
does not suit themselves.
The powerful civil service vote will have
great weight in the N.S.W. Federal elections,
and the successful candidates for both East and
West Sydney (Toorawal and Warrang) will have
to study it. Ex-Premier Reid, who has lost the
confidence of the Service, moved ah amendment
on the Lyne Harbor Trust Bill in committee, that
in appointing employes to the Trust preference
should be given to those already employed by
Govt. He gave no reasons, but forced a division
without discussion. Of course his motion was
lost, but Reid’s object was gained. He is the real
friend of the Service, he will say, quoting this
incident. But N.S.W. Public Service has not yet
forgotten or forgiven 1895.
The personal nature of the disputes of
Lyne and Wise v. Reid are making things very
unpleasant for the higher public servants in
Sydney. In the wrangle as to whether the ex-
Premier did or did not muddle up his finances
to the extent of a few odd millions, each side
confidently appeals to one or another Govern-
ment officer. The Lyne crowd appeals con-
fidently to one Martin, who is now Lyne’s
official authority on finance. Mr. Martin was
under T. A. Coghlan in the Statist’s office before
going over to the Treasury, where he is right-
nand man to the Minister. In the recent
inquiry over the Public Accounts, Martin’s
figures were pro-Lyne down to the last penny,
though he was only giving public figures, the
same figures any other public officer would have
had to produce. Ex-Premier Reid roundly
states that Martin is simply anti-Reid and
juggled the figures accordingly, while if the
Commission had called Coghlan, he says, the
thing would have worked out differently. An-
other grievance of his was that the Commission
didn’t call Mr. George Brodie, the deputy-
Auditor-General in Reid’s time; the evidence
Brodie should have given was entrusted to one
Pitt —one of the Treasury Inspectors—a subordi-
nate of Mr. Brodie’s. Again, Reid makes out that
Pitt is pro-Lyne, while Brodie would have given
evidence in support of the Reid figures. When
the Premier asked for a report from the head of
the Charities Dept, on the inner workings of the
Metrop. Charities’ Association, the ex-Premier
declared that Mr. Hanson, acting under orders,
had attempted to besmirch his (Reid’s) public
acts. He denounced Mr. Hanson at a public
meeting as unfit to discharge the duties of his
office, because he used them for the political pur-
poses of the party in power. The public servants
naturally don’t at all relish this sort of thing.
They care little enough who is the upper dog for
the moment, and object to even appear in the
ructions between the various contending parties.
They know well enough that the balance of power
will one day change, and that the outs will
be in, so they don’t like it. It smacks too
much of the American political system, as de-
picted in the English press.
On a matter of much Australian historical
interest, “ Waranee ” writes:
In referring to the death of the late Father Dunne, of
Albury, The Bulletin stated that it was at his house that
the late Peter Lalor sought refuge after the defeat of the
Eureka stockaders. This is incorrect. The circumstances,
as related by Mrs. J. P. Kelly, of St. Kilda, Melbourne,
who is still living, are as follows : Late one evening Lalqr
arrived in Geelong, concealed under some loose straw in
a dray. The cart was driven to a cottage occupied bv
Miss Dunne (Lalor’s betrothed) and Mrs. Kelly (then Miss
M. T. O’Connor), both teachers at St. Mary’s Catholic
school, Geelong. _ Here the fugitive remained for about
three weeks, during which time Miss Dunne dressed his
terrible wound (in the arm), being afraid to call in a
doctor. When, however, Father Dunne came to know of
the matter he was very much annoyed, and said Lalor
would have to be removed. Miss Dunne then arranged
with a Geelong doctor to take the patient to his private
house, where he soon became convalescent. Mrs. Kelly,
who is the only survivor of those concerned, wishes it to
be known that Father Dunne in no way countenanced the
hiding of Lalor. Father Dunne was then in receipt of a
Govt, pension, and it would not have been honorable of
him to hide Lalor, who was technically a “ rebel,” and for
whose capture had been offered a reward of £5OO.
