It costs N. S. W. Govt. £26 16s. a year to
keep the average prisoner in Biloela goal —the
old island prison in the harbor—as against £156
12s. to keep him in Wilcannia gaol. The labor
cost in the one case is £l3 165.; in the other,
£llB 2s. At Darlinghurst, where more are con-
fined than anywhere else, the cost totals £43 18s. ;
in 1897, though, it got down below £37. Of the
country gaols the least costly to the province is
that at Goulburn. The cost at Berrima has gone
up during the last two or three years from £6l to
£7l. The extension of the cellular system is a
strong factor in raising the cost.
Lord Cecil Manners, if you please, writing
to the—haw!— Morning Post from Pretoria,
where he was a nominal prisoner awhile, stated
that he “tried to look as much like a Boer as
possible” in a “quiet suit of grey flannel,”and
had once (at Bloemfontein) been positively mis-
taken for—haw !—one of them by an intelligent—
ah !—policeman. Which is rather rough on the
early stories of Boer bestiality. Lord Cecil must
be nearly related to that other Lord Mannahs
who immortalised himself in the verse—“ Let laws
and learning, arts and commerce die, But leave us
still our old Nobility. ”
That chain-letter curse. Since Jan., 1897,
no less than 83,000 insufficiently-stamped letters
have been returned to Sydney G.P.O. by Miss
Griffin, of Kogarah.
S. M. Herald is setting its Melbourne
agency in order. Large modifications in the Syd-
ney and Melbourne work will be effected within
a few months, so that the Federal business, may
move smoothly. A bright speculator has tried to
get the Sydney dailies—if not the four, at least two
of them—on a string to work the reports from
Melbourne as the cables are now worked from
London. He shows yards of figures in justifica-
tion. A proposal of another kind emanates from
Melbourne. Both Argus and Age are willing for
due consideration, to wire duplicates of their own
reports over-night and thus obviate the need of a
Sydney staff at headquarters.

A Melbourne paper has made the alarm-
ing discovery that there are Boer spies at this
moment in Australia. Now, will any calm and
dispassionate person try to figure out exactly what
information there is in Australia which would be
of one penn’orth of value to any Boer ? r l here
are no more Contingents leaving here, and
Roberts’ secret plans and the British Govern-
ment’s intentions are not to be found lying loose
on the public-house counters in Bourke-street. A
Boer agent here would be about as much good as a
Bulgarian spy on the upper waters of the Amazon.
It doubtless feeds the vanity of a certain military
clique to picture this place as haunted by secret
agents; it seems to set Australia on a par with
great European Powers, and to suggest mystery
and diplomacy and intrigue. But, as a matter of
fact, a Boer agent might listen till his ear dropped
off at all the official keyholes in Australia, and
gain no information worth mentioning, for there
isn’t any. This country doesn’t even know where
its own Contingents are half the time.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Once Rosehill was known as the “Punters’
Paradise,” but happenings at last Saturday’s and several
Erior meetings would suggest “ Mo.’s Harvest” as more
tting. Trouble began with Granville Stakes, when a 20
to 1 chance —lodine—(only nibbled at) got home in front
of the fast-finishing Australian Colors, which divided first-
favoritism with Maranoa. Steeplechase went to the big-
gest outsider (Satan II.) in a field of four only. Parra-
matta Handicap was won by Mt. Morgan W. R. Hall’s
Reviver (a 10 to 1 chance) who beat the first favorite—
Bob—in a hot finish and after a race that makes his (Re-
viver’s) Epsom prospects look rosy. Only first favorite to
get home throughout the day was Kelso’s Martini-Henry
—Vain Glory gelding Dandy in the Spring Stakes, run
under modified weight-for-age conditions. He fairly
romped away in the straight from a more than ordinarily
good field and a much better one than has been seen on a
course other than Randwick for many a day. Dandy’s
present form is such that one could back nothing else for
next Saturday’s big mile event with any confidence.
Rickshaw (6 to 1 against) just little more than pipped
first favorite (Glenore) in Aug. Handicap after a gruelling
finish. A rank outsider (Clydon) downed heavily-backed
Loch Olieveden (7 to 4 against) by a head through latter
losing her place at one Stage of the race. Many Derby
candidates were out. Yarrabee ran third in Granville
Stakes; Haulette, Hautesse, Wigelmar, Philibeg and
Schimmel figured in the Spring Stakes. Hautesse was
second to Dandy and therefore beat all others—carrying
a 101 b. penalty. Haulette, also humping an extra burden,
was fourth, while Schimmel and Philibeg shaped well.
* * * •
The disappointing Clarion, by Carbine—Clare,
has been leased to Mr. H. C. White for stud purposes. As
a 3-year-old, Clarion took the fancy of many good judges,
who expected him to develop into a Cup-horse, but the
son of Carbine lacked dash. A beautiful galloper, but a
comparatively slow-going machine.
*****
W. P. Crick, M.L.A. recently got heavily on
to the Committee of the Australian Jockey Club for
alleged hole-and-corner work. In the election of a fresh
committee Crick was badly left, polling less than half the
number of votes recorded for the lowest successful can-
didate ; but he can console himself with the reflection
that, as the result of such election was practically a cut-
and-dried affair, the formal balloting was little short of a
farce. The A. J.C. committee is elected, to all intents and
purposes, by the Union and Australian clubs, and most of
the men chosen are of extremely high personal repute.
But some of them are very slow, and it would do no harm
to have an aggressive outsider like Crick as a member, to
liven things up occasionally.
* * • •
Record Reign, one of Maoriland’s best handicap
flat-racers, lately won Maiden Hurdles and then the N.Z.
Grand National, carrying in latter 12,12 (inc. penalty).
Seven starters were beaten off; the time (2 miles),
3.46 3-sths, was 6sec. inside record for the race. On last
day of meeting winner was weighted at 13.13—a grand-
stand impost—but declined. The minimum was 9st. He
is engaged in N.Z. Cup at 8.11.
- * * .* *
Mr. J. A. Buckland’s three-year-old trotter,
The Heir, full brother to champion Fritz, won the classic
Juvenile Stakes of £2OO at Canterbury, (M.L.). Trotting
Club’s meeting by a head. The biggest field—3o—that
has ever faced a starter in a M.L. trotting event—went
out for the Bracelet at same meeting, winner paying
£34 10s. Another good div., £3O was returned during the
day.
* * * *
Reliance, who has won the Coolgardie Cup
(worth 500 sovs.) and the Boulder Cup, was bred by W. R.
Wilson, but did so wretchedly that that first-rate judge
of live stock, C. N. Kidman, was able to buy him for 29 sovs.
Mr. Kidman handed him over to Bolger to train, giving
Bolger a half-share in him. As betting was heavy over
both events, and as the horse was at one time at 8 to 1,
the stable must have made a “ punch.” Kidman was
pretty well going out of racing (he has his hands full of
the Westralian “meat-ring” business, being one of the
big shippers of live stock for the goldfields); but this has
given him a taste for more.
* * * *
Barring The Grafter and old Merman, Aus-
tralian horses have been performing poorly at ’Ome, the
latest failure being the quondam N.S.W. crack 3-year-old
Australian who ran “ absolutely ” in a race at the fashion-
able Lingfield Park.
* * * *
Mr. M. Power, who died suddenly on Saturday
morning last from the rupture of a cranial blood-vessel,
was one of Sydney’s best-known horsey men. Manager
of Mr. Sam. Hordern’s horse department, both racing and
utility, no hor ie-sale seemed complete without “Mick”
Power at the ringside. Some years ago he went to Eng-
land under commission from Mr. Hordern to purchase
blood stock, when he bought Haut Brion and Gigue, both
of the celebrated St. Simon blood, and several mares to
this country. Mr. Power was a man of exceptionally
fine physique, and did not look his 47 years.
Shrewd old Rooty Hill (N.S.W.) courser
“ Sam ” Bladon gathered in Sydney C.C.’s Bracelet Stakes
at Belmore last week with Beeline, his third crack per-
former in evidence this season. The veteran has now
scooped all the plums of the year in N.S.W., besides a few
in Victoria. Beeline is a red bitch by Leopold out of
Besant, and a worthy kennel mate to Braddon’s Blot and
Booty, which is saying a heap.
* * * •
Bill Beach made another of his periodical
swoops upon N.S.W. Gun Club, t’other day, and grassed
18—all with the first barrel. When he told that on that
day 16 years before he took the World’s Championship
from Ned Hanlan on the Parramatta, there was great
cheering and many congratulatious. Bill is as popular as
ever everywhere, and, unlike most champions who drop
out of the game, still owns and deserves hosts of friends.
* * * *
All-black pug match—lke Stewart v. Starlight—
at Golden Gate Hall (Sydney), Saturday, ended unsatis-
factorily. Starlight wasn’t “taking any” of the usual
referee—and clever boxer Wally Weekes, utterly devoid
of experience in that capacity, was chosen to fill the
office. Stewart, prompted by his wily seconds (Jim
Barron and Abe Willis) made the pace a cracker from the
jump, but as the mature “Star” knows every trick of
the trade, Ike’s swoop didn’t have the devastating
effects intended. In the second term the cunning “ Star.”
gave Stewart the shoulder as he bore down with all sail set,
and in various other ways—permissible but hardy legiti-
mate- -shook the local black from head to heel. Round
three had hardly begun when the fight was over. They
were breaking away from a clinch when “ Star. ” —quick
as a flash—got a weighty right home on the jaw. An
appeal (without apparent provocation) from Stewart’s
corner caused the referee to declare Ike the winner on
“ a foul.” There was no foul—’twas as fair a thing as
could be. The responsible official’s decision is only-
explainable by the probability that he “ lost his head.”
He went further, subsequently, and declared all bets off.
Why? If the verdict was good what valid reason could
there be for such a proceeding ?
* * * • »
“ Old-Timer ” : Saw Stewart-Starlight fight,
and thought latter most unfortunate in being adjudged
loser. There was no foul at all; both Starlight’s hands
were free, and that being so it didn’t matter how close
Stewart might have been, or even if he were clinging;
but present-day referees don’t know the rules, and the
one who acted on this occasion, though as straight as a
die, had had no experience. Subsequent explanation from
him evidenced that he thought combatants should be
half-a-yard away from each other before either could
strike." This shows the idea of M.Q. rules some people
have. Frequent cries of “Break fair!” from referees
and manner in which fighters go back with arms extended
are amusing. There’s no such thing as “ break fair”—it’s
simply “ break,” and either may land a punch instanter
provided he is not holding his opponent. Starlight had
the better of the deal as far as matters went, and looked
a sure winner. One or other would certainly have been
knocked out before the tenth round, judging by the pace
set.
• * • *
Starlight in the ring is indeed a swell. Entering
the roped*square he distributes his bows with all the
dignity of a king, and when that long “ Newmarket ” coat
is peeled off the effect is dazzling. Pale blue knickers,
with black-lace front and back, are surmounted by a
broad black belt. The boots are a dream in fighting foot-
wear, and extend well up the leg. Hair parted down the
middle with scrupulous exactness tops the lot. Such a
“ Gussie ’’-looking pug hasn’t shaped in Sydney for many
a day. The crowd, unused to so fetching a spectacle,
stared flabbergasted, and then roared with laughter,
especially while the bowing went on.
* * * *
Referee of a Sydney fighting-club was introduc-
ing two darkies (prospective combatants) to the crowd
t’other night. “ Gen’l’men,” he said, “these are two
colored gladiators who are— ” One of them kicked here;
he was a gentleman though his skin might be of inky
hue, and “ he wasn’t goin’ to stan’ there and be called an
alligator by any man.” Sustained roars of laughter pre-
vented an explanation being made and proceedings pro-
ceeded.
• • • •
“Rats”: See second Cribb-Higgins fight in
Sydney this Saturday night is being billed “ for light-
weight championship of ’Stralia.” Why, neither has the
slightest claim to fight for the title. Higgins tried to get
it from holder (Peter Murphy) once in Brisbane, but failed
badly. Cribb has never yet fought at the weight (10st.),
and ’tis doubtful whether he could do so. Anyhow, why
are they fighting again ? Higgins threw up the sponge
before, and would have done so two rounds previously
bub for ’suasion from his trainer.
* • • •
Monday’s cables told of another big win for the
lanky M.L. freak bruiser, Fitzsimmons, who knocked out
that acknowledged toughest mass of brawn in the busi-
ness, Sailor Sharkey, before the second round was
through. Sharkey has been up against all the Yankee
heavy-weights, and never before experienced the soothing
influence of a punch on the point or a jab on the mark.
Fact of freckle-fisted Fitz. spread-eagling the hurricane
Sailor for at least lOsecs. stamps him as the stillest
puncher of ’em all. Bob’s claims for another cut at
champion-of-the-world Jeffries can’t be ignored now.
When latter and Sharkey fought they put up a gruelling,
stoushing go of the severest character for 25 rounds, and
then thousands argued that it should have been Sharkey’s
fight or a draw. Had they stopped at the 20th the
referee couldn’t have got out of giving the verdict to
Sharkey. On this form, ’tis any odds that Fitz. will own
, the world’s supremacy again if the Californian ex-boiler-
maker toes the scratch. Fitz. and Sharkey met in ’Frisco
four years ago. Fitz. knocked chunks off the Sailor, and
in the Bth round had him dead to the world, when gun-
fighter Wyatt Earp, who was referee, declared that Fitz.
had hit low and fouled, and gave the fight to Sharkey
amid hisses and groans from the immense crowd looking
on. Sharkey has had more rough treatment during his
brief career than falls to the lot of most pugs in a lifetime,
notably when the Akron giant, Ruhlin, punched him to a
jelly recently. Hence writer wouldn’t be surprised to
hear that, finding Fitz. much too good for him, Sharkey
thought discretion the better part of valor, and went
“ out ” early so as to avoid another mangling.
*****
“ Old Timer ” : Was looking forward to a record
in poundage had Akron giant Ruhlin and Los Angeles
giant Jeffries come together for world’s championship,
but Fitzsimmons’ subjugation of former puts that out of
the question—for a term, anyhow. Dutchman Harry
Placke (6.5) and Q. giant Dick Barker (6.7) were the
tallest men ever seen in the Australian ring together.
P.R. history tells of some huge bruisers, notably Free-
man, the American giant, who whipped the “ Tipton
Slasher.” Ned O’Baldwin was a big lot when he shaped
up to that fine sample of an English yeoman, Andrew
Marsden, at Nottingham. But, though the Irish giant
stood 6.54, he belonged to the lathy or “ gintale ” section.
Gully and Gregson were much over 6ft. and stout in pro-
portion. Isaac Perrins (a worker in iron) was a monster
of muscle, strength and tissue. Writer remembers read-
ing that Johnson (a corn-porter who fought Perrins)
carried two sacks of corn (5001 b.) every journey for some
time so that the wife of a sick mate shouldn’t lose her
housekeeping money.
* • * *
Erstwhile W.A. pug. Tommy Cox, who beat
“ The Kaffir ” and others but wasn’t considered anything
here, found his way to Honolulu and thence to ’Frisco
with an ex-member of M'Adoo’s minstrel troupe as
manager. Fairy tales were told about Cox’s feats in this
section, and Yankee scribes wrote him up as an “ Austra-
lian Terry M‘Govern,” but there was a big slump in
Tommy’s stock when he met one Cole at ’Frisco last
month. Cox won on points after 20 rounds, but fell so
far short of expectations that judges opine he’ll go no
farther than his next battle —which may not come soon, as
there’s no market in Yankeedom for third or fourth-rate
bruisers.
* * * *
Late files record that that tall, good-looking,
and exceptionally clever but weak-hearted N.S.W. pug.—
Steve O'Donnell —has had what may prove his last show
in the States. Hibernian Peter Maher knocked him out
at Ne w York (July 6) inside 2mins. Source of information
says Steve had his nose broken, was floored three times,
and finally beaten helpless. Why these two men were
again brought together is hard to fathom. Five years ago
Maher settled the 'Stralian in a punch. A year later the
big Irishman came up atop once more ere the expira-
tion of a minute. In ’97 they boxed a six rounds draw
but a month further on Steve only lasted two rounds in
opposition to same man. O'Donnell was a smart electro-
typer and could have done well in Sydney but for the
seductive influence of those fabulous Yankee purses—
mostly mythical, as a fighter’s remuneration ’cross Che
Pacific depends wholly upon how the “ gate ” pans out
and then he only gets the leavings after the promoter has
closed on 50 per cent, and the manager and (often) four
or five others have had their dips into the pile. London
“ National ” Sporting Club purses don’t strike the ear as
temptingly as American rewards, but they’re much more
substantial as a rule.

Harry Graham, chosen 13th man in the ’93 Aus-
tralian XI., and who won the batting average of that tour,
has just returned from Mt. William gold-rush, near
Ararat, after an unsuccessful innings amongst the fos-
sickers.
* * * •
The British amateur cricketer can bat, but he
can’t bowl. In match, Gentlemen v. Players, finished at
Lords, July 18, former made 297 and 339. Players, having
put up only 136 in first innings, were left with 501 to
make, and they accomplished the task with 8 wickets
down, Brown making 163 and Tom Hayward 111. For the
amateurs, R. E. Foster, considered the most dashing bats-
man in England this season, made the double century.
Foster has been running second in the batting averages,
and is now probably just about at the top.
* * * *
Fred. Spofforth, one-time demon bowler, does
not seem to have altered much as old Sydneyites recollect
him. When the last Australian Eleven were in England
they received more than one letter from “ Spoff,” saying
that he felt as well as ever he did, and suggesting that
they throw out one of their bowlers and take him on
tour. How like the “ Demon ! ”
* * * *
S.A. Lacrosse Association has decided to do
nothing at present re importing a Canadian team next
winter, and as Adelaide is practically the headquarters of
the game, this is a decided damper. Generally under-
stood that Melb. Cricket Club contemplate offering to
run the whole show, and that is about the only chance of
the tour coming off, unless some one puts up the £lOOO
necessary to begin with.
* * * *
Last round of first-grade matches in Sydney
local football competitions was contested Saturday.
Glebe and ’Varsity led by 10 to 5, but Glebe came with a
full head of steam, and compiled 15 without a break.
Match topped anything seen this season for brilliancy,
and probably equalled the best inter-club game ever
played before. Western Suburbs had Balmain in trouble
for the first lOrnin., but subsequently were “ all out ” to
prevent their opponents scorings—never once attempting
the aggressive themselves. A draw resulted. Meeting
between S. Sydney and Newtown supplied comparatively
poor football; Souths won by sto 3. There was a lot of
go in E. Suburbs —N. Sydney contest, but neither side
had its best team. Former came out ahead —9 to nil.
* * 4 *
Sydney club football season closed Saturday
with Glebe on top in all three grades—second and third
easily. Senior team has played 14 matches, won 11, lost 2,
drawn 1, and scored 156 points to 67 notched against
them. ’Varsity comes next with 8 won, 5 lost, and 1
drawn. Newton is last on the list—4 won and 10 lost.
» * * *
Vic. premiership football got a severe shock
last week, when Geelong club very nearly got itself fired
out of the league. When the sleepy tovvnship played
“ Sowth” Melb.—on latter’s ground—early in the season,
the latter charged entrance fees to certain lady-sup-
porters, club members. Geelong protested, and, the
protest being unheeded, retaliated by charging admission
to all and sundry, save its own club members, when the
return match was played. Sowth appealed to the league,
which ordered the Geelongers to return the money—£6 Is.
They declined, and were promptly fined £2O in addition, to
be paid inside three days; in default, expulsion from league
for rest of season. The three days passed, also a fourth
and a fifth ; then the Seasiders, realising that they were
cutting off their noses to spite their faces, ate humble pie
and paid up; and peace and goodwill reigned once more.
Also, the gate the following Saturday, when they met
Melbourne, was much bigger than usual. If the league is
to be anything more than a dummy, like the V.A.G., it
must, of course, enforce its decisions.
* * * •
A constable had to draw his revolver to deal
with a Camberwell (Vic.) football crowd the other Satur-
day. The crowd wanted to eat the umpire.
* * • *
Revival of Botany pedestrian Handicaps, at old
Sir Joseph Banks Hotel, Friday, was a complete success.
Entries were good, and canie from all parts. Close on
2000 paid at the gates. Betting was lively on every heat.
Jones (aged 20), from Narandera (on 16yds.), carried most
money, and, before the first heat came on, nothing better
than 5 to 2 could be had, He got home in final, with
Griffiths (17yds.) second, and Vic. G. B. Bowser (13yds.)
third. Time, 11 7-10 sec. Jones is built for the game,
and promises well; albeit, he shaped as if he’d been in
the biz. before. A good sum of money could have been
won. Writer heard that a well-known “ shop ” Mo.
worked a fair commission on the rural flyer.
* • * *
At the recent British championships meeting
(up to then the most important meet on record of World’s
amateur athletes), there were 179 entries and 116 faced
the flag. Holders of five British premierships (Welch,
Newburn, Stokes, Paget-Tomlinson and Pritchard) failed
to appear. Of eight other champions only Tysoe and
Sturgess came out atop. Yankees scored eight firsts,
British Empire, fivd‘. England had 8J seconds and
America 4£. Kraenzlein’s 15 2-5 beat the world’s 120yds,
hurdles (on grass) record. Flanagan’s hammer-throw
wiped-out a British best. The English fastest (10 secs.)
for 100yds. was equalled and the figures for previous
championship performances at pole-jumping and weight-
putting excelled.
• • * •
His performances—in British Championships
and Olympian Games at Paris—indicate that, at most,
Australian champion sprinter Rowley is only a yard or
two worse than the best Yankee article over 100yds. In
the old country’s supremacy, he was a close fourth to
’Muricans Duffy, Jarvis, and Tewkesbury, as named;
then, in the 100 metres event of Olympian Games, July
14, Jarvis won 2ft. ahead of Tewkesbury, with Rowley
almost abreast, third. Later, Rowley was second to an-
other Yankee top-notcher (Kraenzlein) in the 50 metres
contest. Rowley had the bad luck to be up against the
strongest lot America ever produced ; but for their pres-
ence he would have made a clean sweep of the board.
• * » *
Crack swimmer Charlie Hellings succumbed to
pneumonia in Sydney Hospital, Saturday. He was 29,
and had been swimming almost every season since ’B2,
when that N.S.W. pioneer swimming organisation, the
old P.J. Club, flourished. Charlie won championships in
his time and appropriated many other important events.
Last season he was a competitor in several races, and was
tipped as a “good thing” for the big “ Half-Mile,” but
lack of proper “fix,” consequent upon long hours and
hard daily toil, told its tale. He affected the old single
over-arm style, which, in writer’s opinion, is by far the
best, though the ‘ ‘ Trudgeon,” or double over-arm method,
finds most favor now. Percy Cavill, Joey Nuttall (cham-
pion of the World), Tyers and Jarvis (present amateur
champion of England) all adopt the single over-arm
means of propulsion. Charlie Hellings was a brother of
sailing enthusiast and popular comic singer George
Hellings, who died some months back, also Jack Hellings
(the world-famous sprint swimmer, now in England),
Dick Hellings (the noted crack coxswain), and Harry
Hellings (until recently hon. handicapper to the N.S.W.
Amateur Swimming Association).
* * * *
Cable that ’Stralian champion swimmer Lane
had, at Paris Olympian Games, won 200 metres supremacy
in 2m. 25J5., and 200 metres obstacle race in 2m. 385., ex-
plains why no news has come regarding him for some
time; he probably laid himself out for these events. Fast
times would indicate that they were put up with a cur-
rent—probably on the Seine. So far, Lane has only com-
peted once in England this season, and this when Derby-
shire beat him by a yard in 150 yards after a hot and very
fast race.
**. * *
’Ome files give lengthy accounts of English
Long-distance Swimming Championship on the Thames
(’tween Kew and Putney) last month. Distance is esti-
mated at 5 miles and 60 yards. Winner (Jarvis) finished
300 yards in front of second man in lh. 4m. 17s. Previous
best (2.18 slower) stood in Australian Percy Cavill’s name,
and was accomplished three years ago. Race is swum
with the current, hence the apparently wonderful time.
Does not necessarily follow that there was greater merit
in Jarvis’s feat than in Cavill’s, for the Englishman might
have had more favourable conditions. Swimming time-
tests are only thoroughly reliable in water unaffected by
tidal or other influences.
• • • *
A Cycle Paths Bill, to be presented in M.L.
Parliament, gives cyclists in any district power to ask
Govt., by a majority of 2 to 1, to proclaim the district a
cycle district, so that every cyclist may be taxed ss. a
year to keep the roads in order, while the local authority
will have power to set apart portions of a road for the
exclusive use of cyclists, and arrange with cycle-boards
for laying down cycle-paths.
* * * *
Porta, the Italian cyclist, a favorite on so many
Aus. tracks a few years ago, is still in the West. He sued
a broker lately for £lOO, a disputed amount in connection
with a pretty’ big share transaction. But Maximiliano
went under.
• -* * *
In the John Roberts v. Frank Smith match at
Melb. Athenaeum Hall the champion’s “ form ” was
seemingly affected by Smith’s failure to play up to. him.
Conceding 6,000 in 14,000, Roberts found himself with a
lot the best of the handicap at the end of the second day,
and on the third afternoon the game dragged woefully,
Smith’s three best breaks being 71, 46, and 29. In the
evening, when Waxelleucy Madden and co. came along to
see what he was capable of, Roberts showed them breaks
of 219, 181 (twice), 111, and an unfinished 53, whilst
“ pace-maker” Smith was going weaker than ever, so the
old man filled in the spare half-hour with some fancy
strokes. It takes two players to make an interesting ex-
hibition-match. Smith, owing to an attack of liver, or
want of acquaintance with the bonzoline balls, shapea
like an amateur at times when he should have been put-
ting in his best work.
• * * •
“Baulk Line”: “Frank Smith, champion of
Victoria,” was a great disappointment to anyone who saw
him for the first time at Melb. Athenaeum Hall last week.
It was not so much his poor work against Roberts that
disappointed us, as his general appearance of weakness.
He isn't the stuff of which first-class billiard players are
made, though, doubtless, the dapper little fellow looks
big enough when dealing with players of his own class.
Roberts, on the other hand, comes as a pleasant surprise
to many people. Present scribe, for one, had expected to
find him in a rapid decline of “ form,” whereas he is prac-
tically the same inimitable artist who toured Australia
nine years ago. The suppression of the “push” stroke
has reduced his rate of scoring, but his delicacy of touch,
promptness, and fine, manly grip of the situation are as
maaked as ever. The match with Smith, of course, was
mere child’s play to the Old Master, and it put no real
strain on his nerve. Now that he is well into the fifties,
and begins to know what it is to feel uncertain of himself
sometimes, Roberts may be better at exhibition play than
when there is a big money-stake depending on his nerve.
All the same, I think he will be just goqd enough to beat
Weiss for that £5OO. The Australian champion is pretty
sure to make a plucky fight of it, and 7000 points in 21,000
is a long start, but, then, Roberts is an old dog who has
travelled harder roads than that.

* * * •
A John Roberts billiard yarn which deserves
to be true. The great John strolled into the billiard
room of an hotel, and a stranger immediately invited him
to a game. “Just as well that you should know my
name,” said John. “It is Roberts.” “ Yaas,’ said the
stranger, “ I’ve heard of the name. Do you play ?
“Well, I’m John Roberts, the world’s champion at bil-
liards. It’s only fair to mention the fact before we start.’
“ Yaas,” said the stranger, “ I guess under those circs. I
can’t give you more than 20 in the 100 ! ”
Champion Roberts was being tricky in the pre-
sence of vice-royalty in Melb. during the run of a local
match, and one of his specialities was to make the ball
bound off the table over the chandelier, and do some-
thing astounding. The ball bounded all right, but fell
amongst the audience, and close search failed to recover
it. “ I never yet missed a pocket with that trick ” was
the champion’s comment.

Govt., which objects to Tatt’s. advts. as demoralising,
allows the pubs up-north to keep open all hours of the
night, Sunday included—gambling going on all the time
like fury.

At Poverty Point.
Manager Lohr boldly advertises with regard to " What
Happened to Jones ” at Sydney Palace, “ Seats may be
booked six weeks in advance." It is a pretty good
manager with a pretty good show who can feel dead sure
about where he will be six weeks hence.
“ One touch of Nature,” &c. During Maggie Moore’s
last tour of M.L. she visited Wanganui and was taken to
witness a tangi over the dead chief Rangi. The womanly
Maggie was so moved by the lamentations of the old
Maori crones that she “ took a hand ” and started weep-
ing also.
Rumour says that Oarlyle Smythe, now in Europe, has
at last persuaded Paderewski to come to Australia. Be-
lieve it when you see the man of the yellow hair and the
long fingers actually flogging the Erard Grand. Paderewski
signed with Smythe to come to Australia several years
ago, but backed out. The Court Circular, by the way,
lately said that Paderewski “had the honor” of playing
before the Queen, whereupon even so Jingo a paper as
the Sphere remarked that the honor was the other way
about.
Talking of the estimated profit of Donald Macdonald’s
lecturing tour, it is only a fair thing to give the business
manager a line. Young Edwin Geach, who is somewhat
of a pupil of the genial (and wily) Lohr, has a sympathetic
press to deal with in Mac.’s case, and he works the oracle
most energetically. On the morning of Mac.’s opening-
day in Brisbane the Courier gave him a column of leading
article and another column of biographical notice.
Remarkable reference by S.M. Herald of Aug. 4 to Nance
O’Neil’s Melb. performance of Fedora: “ The dying scene
was ruined by the act-drop leaving Miss O’Neil’s head and
shoulders in full view of the audience. This and other
catastrophes were directly due to the labors of the
O’Neil-Rankin Co. in the cause of charity.” Query: If
the co. had given one more charitable performance, would
the poor lady’s head have been cut off ?
Rickards’ Adelaide music-hall experiment is turning
out quite a success. Rickards’ enterprise has influenced
the sleepy Royal, which will now have to hustle to keep
in line with the King William-street house.
Nat Goodwin has, The Bulletin hears from California,
been distinguishing himself again—playing melodrama
off the stage. He enjoyed himself so thoroughly with
hilarious friends at Los Angeles that the curtain had to
be rung down not only on the play, but on the en-
gagement, while for two days Nat’s life hung in the balance,
and fellow-hilarians trembled for the results of their
escapade. It was probably the biggest advt. he ever had,
but a little too costly for the co.
, George Musgrove has a lease of Sydney Lyceum dating
from December next.
! “ Demisemiquaver ” : Have you noticed all the recent
American music-hall songs, how they run into syncopated
rythins ? The whole of one of Irene Franklin’s songs is
syncopated throughout. This syncopation is, of course,
copied from the negro, of whose music it is characteristic.
It is extraordinary how the coon is coming above the
musical horizon. He is intensely musical. The foremost
of the rising “ English ” composers just now is Coleridge
Taylor, a half-caste West Indian negro. Dvorak, the
great Hungarian composer, went to the U. States and lived
among the negroes to get their rythms into his last and
greatest symphony. The coon has captured the music-
i hall now; and he may yet inherit the seats of the mighty
|in the kingdom of sound. And as to color, he doesn’t
wear gaudy green and red neckerchiefs for nothing.
; Who is going to make the burlesque—“ The Tyranny
of Beers ” ?
Johnnie Sheridan, in his lately-published booklet,
mentions that when he re-visited America in ’92, he was
more or less boycotted by press and public for having
(brought over an English and Australian co. He opened
in New York, and had a poor house for his first night.
“ For thirteen years I have played the Widow O’Brien.
Think of it! I have worn stays for thirteen solid years,”
says Sheridan.
“ Anti-Gammon ” : There’s a lot of stuff in “ Hedda
Gabler ” that seems to be gratuitous suggestiveness, or
1 “ indelicacy,” or whatever the ordinary Goth may choose
| to think. For instance, the probability of Hedda having
i a baby is touched upon three or four times. The old
aunt questions Hedda’s husband in the first act as to
i whether he and his bride have “expectations.” Women
in the audience titter violently at this cheap bit of
realism. It is new, but it doesn’t strike them that it is
novel only because other serious dramatists avoid unneces-
sary domestic details calculated to raise cerulean merri-
ment. Ibsen merely puts into his play carefully. the
catch lines which a Pinero would leave out. The idea
that Hedda’s husband’s aunt W'ants her to increase the
population is of no interest to the audience, seeing that
it has not the least bearing on the plot. In “ The Doll’s
House ” (the only other Ibsen play ever produced in Aus-
tralia), a gloomy elderly gentleman wastes a lot of time
over telling the heroine that he has arranged
to die of an inherited unmentionable disease.
The women in the audience nudge one another, and that’s
1 what Ibsen calculated on when he introduced the dirty
“ stroke of genius,” or whatever his admirers call it. But
to get back to “ Hedda Gabler ” and the everyday works of
nature. In the second act, Mr. Assessor Somebody sug-
gests that the lady will probably be having a baby some
day, to which she replies that “nothing of the sort is
likely to happen.” And again women in the audience
titter violently, whilst men speculate as to where the
hidden meaning of the dramatist is going to reveal itself.
The third act contains no reference to pregnancy, as far
as I can remember ; but in the fourth act the old aunt
returns to the subject, and Hedda herself appears
to give her husband to understand that she is in a “ cer-
tain condition,” whereat he promises himself the pleasure
of bearing the glad tidings to his blessed aunt. And
again women in the audience titter. Soon afterwards
Hedda commits suicide, not because she would probably
produce a baby if she continued to live, but because—
well, for some other reason best known to the author. So
all this “ blue ” talk at last proves irrelevant to the story.
The theatrical retrospector of a Melbourne weekly has
been moved “to try and catalogue from memory” the
doings of the Broughs since they first appeared in
“ lolanthe ”at the Royal. It is never safe to talk from
“ memory ” about things one never knew, or has more
than half-forgotten. Said the retrospector : “ When they
went across the road (from the Royal) to the Opera House
and started a burlesque season with ‘ Silver Guilt,’ Mrs.
Brough, in a black satin knee-breeches suit, of which
I was forcibly reminded in ‘ Lady Ursula,’ appeared as the .
Spider and gave hint of her genius for comedy with
which we are now so admiringly familiar. Mr. Brough
was the Wilfrid Denver, &c., &c. Then came ‘Little
Jack Sheppard,’ &c., &c. . . This burlesque season is
notable as marking the break away of the Broughs from,
the comic-opera stage, and their entrance into the more
serious and artistic spheres of the drama. . . It w-as
just about this time that the Brough and Boucicault
Comedy Co. was formed, and took up its abode in the
new Bijou." The foolishness of talking from a bad
memory ! Fact is that the Broughs,' soon after the
old Firm’s productions of ■“ lolanthe” and “Tambour
Major” at the Royal were incorporated in said Firm’s
new comedy co. which “ The Magistrate ” and
numerous other comedies (“ Glass of Fashion ” was one)
at the Bijou, ihe theatre being at that time sub-let to
Williamson, Garner and Musgrove by Majeroni and Wil-
son. It was in “ The Magistrate ” that both Brough and
Mrs. Brough made their first notable successes as light
comedians in Australia. After the Bijou season, and prior
i;o his going to England with young Boucicault to form a
co. of their own, Brough played a couple of special en-
gagements in “ Falka ” and “ Princess Toto ” at the Opera
House. Those were his last comic-opera appearances.
On returning from England the new firm of B. and B.
opened at the Opera House with Henry Harwood as
“ associate manager.” They started with “ Turned Up,”
other farcical comedies foliow'ed before they got to
that “ Silver Guilt ” burlesque whereof the erring retro-
spector talks so wisely. And, as for Mrs. Brough’s alleged
appearance in black satin knee-beeeches suit, the present
scribe doesn’t remember it at all, and he wasn’t forcibly
reminded of it in “Lady Ursula,” and he can’t under-
stand how anyone with a memory like Retrospector’s
can be forcibly reminded of aught that happened on the
stage 15 years ago. Mrs. Brough dressed the part of the
Spider in ordinary man’s clothes—dress suit, light over-
coat, tall hat, &c. So there now !
“Scribe”: Nance O’Neil is poorly supported in
“ Tess ” at Her Majesty’s, Melb. and it would have been
wiser had Williamson kept this melancholic show for the
Crane combination. The American girl is rather too
lumpy for Hardy’s much-pursued heroine too, but she is
superb in the one fine dramatic scene of the drama, after
the knifing of Tess’s protector. Here she strikes the actual
top-note of tragedy. It is only one note, but it still
haunts my nights. Angel has entered and discovered
Tess standing, white, dumb, stunned, mechanically
brushing at her hair like a woman in a dream. He look
in at the body and throws a coakaboutthe gn-l
whisDerine her to come away. What t she cries
sharply loudly, exactly like the cry you hear when you
in a silent house and someone suddenly utters
a terrified call in his sleep. That momentary effect is
compensation for some solid wads of dullness.
London There's no arguing with the illuminated
Ibsenites. They explain their faith in the unanswerable
way of an Adelaide Critic believer, who says - Ibsen
does not write for the multitude ;he f ° r ] t |'®
chosen few, the brightest minds, and makes absolutely no
concession to intellects less alert. Not many men under
stand him, and ladies are hopelessly befogged in the
strange atmosphere of his philosophy. Ibsen s voice is
that of one crying in the wilderness foretelling a new era
—an era that may never be (“ foretelling what may never
be ”is good)—and to appreciate him you must take to the
study of his work an active mind void of prejudices, and
a keen sympathy based on a conviction of the utter lolly
of most of our accepted ideals.” A man who gets on a
pedestal and assumes himself to be the only fellow in the
crowd capable of understanding the foolishness of things
is evidently a first cousin to the simpering cntic in one of
Du Maurier’s old Punch sketches. Du Mauner s johnnie,
striking an attitude of admiration before an alleged vvork
of art, said : “ It’s a beautiful pictchaw ! His friend
protested—“ The figure-drawing is vile, the coloring
crude, and the perspective all wrong.” “No mat tab, it s
a beautiful pictchaw,” droned the johnnie. Heavens,
man, where is the beauty?” roared the other fellow.
“In the pictchaw,” droned johnnie. He belonged to the
chosen few —the fewah the bettah.
The first Australian impersonator of Camille was Mary
Provost, who appeared in the 50’s. She was far gone in
consumption, and made the death-scene sufficiently grue-
some. Her chief supporter was Neil Warner as Armand
—an actor of talent who somehow never contrived to do
himself justice.
Sydney is much amused ab the manner in
which Hordern and Lassetter are “ poking it ” at
each other in their morning-paper advts. The
lawyers begin to look upon the duel with in-
terested and expectant eyes.
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co. reports
that for the past week wheat transactions have been
limited. London cables indicate an easier feeling, and
local values have still further receded. Northern District
promises a splendid harvest. Quotations : Prime Milling,
2s. 9£d. ; Good, to 2s. Sjd. ; Pinched, 2s. sd. to 2s. 7d.,
according to condition ; Chickwheat, 2s. 3d. to 2s. 4d.
per bushel.*
A record sea-speed of 37 113 knots (43
statute miles good) was recently put up by the
British turbine-engined “destroyer” Viper.
This would be smart railway-travelling, but the
turbine is expected yet to give considerably higher
figures, if a still greater steam-force can be pro-
vided. The talk is now of 40 knots, or a shade
under 46 miles.
In Maoriland, out of 117 prisoners released
last year under the First Offenders Act, 38 carried
out the conditions of license, 4 were re-arrested,
and 75 still rema in under supervision. Out of
£461 costs ordered to be paid, half has been re-
ceived and the rest is to be paid by instalments.
The approximate cost of keeping this crowd in
prison would have been £3715. Of the 1120
persons placed on probation since 1886, 926 have
been discharged after carrying out the imposed
conditions; 67 were re-arrested, 1 suicided, 2 died,
1 was asylumed, 27 absconded, and 96 still remain
fulfilling the terms of license.
Etiquette has hitherto demanded that
M’s.L.A. should confine their questions strictly to
their own electorates, but with a Federal seat as a
possibility members have now to make themselves
solid over a wider area. John Norton waxed in-
dignant t’other day because a member of a constitu-
ency adjoining his own (Northumberland) had
been trying to attract the free and independent of
latter electorate by plying Ministers with ques-
tions re what will be the combined Federal Con-
stituency. On appeal Premier Lyne joined in
condemning the practice, and promised that he
would not answer such questions in future.

Vosper of the Mane will presumably look
for a fresh seat since the W.A. goldfields gave
that solid vote for Federation. The Long-Haired
One was a strong Federalist when the Federal
cause seemed doubtful, and ratted just when vic-
tory was in sight. He evidently miscalculated
the chances. The public for a little while almost
looked forward to the time when Vosper, with his
haircut, would hold a portfolio in Forrest’s anti-
Federal Ministry. But just after Vosper came
over to join Forrest on the anti-Federal side, For-
rest went over to Federalism. It is a sad, sad,
thing to leave your principles to join a
man who has billets to give away, and then find
that that man isn’t there when you arrive.
At a Sydney missionary meeting,
t’other night, a speaker graphically described
the progress in civilisation of the Japs,
attributing same to > the influence of Christian
missions. What about the massacre of 50,000
converts of Xavier in 1603, followed by the post-
ing of public notices throughout the land to the
effect that if the Christian’s God was found in
Japan he would be beheaded? Also the continuous
rigid exclusion of foreigners till 1853? The
missions were responsible for that. The present
progress in civilisation is attributable to the forced
opening of the treaty-ports in 1854, and the con-
sequent discovery by the utterly materialistic Jap
that the new intruders were not proselytisers but
men of trade by whom they could profit. Marquis
Ito, the maker of New Japan, said : “ I never ex-
pected Japan would become a Christian nation,
for Christianity is a religion which no thinking
man could accept. Its only use here would be as
an agent to bring us into line with Western civili-
sation.” Again: “Japan should adopt Chris-
tianity as a convention along with the dress-coat.”
And the missionaries flatter themselves that Japan
takes them seriously !
Hobart Mercury still bitterly reproaches
the British Government at intervals for not badly
mutilating the Commonwealth Bill. Its theory
is that the most loyal intellects of Australia
trusted to the great mind of Chamberlain and Co.
to put into shape the crude work of the deficient
Australian intelligence, and that Chamberlain and
Co. failed in their duty. The Mercury calls this
“ betraying those who trusted them ” and other
hard names. Boiled down the Mercury’s views
are these:
(1) We (Mercury) held that it must be a crude, imper-
fect. Bill because it was drawn up by our own people who,
by reason of the deficiencies of the Australian mind,
are not equal to that sort of thing. We trusted to
Chamberlain’s great intellect to put things right.
(2) Chamberlain said it was a very good Bill and didn’t
need putting right. As a man of great intellect he ought
to know.
(3) Now we (Mercury) with our poor deficient Austra-
lian intellect, lecture this great man for being such a fool
as not to know a bad Bill when we tell him it is bad.
(4) Our prayer and attitude is : “ O, great man, we are
but an intellectual worm, and trust entirely to your
vast intelligence to see the deficiencies in our work and
put them right. And if you don’t see the deficiencies
you are a mere idiot, for we tell you there are deficiencies
and we know a great deal better than you do. There-
fore we humbly submit ourselves to your guidance and
better knowledge, and if you say, in your better know-
ledge, that our work isn’t nearly so bad as we think, you
are a liar and a mule, for our knowledge is much better
than yours. We are ah o by comparison with you, and
if you don’t agree with us about our being a worse fool
than you then you are a worse fool than we.
It is a curious position, when you come to think
it over.
An Australian, lately from S’Africa, states
that the newly-arrived “Tommies” had the
haziest ideas about Boers and their history. One
Tommy asked him if he had ever seen a real Boer,
and when he explained that he was 12 years out
in the country, the interested warrior said, “ Then
you know what they’re like. What are they like 1 '
Old, grey-bearded blankers, ain’t they?” On
hearing that Boers varied in their ages and
weights like other people, and were often fine-
looking fellows, the Tommy remarked, “ Oh,
well, they’re goin’ to git done bad, any’ow.”
Writing to a Bulletin correspondent, a
nurse who was on board gives an interesting
picture of the voyage of the American hospital
ship Maine to aid S’African wounded :
The Maine left London Dec. 24th. Lady Randolph
Churchill had a large reception-room, a bath-room, and
a bed-room on the promenade deck and played the great
lady philanthropist with much fuss and feather. The
skilled nurses and other folk were very small potatoes.
Some of them were put to such work as scrubbing decks,
and various other duties of seamen. On January 4th,
Lady Churchill had every scrap of religious litera-
ture —tracts, Bibles, periodicals, leaflets, &c., brought
up on deck and the whole pitched overboard for
the moral instruction of the fishes. But, to compen-
sate, she had the ship ransacked for caramels which “ she
knew were somewhere.” Finally thev were found and she
distributed 50 boxes among the orderlies and nurses.
Jan. 10th was Lady O.’s birthday, and the Maine crossed
the line, dressed in all her bravery of flags. The custom-
ary initiation of men who had never crossed the line before
was carried out while Lady C. and her companion, Miss
Warrender, stationed themselves on the bridge with
kodaks to get snap-shots of the different victims as they
were being lathered and shaved by Father Neptune’s
barber. The head of the non-commissioned staff of the
Medical Corps—an American —was badly handled and
dragged out of the rigging by the crew. They got
him up to the tub of tar and slush when
all the Americans rushed to his aid. The English-
men helped their side and there was a
free fight. Finally the Americans prevailed and
their man was not shaved. But Lady Churchill
came down from the bridge and lectured him for damag-
ing her fun, told him he was poor-spirited, had no sense
of humor, and had spoilt everything by his stubbornness.
The Maine reached Cape Town Jan. 21st. A round of
wild gaiety began. Sir Alfred Milner and-other poten-
tates came on board, and there was a big English recep-
tion on shore with Americans absent ! Durban was
reached Jan. 29—brilliant breakfasts, receptions, lun-
cheons, dinners, and teas were the order of every day.
When the first trainful of wounded soldiers came iii from
Pietermaritzburg Lady C. and Miss W. put red crosses on
their arms and went forth with a kodak to meet the
trains. The ship looked very much like Lady C.’s
pleasure-yacht. There were no lectures, no instructions
given ; the time was passed in lounging, drinking,
cigarette-smoking—by ladies as well as men. The badly-
wounded soldiers got everything they wanted on the re-
turn journey, but the convalescents were not allowed
the beer and stout which had been put on board for them.
At Durban 1000 cases of crackers, each containing 36
packets, were converted into cash, Lady C. deciding that
as money was running short thev could be sold. The
Maine was back at Cape Town March 21st, and there was
thore gaiety. Col. Rhodes was a guest on board and was
entertained largely on shore. More stores were sold, and
on 23rd the Maine sailed. At St. Helena there was more
fun, and more of the supplies were converted into cash.
On April 15th the Maine reached Madeira. The wounded
were most anxious to reach England as soon as possible,
especially Sergt. Grantham, of the Army Hospital Corps,
who was very ill. The ship was to have sailed at 4 p.m.,
but Lady C. dined ashore and did not return till very
late. Sergt. Grantham died at midnight. Lady C. begged
for another day ; the captain granted it, but when yet
another was asked for, she was informed that if not back
at the ship by a certain time the ship would sail without
her. After leaving Madeira Lady C. was very charming
to everybody and the nurses all had their salaries raised.
We reached London on March 23rd, and Heaven preserve
me from such another trip !

Several hundred people in New South Wales are to-day considering whether they shall or shall
not buy the London “ Times ” Reprint of the Ninth Edition of the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica,” and
it may be assumed that in almost every instance their hesitation is due to comparisons which they are
makmgbe tween the Encyclopaedia” and some other object which can be procured at about the same
cost. Hardly anyone who is likely to read this advertisement is so circumstanced that the immediate
°* twenty-one shillings, and the prospects of making certain further payments of twenty-one
s mlings each, need be very alarming. But even among the more fortunately-situated classes there is
a limit to the number of available guineas, to the number of procurable pleasures. Opportunities to
spend money increase at a rate of compound interest which surpasses even the power of money to
beget money. I rom the child with the halfpenny in its palm, making its choice among the sweets in
the shop-window to the land-owner who doubts which one of two estates he shall add to his posses-
sions, we are all forced to undergo one acquisition for the sake of another. Is the acquisition of the
Encyclopaedia Bntanmca that which should be foregone ?
I**? the purpose of this advertisement to settle some of the doubts which delay the purchase of
the Encyclopaedia, and this can only be done by endeavoring to give these doubts, which are prone
to be themselves somewhat vague and formless, a definite and assailable form. At best this is but a
conjectural task. The wisest of the 1100 wise men who made the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica ” never
knew, and never will know, why certain people do not buy the book. He helped to make it; he
knows that it is a book worth buying; he is proud of it, and can defend it against any criticism. If
a man said to him : Sir, I desire, since you are one of the writers who wrote this book, to explain
to you upon what grounds I refrain from purchasing it,” he would be interested, and if he
were in the humour for chopping logic, he would no doubt find it easy to convince his
adversary. But all written descriptions of a book go out into silent space like pieces of paper
tossed into the air from the car of a balloon. One reader recognises the fact that a
great opportunity is presented to him, and he buys the book. The next man who reads the advertise-
ment is unconvinced—perhaps because he thinks the book costs too much money, perhaps because he
thinks it takes too much room, perhaps because he thinks he is too old to read' it, perhaps because he
thinks his children are too young to read it, perhaps for no other reason than that he has glanced at the
otter and thought no more about it. If he has given any consideration to the advisability of buying
or not buying the Encyclopedia Britannica,” he has perhaps thought of it as a book, and thought of
its price as the price of a book. Herein lies an error. One must for convenience speak of it as a book
but ail sorts of confusion will be avoided if one does not think of it as a book.
It is in truth a library of books, a collection intended for reading, study, and reference.
If its five-and-twenty quarto volumes were reduced to small octavos, the “ Encyclopedia ” would
occupy half-a-dozen shelves of an ordinary bookcase. If 500 of the more copious among its 16 000
articles were printed as distinct volumes they would make a library of 500 of the most valuable books
one could have. But all the matter has been run together for convenience. It was not necessary to
tr j a sl;ior t story into a long story in order that a brief discussion of some one subject might be
made to fill a volume, nor was it necessary to cramp the writer’s thoughts and distress the reader’s eve
m order that a spacious treatise should be forced between the two covers of an arbitrary size A fur-
ther advantage of this plan of arrangement is that one copious index can definitely point the wav to
ail that is m the various books. J
In the ordinary way, when one takes a book from a bookshelf one has to turn page after page and
even search through chapter after chapter before one finds the isolated statement, the solitary phrase
the one thing m all the book that is of any service in that moment of immediate need. In the “ . Ency-
clopaedia Bntanmca the reader does not even have to read the whole of an article At the cost of
enormous labor a running digest is printed on the margins of each of the long articles, so that a page
is in many cases broken up into four or five perceptible divisions. All these elaborate methods of sub-
division increase the value of the “Encyclopaedia Britannica,” and serve to mark it as a library a
collection of books, as contra distinguished from a single book. J ’
This view of the book is the only point of departure for an examination of its price.
«<rru Th m- twe^,t^ five . vol l uraes in th , e binding were originally catalogued by the publishers at £37
• i, 16 iime ® . Reprint has recently been sold, and will be sold until the 15th of September for £l7
m the same binding. Under the novel system of monthly payments which has been adopted, the
purchaser, instead of sending a cheque for £l7 to the Manager of “The Times” (New South Wale'
Office, Sydney), need only pay one guinea before the 25 volumes have been delivered to him, and then
complete the purchase by 16 further monthly payments of one guinea each.
What other Library can be bought for such a sum ?
One may buy an edition of the standard poets, but the standard poets do not answer questions.
One may buy a collection of engravings for the same money, but there is no such collection in existence
which, like the nine thousand illustrations of the “Encyclopaedia Britannica,” may be called a
systematised pictorial library of art and science. No matter how many other books one may have
bought before one buys the “Encyclopaedia Britannica,” and no matter how many other books one
may hope to buy after one has bought the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica, ” the “Encyclopaedia” is the
book to buy next. It is not, however, possible to arrive at this conclusion and to go no further. One
c < ini ) o f say, “ Yes, I will buy the ‘ Encyclopaedia Britannica ’ before I buy any other book ; I will buy
the ‘ Encyclopaedia Britannica ’ after Quarter Day,” for when quarter-day comes it will be too late to
buy the Encyclopaedia Britannica ”at the present prices and upon the existing terms. “ The Times ”
Reprint was published in accordance with certain arrangements made by The Times, and by Messrs.
.• ai B. Black, the publishers of the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica.” These arrangements made it pos-
sible for the moment to procure the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica ” at a reduction of more than 55 per
Ce i n - t 'i f m m the ori S inal publisher’s price, but on the 15th of September, eight weeks from the day on
which The Times Reprint of the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica ” was first offered to the New South
Wales public, these arrangements cease to be operative, and those who fail to buy the work within the
limited time will, if they desire to procure it on the 16th day of September, be obliged either to do
witliout it or send to England, wait three months for the books, and pay carriage and other costs from
All of the other arguments in favor of the purchase of the work could have been equally well
advanced ten years ago, when the Ninth Edition was completed, and will be equally valid ten years
hence. But this is the argument of the hour, this is the consideration which appeals to the purchaser
who wants to get the best value for his money.
.To buy The Times Reprint of the “ Encyclopiedia Britannica” is not a formidable under-
taking. It is not like buying a house that one must compare with other desirable houses, that one
must visit and revisit, of which the drains and the walls and the sub-cellars must be examined and
tested, the title-deeds to which must be scrutinised by solicitors.
It is only necessary to send a guinea to the New South Wales office of “ The Times,” Svdnev
accompanied by an order form, m order to secure the immediate delivery of the 25 volumes. It is’
indeed, one of those operations which are so simple that one unconsciously classes them among the
things which one can do at any time, from which one turns away to do more complicated things. But
untll m °l row p™. Will be very likely to postpone it for three weeks, until he
loses altogether the greatest bargain which is to be found in the whole world of books.

got to get back !—and American General Chaffee
guesses that’s going to be a mighty tall contract.
A London thief-paper, Photo Bits, cribs
from The Bulletin many drawings by Souter.
The signature is always carefully cut out, unless

Byron Moore’s fairy-tale book published
for the _ benefit of Melb. Children’s Hospital is
interesting in more than one respect. The simple

Fourteen members of Victoria’s Leg.
Council come to the end of their term this month,
and by all appearances every one of them will go
back unopposed. Men with the required property
qualification, with a taste for politics, able to
spend a great part of their time in Melbourne,
and willing to work without salary in a House
where there is hardly any prospect of rising to
political greatness, are very few, and the few are
mostly Tories, so it isn’t worth while rousing up
Hie Tory who is out to oppose the Tory who Is in.
The Victorian House of 48 Rich Landlords prac-
tically dropped the hollow pretence of being an
elective body even so many years ago.
S.A. Leg. Council declined, by 13 votes to
10—which, with the President, accounts for the
whole Chamber—to enfranchise all householders
and their wives ; but it has agreed to give the suf-
frage to the wives of all present electors (the rental
qualification being £25 yearly and the freehold
qualification £5O land value), and to enroll all
Crown lessees who own improvements worth £2OO.
The Govt, shows an inclination to accept this small
concession, in the hope that it will lead to better
things hereafter. The rich man, they argue, has
got his wife on the rolls already, and so it will be
the middle-classes and the working-man who will
gain by the amendment.
The final results of the Federal Referen-
dum in Westralia show: —Federal votes, 44,700;
anti-Federal, 19,691; Federal majority, 25,009.
The goldfields supplied 24,394 of the majority, so
even without them the Federal party would have
pulled through with 615 to the good. The
big Federal majority in the eight Perthmantle
electorates was a revelation. Curiously enough,
however, the boss Federal vote was at lone, remote
bullock-raising Kimberley, which went Federal by
57 to 1. The one solitary Parochial vote was cast
at Turkey Creek. Wyndham and Hall’s Creek
and Wild Dog were Federal to a man.
Be the cabled announcement that two Aus-
tralasians have been selected by their comrades to
receive scarves made by the Queen herself as re-
wards for bravery in S’Africa. Gen. Evelyn
Wood relates how the first man elected by his
comrades in the Crimea to receive the Victoria
Cross was the man who used to carry the grog-can
daily to the trenches, and who, never having been
under fire for more than half-an-hour a day, had
never been in any particular danger. He was
simply elected because he was an extremely useful
and comforting person. The soldier who has
really seen war is rather scornful of deeds
of mere ecldt, and prefers the useful person who
simply does his duty. Apropos, the fact that no
Victoria-cross has yet come Australia-wards is
rather rough on the two young fellows who, it
was announced, had been recommended for the
distinction, and who bore themselves so modestly
that it was plain they had taken no hand in
advertising themselves over the matter.
Be the recent acquittal, in Tasmania, of a
half-caste Chinese girl who had shot two men (one
fatally) for, as alleged, trying to force their way
into the house in which she was alone — The Bul-
letin is informed that there is a somewhat different
side to the story. The house from which the
shooting took place is in any case, it is alleged,
“ within call of the Chinese camp, and situated on
clear ground, so that the girl could have got away
by the other door and reached the camp aforesaid
before the men could have known she had
escaped.” The people in the neighborhood, who
know a lot more of the matter than has got into
print, consider that there has been a gross mis-
carriage of justice. The fact that the men were
ten yards away from the house when they were
shot looks awkward, in view of the assertion that
they were shot for trying to force their way into
the house.
“ A Resident ” to The Bulletin :
Although the Chief Judge in Equity on Monday last
granted an injunction restraining N.S.W. Govt, from
allowing a continuance of the rifle-shooting on Randwick
Range, said shooting has since gone on merrily as ever.
It was stated on behalf of the Govt, that its military
authorities had advised it that it was very improbable
that bullets would, as stated, pass over the hill at the
rear. But it is an absolute fact that we residents in the
Coogee neighbourhood have for some time had bullets
flying around, and, in some cases, into our houses. I
saw a boy actually wounded some time ago by a bullet
which gianced from the iron roof of a shed in my yard,
and only the other day one of my sons barely escaped
being shot through the head. Numerous other residents
can testify to the arrival of bullets upon their premises;
and, in view of such unpleasantly hard facts, it’s a bit
ridiculous to maunder about theoretical “ improba-
bility.”

More Queensland testimony as to Queens-
land defections. Letter to Cookieb (August 24)
says: —“We are having plenty of workout here
with patrols by day and Cossack posts by night,
as there are less than 50 men to draw ivom-rfifty
out of 4001 Where all the rest get to I don’t
know. There were only 37 of the Second Contin-
gent in the ranks as we marched into Pretoria,
and I think 22 out at Bronker’s Spruit, the
battle 14 miles north of Pretoria.” Same corre-
spondent declares how 40 Queenslanders chased
100 Boers into some hilly country, when a rifle-
duel opened. Both parties fired until all the
ammunition was exhausted. Result —“ One
fellow was shot through the coat.” Evidently,
there’s a terrible waste of lead in S’Africa just
now.

The Sydney Wharves Resumption, which
might have been the beginning of a new era of
honesty and common-sense, is apparently going to
be the same old swindle as all the other resump-
tions. There is no provision for basing the pay-
ments on municipal valuations, land-tax valua-
tions, rental returns, or any other fixed basis
whatever. Values are to be fixed by the law-
courts —the same process which has always led to
the State paying about twice the fair and honest
price for the property. That hired humorist, the
“expert,” who swore a few months ago that a
resumed property was worth, say, only £50,000 for
probate purposes, will doubtless now turn up
as usual and swear that it is worth, say, £150,000.
The owner who gets £lOO,OOO for his pro-
perty when he has for years paid tax on a
■municipal valuation of £30,000, isn’t made liable
for his back rates or for the land-tax he has
diddled the Department out of. The Lyne Govt,
follows woodenly in the steps of its predecessors,
and there are no new ideas in it —except when it
sends two “assistant paymasters” with the
Chinese contingent.

The Tasmanian Government, after a
momentary pretence of firmness, has again sagged
at the knees. The Great Western land-grant
railway syndicate has promised to really start
work at last, and' in view of this 55th promise or
thereabouts —the fragments of the other 54 pro-
mises can be found all over the island —the Lewis
Govt, promises to try and persuade Parliament to
give the dreadful “cat’’another year’s extension
of time. It shouldn’t get another second’s exten-
sion,. and its £lO,OOO deposit and its line should be
remorselessly forfeited if the railway isn’t com-
pleted to the tick of the clock. The State has
as much reason to demand that other people shall
abide by their contracts with it, as it has to abide
by its own contracts. It is just because it is a
weak-kneed concern that never enforces its agree-
ments that the syndicates with which it makes
bargains never think it worth while to keep faith
with it. The chances are that inside the next two
years the Federal Parliament will have taken over
the railways, and be prepared to build the Tas.
West Coast lines quicker and on better terms
than any syndicate, and to work them at half the
fares, and for that reason alone the Great Western
“cat” should not receive another penn’orth of
encouragement.

A PRAHRAN PROOF,
Drugs, Doctors, and Hospitals prove quite
impotent,
Clements Tonic Brought in and all Diseases
Melt Away.
The Case of
MRS. ELIZABETH HILL.
Another Proof for Sceptics,
Of the many populous suburbs surround-
ing Melbourne not one is to be compared to
Prahran j its main street of thriving stores,
roads bowered in trees, and the beauty and
animation of the whole place, make a most
attractive locality. This was the feeling of
our reporter as he trod tne busy icotpath of
Chapel-street seeking for No. 24 Chatham-
street, the home of Mis. Elisabeth Hill.
This lady’s residence was easily found, for
it is close to the Town Hall and the main
artery of traffic. The lady herself chanced
to be at home, and in answer to our ques-
tions graciously consented to relate her
remarkable experience:—
“Some time ago,” said Mrs. Hill, “I
suffered from one of the accidents peculiar
to married women. X was properly attended
and cared for, yet the shock to my system
was such that no medicine or food would
give me back my past health and vigor.
Something was tadically wrong with me, I
had no recuperative power, and time,
instead of making me stronger, Weakened
me.” _
“Did you go to a doctofr, Mrs. Hill?”
inquired the lepotter.
“ I went to three medical men altogether.
.3Tirst to our regular doctor, who said I was
r>i’flWing from neivous debility ; but his
U/i'iuiment doing me no good, I tried another
f doctor, and later, when my husband went
f to West Australia, I became an out-patient
at the hospital. But the result was always
the same; no matter where I went, the
• drugs prescribed were quite impotent to do
me good.”
“ What were the symptoms, Mrs. Hill, of
this complaint that so set medical skill at
defiance? ’ inquired the pressman.
“1 had a dieadful pain in the back part
of the top of my head, a fearful, horrible,
agonising pain that made me feel I was
going mad. Look ! it turned my hair quite
grey in that spot.” [The reporter here
notices that in the place indicated by Mrs.
Hill her naturally dark hair was streaked
with white.] “I could not sleep at night, nor
get proper rest, horrible dreams woke me
up in a cold sweat if 1 got a shoit doze, and
the hallucination would lemain with me
after 1 was faiily awake, for 1 would jump
out of bed with terroi and huiry from the
room. Only when the daylight was just
shining in the morning was 1 able to close
*r?.v eyes for a shoit.time. As I lay restless
in bed, turning uneasily fiom side to side, I
tried all the ways I could think of to induce
Bleep. I woula count figures over and over
again, or repeat lines of poetry in endless
repetition ; but it was no use, for the ■ nd of
every effort was that my mind switched
back to the constantly melancholy thoughts
that possessed me. And the least thing
would excite this morbid brooding; if I read
something horrible in the newspaper, some
murder or the like, I had a sort of irresist-
ible feeling that I was about to be involved
in a like catastrophe. This dread would so
master me that I dared not stay in the
house, but had to go to my mother’s for her
company and assurance. The least thing
startled me, I would leap right out of my
chair at a sudden noise, even though I
expected it. My husband, knowing this,
would walk very quietly into the room and
touch me with his hand gently; yet this
would make me tremble all over with a kind
)f shiver. After these nervous shocks my
leart would flutter and vibrate, and if I
were in bed I could hear it go thump
thump as it seemed to move the covering.
1 remember that once the doctor gave me a
deeping draught; he said it was of vital
importance that I should have rest, and I
was so delighted thinking of the oblivion
that was going to come for a short time to
my misery, that I got excited with expecta-
tion, and the medicine had no effect. It
was quite a common thing for me to cry and
sob all night long. I simply could not eat
at, all, X had no appetite, I became very
thin, losing my natural plumpness of body,
my skin got very yellow in color and was
blotched with brown patches. In the
morning the nasty taste in my mouth made
1 sick, and there was white fur over
in' io .gu •. There was a sharp paiu in the
back of my neck and a singing in my ears,
a sorb of buzzing that hardly ever left me.
Wind on the stomach was another source of
annoyance. There were muscular pains in
my arms, and at times in the palms of my
hands and soles of my feet a tingling,
pricking sensation, as though they were
going to sleep. My eyes were I
am sure they sank back in my head quite
an inch, and the sight became blurred and
misty with little stars dancing before me.
Sometimes a feeling like a veil falling would
come down over my head and face and creep
to my shoulders, a kind of dead ache, as
though all that part of my body was losing
life—an indescribably horrible feeling, I
am naturally of a lively disposition and
cheerful in thought } but now I was utterly
tired of my life. If it had not been for
thoughts of my family 1 would have
welcomed death. And my imagination was
so worked up that I had only to hear
accounts of other people’s maladies to fancy
I was suffering from like complaints in
addition to my own special ailment. My
temper became very irritable ; sometimes I
fancy I hardly knew what I was saying ; it
is certaih I did ttot hrean all I said. And I
was as easily upset abd turned to tears as a
little child, my herves and impulses being
quite beyond my own control. But while I
tell you of all these smaller things, do not
forget that my great and master trouble all
the time was the fearful pain in the head I
have told you of, that seemed in some way
to be the cause of all my other sorrows.
My last doctor did nob speak very hopefully
of my case, he said it would take twelve
months’ treatment to cure me. And after
my long experience of doctors’ treatment
you may be sure this prospect did not
encourage me. I tried the patent medicines
that are much advertised, but in my case
they failed to do as promised. Then I
chanced to read in the newspaper the re-
port of a cure by Clements Tonic, and I
fancied from what I read that this remedy
might suit my case. I purchased a bottle
of Clements Tonic, and it did me so much
good I bought another; and it was after
this second bottle that the dreadful pain in
my head went right away, and that I began
to care for my meals and get sleep at night.
Then all the troubles I have told you of
melted away one after the other, like ice
melts under the sun on a warm day, so did
all my distressing symptoms dissolve in the
healing effects of Clements Tonic. I can
not over praise Clements Tonic for what it
has done for me ; there is no doubt it was
the one thing that had been wanted during
all my sickness, and the finding of it at last
saved my reason if not my life. I know
another case in my own family where
Clements Tonic wrought another cure as
surprising as my own, so that I have double
proof that my faith in Clements Tonic and
its wonderful powers is well founded.”
“ I should like to be allowed to report
your case, Mrs. Hill,” said the reporter
interrogatively, for it will make yet another,
and not the least important, of the mighty
volume of testimonials gathered by Clements
Tonic.”
“ I am sure you are quite welcome. * Out
of a full heart the tongue speaketh.’ If I
have said much it is because I feel much,
and I shall only be too glad if what I have
said reaches and convinces some person who
suffered as I suffered. So publish it as much
as you think advisable.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Elizabeth Hill, of 24 Chatham-st., Prahran;
Melbourne, in the Colony of Victoria, do solemnly
and sincerely declare thatT have carefully read the
annexed document, consistii\ of six folios, and con»
secutively numbered from one to six, and that it
contains and is a true and faithful account of my
illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains
my full permission to publish in any manner my
statements—which I give voluntarily, and without
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of an Act of the
Parliament of Victoria rendering persons making a
False Declaration punishable for wilful and corrupt
perjury.

forting in allaying irritation in the throat and giving strength to the voice, and it neither allows a Cough or Asthma to become chronic, nor Consumption to develop. Consumption has

CONSUMPTION.
TOO ILL TO LEAVE HIS BED.
A COMPLETE CURE.
Mr. W. G. Hearne.—Dear Sir, —I am writing to tell you
about, the wonderful cure your medicine has effected in
my case. About ttiree years ago I began to cough. At
first the cough was not, severe, but it gradually got worse,
and I became very weak and troubled with night sweats,
pain in my chest, and great quantities of phlegm. On
several occasions there was blood in the expectorated
matter. I had been treated by a doctor, who pronounced
my case to be Consumption, and various other treatments
had been tried, but without benefit. It was at this stage
that I heard of your Bronchitis Cure, and sent to you for
a course of the medicine. When it arrived I was too ill to
leave my bed, but I commenced taking it at, once, and
gradually improved. lam glad to say that the two lots
of medicine you sent have effected a complete cure, for
which accept my very best thanks.—Yours gratefully,
J. BLAIR.
Westminster Bridge-road, S.E., London.

HER DAUGHTER HAD BEEN VERY ILL.
SPITTING UP BLOOD.
THE DOCTOR SAID NOTHING MORE COULD BE
