Several times this season a problem has attacked
me. Is dancing going to last ? Various things
make me think that the beginning of the end is
coming, that the queer custom of twirling round
and round eighteen times in succession with a
man’s arm about your waist, eating a lot of cream
for supper, and going home with sore ankles, a
head-ache, and some ragged frills, and wondering
why you did it, is going to wane away into dis-
use. Yes, I have actually sighted the day when a
dance will be as a croquet-party a something
they used to do, the poor, dear, dead people who
didn’t know anything better.
To support my theory : There was a dance lately *
heaps of pretty girls, a fizzy supper, the best of
music, the best of floors. Yet lam told that
every second man said, as he went to the cloak-
room for his crush-hat afterwards, Tins is the
last dance I shall ever go to.” There was nothing
wanting to make that dance what a dance should
be, and yet there they were, grumbling and
groanins".
Here is a dance story. It was at a private hop.
The host, a genial legal gentleman, saw a forlorn
young man standing alone. He was just like
every other host, not knowing half his guests, but
thought he ought to help things on. “If you
come with me,” said he, “ I’ll find you a partner.
Awfully pretty girl. Awfully nice. Heaps of
money. Got an awful brother,” he added, chat-
tily. They stopped in front of a pretty girl.
“ Allow me to introduce” “ Why, she s my
sister ! ” That host is going to be like other hosts
in future, and not try and make things hum at his
own parties.
Some Sydneyites will be interested in these de-
tails of carriage arrangements for the Queen s
afternoon-party on July 11th : “ Only those hav-
ing the privilege of the entree and holding white
carriage cards can enter by that door. I hose
holding green carriage cards will set down at the
main entrance. . . . Holders of green, blue, and
pink carriage tickets may set down at any gate
except the entree in Buckingham Palace Road,
but in all cases every lady or gentleman must pro-
duce and give up the white ticket marked Per-
sonal Admission.” How delightfully Beau-
champy !
Another story of a certain amorous septua-
genarian, who married a comparative juvenile
some months ago. The enterprising ancient
indulges his passion for letter-writing by sending
to his daughters (in Australia) full accounts of
his honeymoon travels, spiced with rapturous
reference to his darling bride. Every day adds
something to his store of new-found bliss, it
appears; but there was one particular day at
Rome which will ever live in his memory.
Delightful weather, delightful outing, glorious
little luncheon and dinner and such a smile on the
face of the Dear One as filially convinced the old
gentleman of her semi-celestial preciousness.
Some marriages are, indeed, made in Heaven,
and theirs is one of the most beautiful that Provi-
dence has been good enough to ordain. Well,
page after page of letter was devoted to the doings
of this halcyon day at Rome. Words couldnt
express the utter joyfulness of it all,, but he had
tried his best to paint a gem of a word-picture m
his diary under date so-and-so. And the daughters,
who are not too happy to remember important
family events, noted that the day when dear Papa
beat his record in the matter of joy was the first
anniversary of their mother’s death.
Society amuses itself merrily over on dits about

wasn’t polished. By way of a good beginning she
gave the gardener his conge. He demurred.
“What,” said she, “you think because I am
H’ignorant that I don’t know what your work
ought to be ! Now, I’ve had my H’eye on you
this long time ; Fve been watchin’ you neglectin’
my observatories and things.” (There’s such a
difference, too, if she’d thought it over; dll the
difference.)
A neat storyette reaches me from a northern
suburb not six miles from the G.P.O. A resident
remarked to the mounted postman, who was
always burdened with one or more brown-paper
parcels, that the parcels-post delivery seemed to
be very heavy in the neighborhood. “It s nothing
to do with the post,” responded the man ; “its
washing that the different washerwomen kid me
into carrying home. I’m going to strike —that I
am.”
A professional (known here) who had made a
most congenial but not financially brilliant mar-
riage had to undergo a temporary separation from
her husband some little while ago. To her came
one day a rich and influential friend, warm-
hearted and well-meaning, but with a yearning to
poke her finger into the domestic pie. “ Now, do
tell me,” she began confidentially, “ why is your
husband so badly off—what is the cause of it all ?
In short, who is the woman?” The foreigner
drew herself up and smote herself tragically on
the breast. “ Madame,” she exclaimJ, “lam ze
woman ! ”
At a smart wedding near Sydney not very long
ago, the parson, after asking some preliminary
questions, said to the blushing bridegroom: “ Well,
that’s all right; you are sure you answered cor-
rectly ? ” “ Well, all but one thing,” was the
reply ; ‘ ‘ you asked me was I of age, and I said
‘Yes,’ but I’m not—l’m twen‘//-sixJ ” r And then
the organist joyously thumped out “The voice
that breathed o’er Eden.”
T’other day I went with some girls to a
charitable institution in this city. As we could
neither sing nor play to the inmates, we took with
us some cakes and tarts, which were much ap-
preciated and eagerly devoured. Suddenly, one
girl, a harmless imbecile, began to cry piteously,
and hunt about as if in search of something.
“ What is the matter ? ” “I’ve lost the jamout
of my tart,” she sobbed. “ How did you lose it.
inquired one of the visitors. “ I ate it! ” was the
heartbroken reply.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent: —
“When Gov. Hopetoun arrived in Victoria, he
sent home newspaper accounts of his official wel-
come, etc. Lady Dorothea Hope and her sister
apparently winked the other eye while reading
these chronicles, and first time they met anyone
likely to know, they asked, ‘ Do they really make
such a fuss over my brother in Melbourne? Its
just as if he were a king. He sent us papers, but
we thought he was. fooling us D In Melbourne,
under vice-regal wing, these ladies were coerced
into pomp and ceremony, and looked very well in
evening-dress, thanks to smooth skin and shapely
shoulders. On their own, they’re poorly and badly
dressed. Their dressing-gowns, short in front and
long behind, once upon a ti le aroused the pity of
fellow-travellers, who were also surprised to notice
that the wandering aristocrats had apparently
never learnt dancing, though they good-naturedly
did their best at boardship hops and candidly con-
fessed their ignorance to partners. The latter, by
the way, still remember the feel of the Hope
hands, rough as nutmeg-graters, through pottering
about farms. Two more natural, bright, un-
affected women never breathed,
“Returned Major Holmes (N.S.W.) tells a
funny story illustrative of the innocence of con-
vent ladies. Big Capt. MTnerney, now in Pre-
toria, has a sister in a Melb. convent. When he
reached Melbourne the Major, as a friendly thing,
called to tell the lady news of her brother. Many
nuns gathered round to hear the warrior s yarns.
He related that the captain was hardly recognis-
able now, as he had a big beard. ‘ A beard ? said
a nun with surprise, ‘ Why, how did he get it .
Holmes says he was just a bit flabbergasted at
first, and took pains to explain that it grew.
“ A perfect lady went to Melb. Public Library
to look for her ‘family crest.’ The attendant
found it for her in a gorgeous work, worth a small
fortune. Then she took out a nice, dainty pair of
pocket-scissors and said with a sweet smile, 1
suppose there will be no objection to my cutting
out this page, will there ? ’

“A Queensland patriarch bought a little place
in the country when his motherless only
child left school. In this retreat, he boasted,
there was nothing to unsettle a young person s
mind, unless she took to ‘envying the powers
their colors and the birds their plumage. lhe

‘ ‘Affable Earl Hopetoun is not singular in his fancy
for ‘ meeting men at their honest toil,’ as shown
by the admiring crowds who gather at the Central
Fire Station on display-afternoons when the
flame-fighters rehearse their deeds of derring-do.
The knights of hose and hydrant are fine fellows
to look at, and their barracks are so radiantly
clean that the visitor quite tires of her own reflec-
tion in buttons, helmets, engines and the glossy
coats of the horses.
“ This all-over spruceness would be less com-
mendable if those responsible took time to prink
nests and preen plumage. But a fire-alarm by
day means a start for the blaze within 17sec.,
while 25sec. are permissible at night. Unlike
that nursery-rhyme sloven, ‘my son John went to
bed with his breeches on,’ a competent fireman
makes Blanket Bay in seemly night-attire like
other less-hurried Christians. Over-night he sets
boots and clothes, all in one piece, at his bedside,
and if required dives into them feet-first in one
act, so to speak. Married firemen, who live on
the first floor, are provided by the management
with a short cut to duty in the shape of a slippery
pole to slide down. Remembering the feverish
impatience of ordinary, slow-going Man kept
waiting, it must be a brave woman who weds one
of these lightning-change artists.

Not a Lily Langtry.
If there is one thing more than another that
Lily Langtry is noticed for it is her complexion.
Unfortunately we see in the streets every day
young ladies who are not Lily Langtrys, who, in
other words, have pimples on the face, and an
unhealthy, pasty look. The dark look under the
eyes which accompanies this paleness is a sure
sign of an unhealthy body with anaemic conditions
in the system. The healthy girl has a fresh, rosy
complexion which is admired. Let us inquire
into the reason of these very opposite conditions.
Healthy blood is absolutely essential to a good
complexion. Many girls with almost perfect
features are annoyed at finding they are neither
pretty nor attractive. They think their trouble
is their face, when it is really their liver. To have
good blood their liver must be in good working
order, the stomach able to perform its part of the
contract, and the person must be free from all
digestive troubles. Bile Beans are compounded ex-
pressly to bring about these conditions, and girls
with a pasty, white skin cannot do better than try
them. Once the organs of the body are in
good order, and the functions of the body carried
out regularly, the skin will assume a beautiful
color without the aid of cosmetics. Bile Beans
will have made the blood pure and the result will
be shown on the surface. Their action is gentle,
there is no griping, purging, and no evil after-
effects. Their formula is purely vegetable, and
such drastic ingredients as aloes, calomel, and
mercury, found in most purgatives, are emphatic-
ally and positively not employed even in the
smallest degree. Occasion may come when you
wish a medicine to thoroughly search out and get
to the root of a digestive ailment. If it does,
remember Bile Beans are the people’s popular
priced preparation, and they cure when other
remedies fail. Those who are troubled with pim-
ples should try a course of Bile Beans, and the
young lady taking them may be assured that her
complexion will at least be improved, if not as
fascinating as Lily Langtry’s.

The Old Arm-chair.
After the worries of business there is nothing
more refreshing than to come home and be wel-
comed by the embrace of your wife, or if you are
not married by the whole-souled greeting of your
mother or some one near and dear to you. It is
then when the cares of the day sink into oblivion.
You put on your slippers and sit in the old arm-
chair. It is strange what a fascination that old
arm-chair has for you. The older it is the better
your like it. It recalls recollections of the past.
Your father sat in that chair, and perhaps your
father’s father. There is nothing infatuating in
the old arm-chair. Dean Swift was right when he
designated it “ a human piece of furniture.” But
even the old and faithful arm-chair is useless
unless you have health. Rather sit in front of
your fire on a lump of stone being in perfect
health. If you want to be well do as thousands of
others have done, take Bile Beans for Biliousness.
This marvellous medicine is not a cure-all, but in
cases of Biliousness, Indigestion, and all com-
plaints emanating from the liver Bile Beans for
Biliousness are invaluable.
Mr. T. Reece, of 28 Clayton-street, Ballarat,
says For the past 15 years or more I have been
a martyr to headaches, sickness, and biliousness
in all forms. 1 had consulted nearly all the
doctors in Ballarat, but they certainly did not
seem to understand my case, they were baffled
because I never smoke or drink or keep late hours.
In short none of them did me any good at all and
one medical man went so far as to say that I would
have to put up with the trouble and that there
was no cure for me. At last I was recommendedi
to try Bile Beans, and about two months ago I
purchased a box. I have much pleasure to say
that since that time, after taking this medicine, I
have enjoyed perfect health and have no objection
in the interest of humanity for you to publish this.

The Artist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a small old man, slight of build, and
meek of manner. His face was clean-shaven, save
for a tuft of fluffy grey hair on each side just
below the ears ; his mouth retreated deeply, and
his chin shot out to a remarkable point. Over his
whole face was spread a suggestion of appealing
innocence such as sometimes shines in the face of
a wondering child.
His shop was in the crowded Market. He
called it his studio. The sign, badly lettered in
red on a yellow ground, read : “ Edwardo Pink,
Artist, Painter, &c.” Not long ago it read:
“Edward Pink, Fancy Goods.” The change of
Edward to “Edwardo” was a concession to art
conventions, as the old man understood them.
His long, grey hair, carefully parted in the
centre, and falling smoothly to his shoulders, was
another. Edwardo would have preferred to have
worn it ruffled and leonine, but there were things
his wife would not endure, even in an artist, and
untidiness was one of these.
In spite of the new sign Edwardo’s neighbors in
the Market persisted in calling him “Old Ned.”
But the Market was not disrespectful. Really it
admired “Old Ned”; it was proud of him; it
took credit to itself for having produced an artist
who actually made pictures in red and blue and
green, and who was engaged on a mysterious
masterpiece that might yet be a standing adver-
tisement for the Market in the National Gallery.
In the window of Edwardo Pink’s studio many
wonderful water-colors invited the attention of
the passer-by, and a square foot of card, with
amazing purple scroll-work, bore the announce-
ment that “Painting, drawing, and all kinds of
artists’ work ” were done within at cheap rates.
There, too, was a large, musty, confused print of
one of Michael Angelo’s cartoons, labelled boldly :
“By Michael Angelo.” By its side, bravely in-
viting comparison, stood a painting of three
scarlet roses in a blue bottle, with the triumphant
legend: “By Edwardo Pink.” The Market looked
on this picture and on that, and realised in a
moment the enormous progress of Art since the
days of the Italian master.
Edwardo painted portraits, too, at astounding
prices ranging from half-a-crown to seven-and-
sixpence, according to the size of the sitter. His
picture of a market celebrity was on view, and
was recognised as a speaking likeness in spite of
the bluish hair and beard and the eccentric dis-
position of the right eye.
On the day on which I purchased the famous
“Two Cats Drinking,” and the “Bee on a Ger-
anium” —two authentic Pinks now in my posses-
sion—Edwardo was busy on a plaid fox-terrier to
the order of a dog-fancier in the Market. The
artist explained that he much preferred flower-
subjects, but, he said, the artist was not free to
follow closely the bent of his inspiration, being
forced to accept commissions for animal-paintings
and even to draw dog and cat-pictures for stock, as
a concession to popular taste.
The old man worked behind his counter; he
painted on cardboard with water-colors, using the
cheap boxes of small paints usually given to
children as toys; but for all that he found
material very expensive, being prodigal of color.
There were hundreds of pictures of all sizes on the
shelves behind him. These represented his stock.
They were nearly all flower-drawings at 6d. each,
with liberal reductions to anyone taking a
quantity. He admitted that he painted flower-
pictures very quickly and proudly displayed
his list before me, pointing out their qualities with
a trembling hand, and peering eagerly for signs
of appreciation. He showed roses that resembled
bits of underdone beef, and lilies that might have
done well as turnips. He was very prolific of
chrysanthemums. Chrysanthemums, he said, in
his thin, quavering voice, were the easiest kind of
art, because any color would do for a chrysanthe-
mum. No matter what mistakes you made, a
chrysanthemum could be found to match the re-
sult. He was proudest of his own flowers, the
flowers he invented. He paraded them as a
father might parade his beautiful babies, enlarg-
ing on their virtues in short, nervous sentences,
pathetically, childishly famishing for applause.
“ That ain’t no particular kind of flower,” he
said, holding up an astonishing creation, crude of
color, uncouth of form; “ it just came as I painted,
but it’s as pretty as if it grew, maybe.”
“Much more beautiful.”
“Eh? So it is—much more beautiful —much,
much! ” He squared his shoulders, his cheek
burned, and I did not repent the lie. “ I always
thought that,” he said, casting a timid eye
towards the door, “but I didn’t dare to say it.
You see his voice fell to a thin whisper—“ the
people in the market are very good ; but, between
you and me, mister, they don’t know Art.”
I praised his work again, using all my favorite
Art cant, and purchased two “invented” flowers
and a painting of a girl with a wry neck.
The old man grew under it, his head came up,
and his breast expanded.
“I’m glad you’ve come,” he said. “It’s good
to talk with someone who Knows. Now, none of
my friends really Know. Even the missus don’t
know ; though I’d never tell her, mind you—l’d
never tell her”
Great artists of the past, who toiled unknown,
and suffered the contumely of the unseeing and
the unlearned, were instanced, and Edwardo grew
more confidential and quite garrulous.
“ Do you know,” he said, in .a low voice, as if
fearful of making his secret known to the vulgar,
“ twelve months ago I had no idea I was an artist
—no idea in the world. I painted my own tickets
for my goods in the window, and sometimes put
little flowers in the corners, but I had no notion —
no notion, bless you ! It was Madame Vee who
told me. Have you been to madame ? She is the
great palmist two doors down. She read my
hand, and said that I should have been a great
artist. It disturbed me—it disturbed me ! I
couldn’t sleep for thinking of it. I worried
and worried. It made me ill, but in my
heart I knew it was true. I always
tried to draw things. If they put a pencil in my
hand when I was a mere child, I made pictures.
One afternoon I went to Professor Bonn, the
phrenologist, on the other side of the market. He
is very clever. He told me that Nature had meant
me for an artist —God’s intention had miscarried,
his chart said.”
Edward Pink’s tremulous hands knocked on the
counter, his head was thrown back, a great pride
glowed through the natural mildness of his coun-
tenance.
“I’ll be Great one day,” he said. “I’ll be
Famous ! This don’t pay as well as the fancy-
goods—not near so well; but it will—but it will! ”
I was positive it would. It seemed natural and
necessary to lie.
“Fame must come —it’s coming ! People talk
about Edwardo Pink now—ay, that they do ;
and when that is completed the world will talk of
me.” He pointed to an easel in a corner, covered
with a curtain. “ Not now,” he said, as I moved
towards it. “ Come again—come when it is more
advanced, and you shall see. I’ll show it to you
because you know Art and you can feel with the
artist, but I show it to no others.” He lowered
his voice in reverent egotism. “ It is my Master-
piece. ”
He produced more pictures—bundles of them —
all on skimpy bits of card, and spread them on the
counter in lavish array. It was so ludicrous
and yet so sad; saddest of all was the little
gleam of talent in the vastness of his rubbish—the
occasional touch that might show in the work of
a promising boy of nine—the set of a head, the
occasional trace of life, the reality of an expres-
sion, fitful and feeble, but hinting of a wasted
soul.
At this juncture a little, round old woman, with
red cheeks and a bouncing manner, entered. She
had a good-humored eye and mouth, and her face
lit with pride at the sight of Edwardo.
“My wife, mister,” he said.
She seemed to note his agitation, and led him
to his seat. Then she put his brush in his hand,
and, having lovingly smoothed down his hair with
her palm, left him nervously dabbing at his
flowers.
“ Did you want to buy some pictures, sir ? ” she
said.
Assuring herself that the artist was absorbed in
his work, she whispered : “If you ever want any
more, come when I am in the shop. I sell them
much cheaper. I sell all these at 3d. each.”
It was a shocking sacrifice, I assured her, on
my honor as an art-critic.
“ Oh, I don’t let Edwardo know, but sixpence
is too much. People can’t afford it, yet he will
not let them go under sixpence. He says he owes
it to his art. I sell them though, otherwise we
would not live. Sir,” she lowered her voice again ;
“ Art does not pay. We did well with the fancy -
goods, well enough to buy two small cottages, but
when my husband became an artist he gave up
the business, and now it is hard to live—very
hard. This Art is a mistake. I know Edwardo
is very clever, but people do not want art—they
want fancy-goods. Art is dying out.”
When I saw Edwardo Pink again he was look-
ing much older and was much more tremulous,
but still eager. He showed me his Masterpiece.
It looked like a brickfield in a fog, and my critic-
ism was a fine effort in vacillation, for I had not
the remotest idea what.it all might mean and was
fearful of committing myself.
“It —it —it’s beautiful ?” said the artist timor-
ously.
“Very,” I answered with emotion.
Edwardo expanded, laughed like a conqueror,
and his eye flashed. r
“Very beautiful,” he said emphatically. Very
—very beautiful! Edwardo Pink’s Masterpiece,
that, sir.” He passed a trembling;, caressing hand
over the garish smudge. “ There is my h ame, my
"fulfilVP /
“What will you call it?”
“ ‘ Cows Feeding ’ —just that.”
He showed me bundles of recent pictures, all
bad, painfully bad. He wept when I lied over
them, and I lied staunchly. One could not tell
the truth before the weak, twitching lips and
anxious, prying eyes of the artist.
A month later, walking in the main street, my
eye encountered a picture in a tailor’s window. I
drew-up. Surely I knew that touch ; those flaunt-
ing tulips, that tawdry vase afflicted with spinal
curvature. It was a true Pink. Presently 1 was
conscious of someone pressing at my side, and
looking down found Edwardo there, craning to
look into my face, eager to see what impression
his picture had made. At that moment he recog-
nised me, and clutched my arm.
“Ah, it is you ! ” he said, excitedly. “Why
have you not been to see me ? I wanted to tell
you. Come, come ; let me show you.” He dragged
me across the road, stumbling and muttering as
he went. He was now a mere wisp of a man, his
body was bent low, his limbs worked with strange
involuntary movements, and he was weak as a
child.
“At last! At last! ”he said, waving a feeble
hand in the air. “It has come, mister. I will
show you!”
He drew me to an hotel-door, and pointed out a
picture of his pinned on the bar wall.
“You see? You see?”
Further up the street there was one in a hair-
dresser’s window. There were two more in a
furniture shop.
“You see—you see!” he repeated, trium-
phantly. “It has come. I told you it would ! ”
Over the road again. He dragged me to the
stationer’s, where two more Pinks were displayed.
In three other shops were gorgeous flower-paint-
ings signed with his name, and at last he stopped,
exhausted, clinging to my arm for support, cough-
ing painfully and gasping : “ You see ! You see!”
“Is it success ? ” I asked.
“Success ! ” he stammered “It is Fame ! ”
About a month later I wandered to Edwardo
Pink’s studio, and found the shutters up and little
Mrs. Pink inside dressed in deep mourning, tying
the “stock” in various bundles. Edwardo had
been dead a week.
“He thought he was famous when he died,”
said the widow, whose tears began to flow at
the sight of me. “He said that all Aus-
tralia knew his name—but it didn’t, sir.” She
seemed to give me the latter fact in strict
confidence. “ I deceived my poor husband. I
gave people his pictures to put on their walls and
in their windows, because it pleased him so. He
thought that they had bought them, that every-
body was wanting his paintings, but really we
had not sold one for a long time. I deceived
him.” Pier voice broke, and she sobbed outright.
“I deceived my poor Edward. He thought
when he died that the Governor and the Chief
Justice and other great men would be his pall-
bearers. But they weren’t, sir”—this too was
strictly confidential—-“they weren’t. Only the
oyster-man, and the dog-fancier, and two others
from the saveloy-stalls ; but all the Market went
to his funeral. Ah, sir, Art is dying out. I’m
going to start the fancy-goods again.”
EDWARD DYSON.
